23.1  Introduction
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
The purpose of this chapter is to provide details in the form 
of reviews of the latest research into the mechanisms of toxi-
cology with a focus on specific instrumental technologies 
employed to obtain the mechanistic information. Should the 
reader have interest in more in-depth descriptions of the spe-
cific instrumentation itself and its underlying principles, then 
the information appearing in the previous edition of this text 
may be consulted [1]. These reviews will cluster upon the fol-
lowing analytical methodologies, gas chromatography (GC), 
liquid chromatography (LC), GC with mass spectrometry (GC/
MS), LC with mass spectrometry (LC/MS), LC with mass 
spectrometry/mass spectrometry (LC/MS/MS), supercritical 
fluid chromatography (SFC), infrared (IR) and Raman spec-
troscopy, ultraviolet-visible spectroscopy (UV-VIS), nuclear 
magnetic resonance spectrometry (NMR), atomic absorption 
spectroscopy (AAS), inductively coupled plasma (ICP), and 
sample preparation technologies. The reviews will mostly focus 
on how the instrumental advantages are leveraged to obtain 
both quantitative and qualitative information on toxicology 
mechanisms. When deemed appropriate, some of the discus-
sions will be directed toward advances in the technologies even 
though specific applications to the direct study of mechanisms 
in toxicology may not have yet appeared in the literature.
23.2  Gas Chromatography and 
Liquid Chromatography
(Dr. Serban C. Moldoveanu)
23.2.1  General Comments
The term “chromatography” designates several techniques 
that allow the separation of different molecular species from a 
sample. When used for analytical purposes, the separation is 
typically followed by detection/measurement of the molecular 
species of interest. These species are indicated as analytes. 
The rest of components in the sample is indicated as matrix. 
Besides the separation of analytes, in many analyses the matrix 
also requires the separation from the analytes. In toxicologi-
cal studies, it is very common to have mixtures of analytes 
which are associated with a complex matrix (e.g., made of pro-
teins, carbohydrates, and inorganic salts) that can interfere in 
the analysis. For this reason, many toxicological studies that 
require information on the nature and composition of specific 
compounds utilize chromatographic analytical methods.
For a chromatographic separation, the sample is introduced 
(injected) in a flowing mobile phase that passes a stationary 
phase. The stationary phase retains stronger or weaker differ-
ent passing molecular species and releases them back into the 
mobile phase, separated in time. When the mobile phase is a 
gas, the chromatography is indicated as GC, and when it is 
a liquid, is indicated as LC. Other types of chromatography 
include supercritical fluid, countercurrent, and electro-chro-
matography. A schematic diagram of the chromatographic 
separation process is shown in Figure 23.1.
The eluted molecules from the chromatographic column 
differ from the mobile phase components by certain physico-
chemical properties, which makes them detectable. Common 
properties used for detection in GC are thermal conductivity, 
the quantity of ions formed in a flame, the type of ions in a 
mass spectrometer, etc. For LC among the properties used for 
detection are the density, the UV absorption, the fluorescence, 
the type of ions formed in a mass spectrometer, etc. The role 
of the detector in a chromatographic analysis is to produce an 
electrical signal which depends on a specific physico-chemi-
cal property of the material eluting from the chromatographic 
column. This physico-chemical property which is different 
between the mobile phase and the analytes allows the analyte 
detection generating a chromatographic peak. The electrical 
signal caused by the detection of specific molecular species 
along a chromatographic separation (chromatographic run) 
is translated into a graphic output which is known as a chro-
matogram. The separated components of a mixture eluting 
at different times (known as retention times tR) are displayed 
as peaks in the chromatogram. Due to diffusion (and other

23.2.2  Parameters Characterizing the Separation 
on a Chromatographic Column
A considerable number of parameters are utilized for the 
characterization of a chromatographic separation. Some 
parameters are common for both gas and LC, and others are 
characteristic only for one type. Dedicated literature describes 
in detail many of these parameters [1], and only a few of them, 
that are more common, are listed below:
	
1.	Dead time t0, which is the time required by a 
non-retained molecular species to elute from the 
chromatographic column. This dead time can be 
considered as the time required by a molecule of 
solvent to elute and depends on the flow rate of the 
mobile phase. For example, for a column of length L 
and a mobile phase with a linear flow rate u, the dead 
time is t0 = L/u.
	
2.	Retention time tR, and reduced retention time t′R, 
where tR is the time from the injection of the sample 
to the time of compound elution, taken at the maxi-
mum of the peak (apex). The reduced retention time 
is given by the expression t′R = tR ′  t0.
	
3.	Equilibrium constant K which characterizes the equi-
librium of a specific compound X between mobile 
phase and stationary phase, given by the formula:

23.2.3  Qualitative Analysis Using Chromatography
Qualitative analysis is based in chromatographic methods on two 
different types of parameters, one type being related to the sepa-
ration process, and the other related to the detectors utilized. The 
main parameter from the separation process used in qualitative 
analysis is the retention time tR which is rather specific for the 
analytes. The expected tR for an analyte in a given separation can 
be determined using a standard, and the tR can be used to assess 
the presence or absence of the analyte peak in the chromatogram 
of a sample. This procedure, although very useful in many appli-
cations, has several shortcomings including the need to use stan-
dards in order to know at which tR the analyte of interest is eluting 
and as a result the inability to identify an unknown compound. 
Another problem is the possibility that other compound may elute 
at the same tR as the analyte which would lead to interferences 
and incorrect identifications. These make the use of tR a relatively 
weak procedure for qualitative identification. Another parameter 
related to the separation used for the compound identification is 
the Kováts retention index, and tables of retention indices are used 
to identify peaks by comparing measured retention indices with 
the tabulated values. The Kováts retention index has more gener-
ality than retention time for compound identification but also has 
considerable limitations.
When the parameters related to the separation process are 
not sufficient (or fail) to provide qualitative identification of a 
compound, the information from the detection may provide the 
solution. A variety of detectors are used in GC and in LC, some 
being indicated as “universal”, meaning they respond to all ana-
lytes but do not provide any qualitative information. Other detec-
tors, such as the mass spectrometric detectors, provide highly 
accurate qualitative information, and depending on the goal of 
the analysis they should be utilized for this purpose in combina-
tion with the tR for the compound. Various types of detectors and 
their capabilities are further discussed in this material.
For many detectors, it can be shown that the concentration 
(or the amount) of the compound of interest CX can be deter-
mined from the peak areas of the analyte using the relation 
(see, e.g., [4]):

where AX is the peak area and b is a proportionality coeffi-

the injected sample. The values of coefficient b are typically 
obtained from the calibration lines for a set of known concen-

standard) and using the corresponding chromatographic peak 
areas {Aj}. After the areas are known, the calibration lines are 
obtained by plotting the system of points {Cj, Aj} and obtaining 
the equation of the line (trendline) using the least-squares fit-
ting. The slope b of the calibration line for the analyzed com-
pound is sometimes indicated as sensitivity. The value b of 
the slope for the calibration line may be different for different 
compounds. For this reason, the generation of calibration lines 
is usually necessary for each analyte that must be quantitated.
Some linear calibrations do not have a zero intercept, and 
the calculation of the peak area must be done using a relation 
of the form:

Several other quantitation procedures are used in chromatog-
raphy, such as calibration using the normalized peak areas 
of the analyte by the area of an internal standard, the use of 
standard additions, the use of a response factor (see, e.g., [4]), 
or isotope dilution. In isotope dilution, for example, a known 
amount of isotopically enriched analyte is added to the sam-
ple. The mixing of the isotopically enriched analyte with the 
sample dilutes the isotopic enrichment and the level of analyte 
is calculated from the ratio labeled/not-labeled relative con-
centrations. The procedure requires the use of mass spectrom-
etry for detection since isotopically labeled compounds are 
not (or are very little) separated from the non-labeled ones by 
chromatographic procedures.
23.2.4  Sample Preparation for 
Chromatographic Analysis
A large variety of samples can be analyzed using chromato-
graphic methods, including materials with a very complex 
matrix. However, for the introduction in the mobile phase for 
the chromatographic separation and analysis, the sample must 
be in a form amenable for this purpose, being a gas or a liquid 
(usually solution). Even if the sample can be in this form, it is 
common that some chromatographic analyses cannot be per-
formed directly on the sample solution. In some instances, the 
matrix of the sample affects unfavorably the separation, or the 
level of the compounds to be analyzed in a sample is too low, 
even for the most sensitive detectors used in chromatography. 
For this reason, a step indicated as sample preparation is com-
monly part of a chromatographic analysis. Sample preparation 
can be performed for sample dissolution, matrix modification 
and/or simplification, analytes concentration, preliminary 
separation of the analytes from the sample, chemical changes 
of the analytes or of the matrix, etc. These processes utilize 
physical changes of the sample such as filtration, distillation,

1244
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
crystallization, solvent extraction, solid-phase extraction 
(SPE), membrane or electroseparations, and/or chemical 
changes indicated as derivatizations. Some materials that are 
difficult to solubilize requires various type of chemical degra-
dation, and this is commonly applied for the analysis of poly-
meric materials such as proteins and polymeric carbohydrates. 
One additional type of degradation is applying heat and ana-
lytical pyrolysis is used in some applications for transforming 
a sample into gas form to be analyzed by a chromatographic 
procedure. Extensive presentation of the subject is available in 
the literature (see, e.g., [5]).
23.2.5  Overview of Gas Chromatographic 
Instrumentation
GC is useful for the analysis of compounds that have some 
volatility such that can be placed in the form of gas/vapor 
phase in the mobile phase of a gas chromatograph. The mobile 
phase in GC is usually hydrogen or helium and is provided at 
a constant pressure or at a constant flow rate. The samples are 
typically in the form of a solution containing the analytes and 
the matrix which is placed in the gas mobile phase through a 
heated injection port using an injector. Solvent-less injections 
are also known and gaseous samples can as well be injected 
into a gas chromatograph. The sample is further physically 
separated in its components in the chromatographic column. 
The chromatographic column is placed in an oven with strictly 
controlled temperature that can be kept constant during the 
separation process (isocratic conditions) or can be modified 
following a specific program (gradient conditions). The sepa-
rated components of the sample are carried into a detector that 
generates an electric signal. A drawing showing a simplified 
diagram of a GC instrument is given in Figure 23.5.
The flow rate of the gas in GC influences the retention time 
tR for the separated components (see formula (23.5)). The 
injection port of a gas chromatograph has a specific tempera-
ture (constant or ramped up). A measured volume of sample 
is placed in the injection port where it is flash vaporized and 
introduced in the gas flow. The vapors from the sample and 
the sample solvent flow into the chromatographic column. The 
chromatographic column contains the stationary phase which 
has the role of separating the analytes among themselves and 
from other sample components (matrix and the solvent of the 
sample). The temperature of the column influences tR as shown 
by formulas (23.2) and (23.5). In modern GC instruments, vari-
ous parameters such as flow rate of the mobile phase, tempera-
ture of the injection port, and temperature program of the oven 
are computer controlled. The separated analytes are passing 
through one (or more) detectors and the electrical signal from 
the detector is electronically processed (amplified) and trans-
lated into a chromatogram.
23.2.5.1  The Injection Port and the Oven 
of a Gas Chromatograph
The injection port in a GC system has the role to include a 
specified amount of sample in the gas flow of the GC. Using 
a syringe, an injector (or autoinjector) places liquid samples 
(solution of the sample) with precise volumes such as 0.5, 1.0, 
or 2.0 ōL into the injection port that is set a specific tempera-
ture necessary to transform the sample in vapors (without 
sample decomposition). The autoinjector has the capability to 
select which sample to be injected from numerous samples in 
a tray containing vials with samples. Several types of injection 
port types are available. The common split/splitless injection 
port consists of a small glass-lined chamber placed in the gas 
flow and set to a temperature higher than the boiling point of 
the sample components and solvent such that the solvent and 
the sample are instantaneously vaporized. This type of injec-
tion port can work in a few different modes indicated as “split”, 
“splitless”, or “pulse splitless”. In split mode, only a part of the 
sample is sent to the chromatographic column, while in split-
less mode the whole sample is transferred. For liquid samples, 
other types of injection are available indicated as “cold on col-
umn” when the sample is injected directly into the front end of 
the chromatographic column, “multimode”, and “programmed 
temperature vaporizer” that allows the injection of a larger 
volume of sample with the capability of eliminating some of 
the vapors of sample solvent, etc.
Besides liquid sample injections in the gas chromatograph, 
various solvent-less type injections are possible. The direct 
injection of gases can be performed by loading the gas in a 
loop of specific volume that is connected to the gas flow of 
the instrument. However, most solvent-less type of injection 
uses preliminary sorption of the volatile analytes on a specific 
solid-phase adsorbent material that is suddenly desorbed in the 
gas flow of the chromatograph by rapid heating. Special instru-
ments can be used for this purpose, in a procedure indicated as 
purge and trap. Miniaturization of the procedure is achieved 
in techniques such as stir-bar sorptive extraction (SBSE), and 
solid-phase microextraction (SPME). In SBSE, the analytes 
are first absorbed on a small glass-coated magnetic stir-bar that 
contains on the glass surface an active absorbing phase. The 
stir-bar is exposed to the sample solution, retains the analytes, 
and then is removed from the sample solution and it is heated 
in a dedicated attachment to the GC for placing the analytes 
in the gas stream. In SPME, the analytes are first retained in 
the absorbing phase that is coated on a silica fiber that can 
be exposed or retracted in a syringe needle. The transfer of 
FIGURE 23.5  Simplified diagram of a gas chromatograph (source of gas, 
injection port, column placed in the oven, detector).

1245
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
the analytes to the GC gas flow is performed in the standard 
injection port of the GC where the fiber is introduced. The 
heating of the absorbing material releases the analytes previ-
ously retained in the fiber coating. Several coating materials 
are available for SPME such as polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS), 
and polyacrylate. SPME is a very successful technique utilized 
for the analysis of compounds that generate sufficient vapor 
pressure to be absorbed in the fiber coatings [6].
The oven of the GC provides a controlled temperature for 
the chromatographic column. In most GC systems, the set 
temperature can be kept within 0.1 °C, and a range between 
′ 100 °C and 400 °C. The low temperatures can be achieved 
using a cryogenic agent (liquid N2 or CO2) and higher tempera-
tures using electric heating. The GC ovens are commonly able 
to provide temperature gradients. Heating rates for the GC 
oven may vary, but standard ovens provide a heating rate as 
high as 30 °C/min with good temperature control. In common 
GC instruments, a sequence of isotherm and gradient por-
tions (usually, three or four ramps) is possible. As the retention 
times tR in GC strongly depend on the temperature (decreasing 
as temperature increases), by using different temperatures and 
heating rates, the separation in the chromatographic column 
can be modulated as necessary for achieving the desired result.
23.2.5.2  Columns in GC
The chromatographic columns in GC can be packed columns 
or open-tubular columns (capillary columns). The packed col-
umns contain solid particles of certain dimensions that can be 
coated with the stationary phase, while the capillary columns 
contain the stationary phase as a film on the inner wall of the 
tube. Although common in the past, the packed columns are 
much less frequently used than the capillary columns. The 
capillary columns are commonly made from silica and have 
an outer coating (polyimide, aluminum, etc.). Based on the 
internal diameter (i.d.), capillary columns are classified as 
microbore for i.d. < 0.1 mm, minibore for i.d. = 0.18 mm, nar-
row bore for i.d. = 0.25, regular for i.d. = 0.32 mm, and mega-
bore for i.d. = 0.53 mm. The stationary phase film thickness 
on the inner wall of the column is a very important param-
eter. The film thickness may be chosen between 0.1 ōm and 
5 ōm, a frequently utilized film thickness being 0.25 ōm. The 
length of the capillary columns can be between 5 m and 100 m, 
with 30 m being common. Conventional columns (minibore, 
narrow bore) have effective plate number n (per 1 m length) 
between 3,000 and 5,000. Dimensional parameters of the GC 
column, include column length L, internal diameter dc, and 
phase thickness df, the value of the phase ratio β for a capillary 
column being given by the ratio β = dc/4df. These parameters 
affect the retention time tR (see expression (23.5)), the resolu-
tion R, and the sample capacity Sc (or sample load). Sample 
load is the amount of sample that can be injected onto column 
without overload, the overload being the sample mass injected 
at which the column efficiency falls to 90% of its normal value. 
A summary of the dependence of tR, R, and Sc on dimensional 
parameters is indicated in Table 23.1.
A variety of stationary phase materials have been utilized 
for the gas chromatographic columns. The phases are evalu-
ated and selected based on different criteria, such as polarity, 
thermal stability, and lack of bleeding. Polarity of a stationary 
phase is usually characterized using McReynolds constants 
[7], which are obtained with the help of Kováts retention 
indexes. The constants are defined as the difference between 
the Kováts index IX for a reference compound X in the phase to 
be evaluated and the Kováts index IX
0 in a reference stationary 
phase that has been selected to be squalane.

with individual McReynolds (x’, y’, z’, u’, and s’) or with an

Based on P, the stationary phases are classified from the least 
polar phase, that is, squalene with P = 0, to the most polar 
phase, that is, 1,2,3-tris(2-cyanoethoxy)propane (TCEP) with 
P = 830.1. The polarity P of the column can be normalized by 
the value for TCEP (and multiplied by 100) and described as 
“Polarity” with values between 0 and 100.
The stationary phase in GC can be made from various mate-
rials. Common materials are specific polymers bonded on the 
inner surface of the capillary. One such material with polar-
ity P ≤ 5 is made of bonded PDMS (e.g., column indicated as 
DB-1, ZB-1, Rtx-1, SPB-1, HP-1, AT-1, BP1, etc., depending on 
the manufacturer). More polar than PDMS columns are the col-
umns containing a certain proportion (e.g., 5%, 35%, 50%) of 
diphenylpolysiloxane in addition to PDMS (indicated as DB-5, 
ZB-5, Rtx-5, ZB-5ms ZB-5MSi with P ≤ 8, DB-35, ZB-35, Rtx-35 
with P ≤ 18, ZB-50, Rtx-50, BPX50, DB-17, SPB-17 with P ≤ 24). 
Another type of column is using a polysiloxane backbone with 
attached cyanopropyl groups, in mixture with PDMS and some-
times also with phenylpolysiloxane. The columns containing 
14% cyanopropyl-phenyl and 86% dimethylpolysiloxane are 
indicated as DB-1701, ZB 1701, Rtx-1701, BP10, etc. and have 
P ≤ 19. Various other structures and combination of structures 
are commercially available, such as cyanopropylmethyl-poly-
siloxane, phenyl-arylene-polysiloxane plus dicyanopropylpoly-
siloxane, cyanopropyl-phenylpolysiloxane, or combinations of 
these phases with different proportions of dimethylpolysilox-
ane. These columns are indicated with various notations such as

DB 624, ZB-624, Rtx-624 but also DB-1301 and Rtx-1301. More

Selection of the chromatographic column for achieving the 
desired separation in GC is very important. Table 23.1 pro-
vides some guidance regarding the choice of specific dimen-
sional parameters for achieving a good resolution R and a 
retention time tR that accommodates the analysis require-
ments. However, the nature of the stationary phase and the 
temperature (gradient) selected for the GC oven are equally 
important factors for achieving the desired separation. The 
nature of the analytes dictates the selection of the nature of 
the stationary phase, and usually less polar compounds can be 
well separated based on their boiling point on less polar chro-
matographic columns, while more polar compounds are better 
separated on more polar columns when the boiling point of the 
compound still plays a certain role, but the compound polar-
ity is an important factor. Optimization of a GC separation 
remains to a significant extent an empirical process where the 
general rules previously indicated are used only as a guidance.
23.2.5.3  Detectors in GC
Several types of detectors are used in GC, some being non-
selective and providing only quantitative information, other 
being element specific, and other offering both the capabil-
ity of quantitative and qualitative identification (such as mass 
spectrometers or IR spectrophotometers). Elaborate descrip-
tions of different detectors can be found in the literature (see, 
e.g., [1, 4]). Detectors can be characterized by their sensitivity, 
linear range, stability, and selectivity. Two parameters describe 
sensitivity: the ratio of the detector response to the amount of 
sample (sensitivity slope) and the minimum detectable level of 
a given compound. Some characteristics of the detectors used 
in GC are indicated in Table 23.2.
Detectors are selected to have specific characteristics such 
as good sensitivity, reproducible response, large linear range, 
capability to use small volumes of sample, stability to the 
change in flow rate of mobile phase, lack of carry over (rea-
son for which the detectors in GC are usually heated to avoid 
condensations). The capability of qualitative identification of 
analytes is also selected based on the need for this type of

a	 The sensitivity units are dependent on the nature of detector. TCD detector responds to changes in concentration, while FID, NPD, etc. respond to 
mass of material entering the detector per unit of time. Also, the flows and the parameters in the detector may strongly influence sensitivity.

1247
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
analysis. A few of the common detectors used in GC are sum-
marily further described.
	
1.	The thermal conductivity detector (TCD) consists 
of a Wheatstone bridge with two resistors consist-
ing of heated filaments, one placed in the column 
flow, and the other in the flow of pure carrier gas. 
When the column flow is also pure carrier gas, the 
bridge is “balanced” and no current flows through 
the measuring device. When a compound from the 
column flow is passing by the heated filament, the 
thermal conductivity of the flowing gas is changed. 
This changes the filament temperature and its electri-
cal resistance, which makes the Wheatstone bridge 
become unbalanced, and a current is measured. This 
current depends on the concentration of the com-
pounds flowing by the heated filament. A schematic 
diagram of the TCD detector is shown in Figure 23.6. 
	
2.	The flame ionization detector (FID) is based on the 
formation of ions that are created by the combustion 
of an organic compound in a flame. The flame being 
kept in an electric field is changing its electric con-
ductibility. The schematic diagram of an FID detec-
tor is shown in Figure 23.7.
The FID uses a flame generated by the combustion 
of hydrogen in air. In this flame is also flowing the 
gas from the chromatographic column that carries 
the components of a sample. The flame is produced 
between two electrodes that provide a potential dif-
ference. The combustion of organic compounds gen-
erates ions in the flame. When the flame is obtained 
from pure hydrogen and air it has a very low number 
of ions. When ions are produced by the combustion 
of an analyte, they are attracted to the negative col-
lector plate of the detector, and a current is produced. 
This current is proportional to the rate of flame ion-
ization, which in turn depends on the concentration 
of the analytes in the sample.
	
3.	The electron capture detector (ECD) is used mainly 
for the analysis of electronegative compounds such 
as those containing in their structure halogen atoms. 
The ECD uses a radioactive metal such as a metal 
foil of 63Ni which generates μ-particles (electrons). 
A makeup gas, usually nitrogen, is also flown in the 
detector, such that the electrons emitted from the 63Ni 
collide with the molecules of the makeup gas, result-
ing in many more free electrons. These electrons are 
accelerated in the detector toward a positively charged 
anode, generating a current such that a permanent 
current flows from the detector. When an analyte 
passes through the detector, if it is an electron-absorb-
ing compound, it captures electrons and reduces the 
current between the collector anode and the cathode 
(the 63Ni metal foil). This current is proportional with 
the concentration of the analyte in the carrier gas of 
the GC. Besides halogens, the ECD is also sensitive to 
organometallic compounds, nitriles, and nitro com-
pounds. As indicated in Table 23.2, ECD can provide 
very high sensitivity but only toward electronegative 
compounds and does not generate an (appreciable) 
signal for other classes of molecules.
	
4.	The mass spectrometers (MS) are frequently used as 
detectors in GC offering the capability of compound 
quantitation and identification with exceptionally 
good sensitivity. GC-mass spectrometry (GC-MS) is 
a common analytical technique and a large amount of 
information is available regarding this subject (see, 
e.g., [8]). In GC-MS, the carrier gas containing the 
(separated) analytes from the GC is transferred to the 
mass spectrometer. The gas flow in the GC is com-
monly set between 0.1 and 3.0 mL/min (at slightly 
elevated pressure). Because the mass spectrometers 
operate at 10∞4 to 10∞7 mbar, they are equipped with 
efficient pumps, which maintain inside the mass 
spectrometer a high vacuum. The mass spectromet-
ric analysis starts with an ionization process. This 
ionization takes place in the ion source of the MS 
instrument, where the analytes are introduced as gas 
phase. There are two common ionization procedures 
used for GC-MS, electron ionization (EI) and chemi-
cal ionization (CI) (other ionization techniques are 
also known but are less common). The EI process 
consists of an electron bombardment, which is done

70 eV. The electrons are generated by thermionic 
effect from a heated filament and accelerated to the 
required energy. During the ionization, the electrons 
interact with the analyte molecules M from the car-

Several fragmentation paths are possible for a given molecule, 
but the nature and abundance of fragments are characteristic 
for a given compound.
Upon electron bombardment, negative ions can also be 
formed although EI-ionization is not frequently utilized. For 
CI, in addition to the carrier gas and the analytes coming from 
the GC column, a reagent gas (methane, butane, ammonia, 
etc.) is injected into the ion source of the mass spectrometer 
at a typical pressure of 10∞2 to 10∞3 mbar. The initial step in 
the ionization takes place by the electron impact on the mol-

to the analyte molecules) forming Rł+ ions (assuming positive 
CI (CI+)). Following this step, the Rł+ ions react with other R

The ions MH+ formed in reaction (23.18c) tend to remain un-

ions Mł+ generated by direct electron impact, reason for which 
CI+ ionization techniques are commonly used for the identi-
fication of the molecular weight MW of the eluted molecules 
in GC.
The ions generated in the MS source must be separated by 
the mass analyzer and measured using an internal detector. 
The separation power of the mass analyzer is characterized by 
its resolution expressed by the formula:
R
M
M
= ∆
                (23.19)
where β M is the closest spacing of two peaks (in mass units) of 
equal intensity with the valley between them less than a specified 
fraction of the peak height (e.g., 50%), and M is the mass of the 
second peak. Based on their resolution, mass spectrometers are 
usually classified as “low resolution”, and “high resolution” or 
HR (“medium resolution” and “ultra high resolution” are in some 
cases recognized). The low resolution typically discriminates 
ions with a difference in mass of 0.2 m.u. (R ≤ 1,000 for M = 200), 
while an HR instrument can discriminate ions with a difference 
in mass of 0.0001 m.u. (R ≤ 2,000,000 for M = 200).
Several types of mass analyzers are utilized for the ions 
separation, such as (a) quadrupole, (b) time of flight (TOF), 
(c) other ion separation techniques such as ion trap, magnetic 
sector, Wein filter, and ion cyclotron resonance.
	
a.	Quadrupole type mass spectrometer is a common 
mass analyzer which separates the ions with different 
mass/charge (m/z) by passing them in vacuum along 
the central axis of four parallel equidistant metal (or 
metalized) rods that have applied a fixed voltage (DC) 
and an alternating (RF) voltage. The field strengths 
(voltage) can be set such that only ions of one selected 
mass can pass through the quadrupole, while all 
other ions are deflected to strike the rods. The cyclic 
variation of the voltage applied on the quadruple rods 
allows the quadrupole to pass through one specific 
ion at a time. The mass range for common commer-
cial quadrupole instruments can be usually set during 
a cycle between 2 and 1,100 a.m.u. With the quadru-
pole instruments, a low-resolution type spectrum is 
obtained. A schematic diagram of a quadrupole mass 
spectrometer is shown in Figure 23.8.
After the separation based on their m/z, the ions 
are detected using different procedures. Two classes 
of internal detectors are known: point ion detectors, 
which are more common and detect the arrival of all

ions sequentially at one point, and array detectors, 
which detect all ions simultaneously on a surface. 
The internal detectors record the number (abun-
dance) of individual ions at each m/z. Several point 
ion detectors are available, such as Faraday cup, elec-
tron multiplier, and scintillator. The signal from the 
internal detector is further processed (amplified and 
analyzed) by an electronic data system.
	
b.	The TOF instruments separate the ions of differ-
ent m/z ratios based on their different energies. For 
example, reflection TOF uses a constant electrostatic 
field to reflect the ion beam toward the detector. The 
more energetic ions penetrate deeper into the path 
within the reflection lenses (reflectron) and take a 
longer time to reach the detector. Less energetic ions 
of the same m/z penetrate a shorter distance into the 
reflectron and take a shorter time to reach the detec-
tor. In this way, the ions reach the detector at dif-
ferent times based on their m/z ratio. A schematic 
diagram of a TOF is indicated in Figure 23.9.
TOF spectrometers work on scans using a pulsed 
ion source. The number of acquired spectra per sec-
ond for a TOF instrument can be as high as 500. 
Depending on their construction, some TOF instru-
ments generate low-resolution spectra and other HR 
(R ≤ 60,000). Some TOF instruments have a wider 
mass range than quadrupole instruments.
	
c.	Several other types of mass analyzers have been 
developed and are utilized. One such type is the 
ion-trap mass spectrometer that uses two electrodes 
of special shape and a combination of electric and 
magnetic fields which separates the ions based on 
their m/z ratio. Magnetic sector instruments were 
also used for the ion separation. In a magnetic sec-
tor instrument the ions from the ion source are sent 
through a non-homogeneous magnetic field where 
they are deflected on different arc trajectories based 
on their m/z ratio and focused using an electric 
field followed by detection. Magnetic sectors mass 
detectors can separate ions within a wide range of 
masses and can achieve HR with excellent sensitiv-
ity. The cost of magnetic sector instruments is how-
ever higher than for other types of mass detectors. 
A number of other techniques for the separation of 
ions are known, and dedicated literature is available 
regarding mass detectors (see, e.g., [9]).
In addition to single mass spectrometers, tandem mass spec-
trometers are powerful analytical instruments. In tandem mass 
spectrometry, also known as MS/MS, multiple steps of mass 
spectrometry separations are used, with some form of frag-
mentation occurring in between the MS stages. Also, mass 
spectrometry alone is used without the help of chromatogra-
phy in certain techniques such as MALDI with applications in 
toxicology (e.g., [10, 11]).
The ions generated from the fragmentation in the ion source 
of a mass spectrometer after being separated and recorded 
are processed using a software package to produce a plot rep-
resenting the relative abundance of each ion as a function of 
its m/z value. This plot is known as a mass spectrum. As an 
example, a mass spectrum of silylated myo-inositol obtained 
in EI+ mode at 70 eV is given in Figure 23.10. (Silylation is 
a derivatization reaction obtained using reagents such as N, 
O-bis(trimethylsilyl)-trifluoroacetamide 
(BSTFA) 
which 
replaces the active hydrogens from a compound with trimeth-
ylsilyl groups (TMS).)
The specificity of the numerous fragments generated by 
electron impact (e.g., at 70 eV) can be used as a fingerprint of 
the molecular species that generated it and allow the identifica-
tion of compounds by matching it with standard spectra found 
in mass spectral libraries The development of large libraries 
with standard spectra (over 675,000) such as Wiley Registry 
9th, NIST14, or NIST17 [12] and of algorithms for computer 
library searches made the use of this technique an invaluable 
tool for compound identification.
At each point along the chromatographic run, the sum of 
intensities of ions detected generates a signal plotted as a total 
ion chromatogram (TIC). The peak areas from the TIC are 
proportional to the compound amount that generates the cor-
responding chromatographic peak and allow the quantitation 
when corresponding calibration curves are generated. For 
quantitative measurements can also be used the peak areas 
generated using the intensity of a single ion in the chromato-
gram (procedure indicated as single ion monitoring). As an 
example, the TIC obtained from a sample of green tea with the 
plant material directly silylated is shown in Figure 23.11. For 
direct silylation, a small amount of plant material (e.g., 50 mg) 
is treated directly with a solvent such as dimethylformamide 
and with a silylating reagent such as BSTFA. The mixture is 
heated for about 30 min to promote the reaction, and the filtrate 
is analyzed by GC-MS. The replacement of the active hydro-
gens with Si(CH3)3 by silylation renders volatile many com-
pounds from the sample. The separation for the chromatogram 
FIGURE 23.9  Schematic diagram of a reflectron TOF.

1250
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
shown in Figure 23.11 was obtained on a DB-5 MS column 
from Agilent (Agilent Technologies Inc., Wilmington, DE, 
USA) heated between 50 °C and 300 °C. An internal standard 
(tert-butylhydroquinone) was included during the silylation 
process.
Mass spectra for each peak allow the identification of the 
corresponding compound. For the peaks from the chromato-
gram shown in Figure 23.11, the peak identification is given in 
Table 23.3. Peak areas from the chromatogram allow a quanti-
tation of the specific compound when a calibration with stan-
dards is previously made.
The previous example shows the capability of MS detector 
to allow both qualitative and quantitative analysis of a complex 
sample.
23.2.6  Selected Examples of the Use of 
GC in Toxicological Studies
Toxicology studying the adverse effects of chemical substances 
on living organisms involves a broad range of chemical analy-
ses. These analyses include that of chemical compounds in 
pharmaceuticals, nutraceuticals, agricultural products, food, 
FIGURE 23.10  Example of a mass spectrum obtained from silylated myo-inositol in EI+ mode at 70 eV.
FIGURE 23.11  TIC of a directly silylated green tea sample.

1251
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
beverages, environment, various consumer goods including 
tobacco products, and also of various metabolites in biologi-
cal fluids and living organisms. The range of concentrations 
that need to be detected is also very broad depending on the 
purpose of analysis or the compound to be analyzed. Many 
of the compounds to be analyzed in toxicological studies are 
sufficiently volatile and the limitation of GC to be useful only 
for the analysis of compounds with some volatility does not 
preclude GC and GC-MS to remain some of the most com-
mon and important tools used for analyses in toxicology. A 
large number of toxicological studies involve analytical data 
and such studies are reported in dedicated journals such as 
Journal of Analytical Toxicology (Oxford University Press), 
Toxicology (Elsevier), or Journal of Toxicology (Hindawi), 
Journal of Toxicology and Environmental Health (Taylor & 
Francis) as well as in books and other forms of communication.
GC-MS techniques were utilized, for example, for the eval-
uation of certain metabolic disruptions caused by toxicants 
(e.g., [13–15]). In one study [13], 3,4-methylenedioxypyrova-
lerone which is a stimulant not approved for medical use but 
documented for some cases of misuse and addiction in humans 
has been evaluated regarding the disruption of metabolic 
profile of mouse hepatocytes. An untargeted metabolomic 
approach based on GC-MS profiling and multivariate analysis 
of the data indicated clear differences between exposed cells 
and control cells. In another study [14], combined hepatotox-
icity of chlorpyrifos and cadmium has been studied in rats 
as model animals. The toxicity in the liver of rats was evalu-
ated for subchronic exposure to chlorpyrifos and cadmium at

environmentally relevant doses. After the animal treatment, 
the liver tissues were subjected to histopathological examina-
tion and a GC-MS technique was used to analyze the metab-
olomic changes in the rat liver. The results showed that the 
toxicants produced oxidative damage and disrupted energy, 
amino acid, and fatty acid metabolism in the liver.
GC-MS methods are also used for the measurement of toxi-
cant levels in biological specimens (e.g., [16–18]). For example, 
in one study [16] the effects of certain pyrethroids (tefluthrin, 
bifenthrin, −-cypermethrin, and deltamethrin) were evaluated 
on rats. Collected blood, liver, and cerebellum were analyzed 
6 hours after administration for the levels of pyrethroids using 
GC-ECD. The results provided information regarding expo-
sure-dose-effect relation for pyrethroids and were useful for 
designing pharmacokinetic models for environmentally rel-
evant exposures to pyrethroid mixtures. In another study [17], 
the levels of non-dioxin-like PCB (polychlorinated biphenyls) 
were measured in maternal blood during pregnancy for 169 
participants, and the associations between prenatal exposure 
to various PCBs and the gene methylation levels were evalu-
ated in infants. 4-Aminobiphenyl/hemoglobin (Hb) adducts in 
fetuses exposed to tobacco smoke in utero were measured in 
another study [18] using both GC-MS and high-performance 
LC (HPLC). The study demonstrated that 4-aminobiphenyl 
crosses the human placenta and binds to fetal hemoglobin 
in higher concentrations in smokers when compared to non-
smokers. Other studies were focused on the elucidation of the 
structure of certain toxicant or anti-toxicant compounds from 
various sources (e.g., [19]). As an example, from corn were 
isolated several fractions with anti-mutagenic activity against 
aflatoxin B1. These fractions were analyzed by GC-MS 
and among the compounds identified was linoleic acid [19]. 
Various studies were focused on the identification of toxic 
effect of approved drugs (e.g., [20]). For example, lethal intoxi-
cation with propofol was evaluated by using a GC-MS method 
to measure this compound in biological fluids.
Several studies regarding the possibilities to reduce the 
effect of certain toxicants using specific pharmaceuticals were 
also performed using GC-MS methods (e.g., [21, 22]). For 
example, sodium salicylate has been shown to be a promis-
ing antidote for the treatment of paraquat (N,N−-dimethyl-4,4−-
biphenyl dichloride) poisonings [21]. The study was focused 
on proving the possibility that a direct chemical reaction may 
take place between sodium salicylate and paraquat, besides 
the modulation of the pro-oxidant and pro-inflammatory path-
ways, and anti-thrombogenic properties of sodium salicylate. 
Detection of specific adducts was made using a variety of tech-
niques including GC-MS. In another study [22], the detoxifi-
cation of nerve agents cyclosarin, sarin, tabun, and VX (ethyl 
N-2-diisopropylaminoethyl methylphosphonothiolate) using 
μ-cyclodextrin derivatized with iodosobenzoic acid was evalu-
ated and GC-MS was used for the nerve agent quantitation. 
Numerous toxicants having non-polymeric molecules were 
also analyzed using GC-MS methods. One such example is 
the evaluation of the level of various μ-carbolines (harmine, 
harmaline N,N-dimethyl-tryptamine) in ayahuasca users [23]. 
The analyses were performed on samples of sweat which has 
advantages over other conventional biological samples because 
its collection procedure is non-invasive.
23.2.7  Overview of LC
LC covers a number of chromatographic techniques where 
the mobile phase is a liquid. Analytical applications of LC are 
very diverse, since most compounds can be dissolved in some 
type of solvent. Most analytical applications are using HPLC 
or ultra-high-performance LC (UHPLC). The HPLC (and 
UHPLC) techniques can be further differentiated based on 
criteria such as the nature of the stationary phase, the nature of 
the mobile phase, the type of interactions assumed to lead to the 
separation, and the range of concentration of specific solvents 
in the mobile phase (e.g., of water). The most common type of 
HPLC used for analytical purposes is reversed-phase HPLC 
(or RP-HPLC). This type of chromatography is performed on 
a non-polar stationary phase with a polar mobile phase. Other 
types of HPLC include hydrophilic interaction LC (HILIC), 
gel filtration and gel permeation, ion-pair, normal phase (NP), 
ion exchange (IEX), and other types less frequently utilized 
such as hydrophobic interaction chromatography, non-aqueous 
RP chromatography, normal phase chromatography, ion exclu-
sion chromatography, ligand exchange chromatography, and 
ion-moderated chromatography. Chiral HPLC is also utilized 
in toxicological studies.
23.2.8  Typical HPLC Instrumentation
The typical HPLC instrument includes the following com-
ponents: (1) a source of solvents (2) a pumping system for 
delivering a specific mobile phase, (3) an injector, (4) a chro-
matographic column, and (5) one or more detectors. The source 
of solvents consists of one or several reservoirs of liquids (sol-
vents such as water, methanol, acetonitrile) either pure or as 
solutions, for example of a buffer or a mixture of solvents. The 
dissolved gases in the mobile phase are usually eliminated by 
a degasser. The solvents are delivered to a high-pressure pump. 
The pump produces the mobile phase at high pressure up to 
about 1,000 bar and with a precise composition and flow rate. 
Modern HPLC pump parameters are computer controlled. 
Following the pump and before the chromatographic column 
is placed an injector that allows the placement in the mobile 
phase of a measured volume of sample usually dissolved in an 
appropriate solvent. The column performs the separation of 
the analytes between themselves and from the matrix compo-
nents. The mobile phase containing the separated components 
of the sample is further delivered to a detection system con-
sisting of one or more detectors. In modern instruments the 
detector(s) signal is processed by a dedicated software package 
that allows the display and evaluation of the chromatogram. 
A schematic diagram of a basic configuration of an HPLC is 
shown in Figure 23.12.
The mobile phase delivered by the pumping system may 
have a constant composition during the chromatographic 
run (indicated as isocratic mode) or the composition can be 
changed (indicated as gradient mode) using a specific program 
or “gradient time table”.
The high-pressure pumps used in analytical HPLC to 
deliver the mobile phase are usually reciprocating pumps, 
although syringe pumps are sometimes used. The reciprocat-
ing pumps are dual piston and driven to avoid flow fluctuations.

1253
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
A  dual-piston pump can handle only one solvent. However, 
since in HPLC it is frequently necessary to use gradient sep-
arations, instruments that handle more than one solvent are 
necessary. Three procedures are used to achieve the mixing 
of solvents: (1) low-pressure mixing, where the solvents neces-
sary for the gradient are premixed with a low-pressure pump 
placed in front of the high-pressure pump, (2) high-pressure 
mixing that uses two dual-piston high-pressure pumps, and (3) 
hybrid mixing (less frequently utilized).
The sample in HPLC, present in the form of a solution, is 
injected into the mobile phase flow using an injector capable 
to make a reproducible and accurate transfer into the mobile 
phase of a determined volume of sample solution. Typical 
HPLC systems have injectors capable to inject between 1 ōL 
and 20 ōL or between 1 ōL and 100 ōL sample solution (special 
systems can deliver larger volumes). For some UHPLC sys-
tems, the injection volumes can be as low as 20 nL. Injectors 
with automation capability (autoinjectors) are common and 
can inject specified volumes from numerous samples in a tray 
or to repeat the injection at a specified time being driven by 
computer packages controlling the modern HPLC systems.
The mobile phase with the injected sample (as a narrow plug 
in the flow) are passing further to the chromatographic column 
and then to the detector.
23.2.8.1  Chromatographic Column and Mobile 
Phase Composition in HPLC
The chromatographic column is designed for perform-
ing the separation in HPLC. The column typically con-
sists of a tube made from metal (stainless steel) or plastic 
(e.g., polyetherether-ketone) that is filled with a stationary 
phase. At the two ends inside the column are special frits that 
keep the stationary phase from moving, and outside are fittings 
that allow the connection with high-pressure tubing. The phys-
ical dimensions of common analytical chromatographic col-
umns vary, and values for length (internal) L can be between 
30 mm and 250 mm (common length 50, 100, 150, 250 mm), 
and internal diameters d can be between 1 mm and 10 mm 
(common diameters 2.1, 3.0, or 4.6 mm).
The nature of the stationary phase is selected based on the 
type of chromatography that is utilized for the separation (RP, 
HILIC, NP, IEX, size exclusion, etc.). A large assortment 
of types of stationary phases (column packings) is available 
(see, e.g., [4]). The stationary phase usually consists of small 
solid particles with special properties. Besides small particles, 
porous polymeric materials, and also monolithic materials 
can be used as stationary phase. When the stationary phase 
is made from small porous particles, these particles can be 
obtained from an inert porous substrate material (usually sil-
ica) that is covered with the active phase. The particles can be 
of three main types: porous, superficially porous (core-shell), 
and pellicular. Porous particles are the most common type of 
stationary phase used in HPLC. They are made from particles 
usually of 1.7–5 ōm diameter with a specific porous structure 
and with the surface of this structure covered with an active 
constituent. This constituent can be physically or chemically 
bonded on the solid inert support, the bonded phases being the 
most common type.
RP-HPLC is the most utilized technique for analytical pur-
poses and the largest variety of HPLC columns is of RP type. 
These columns have a hydrophobic active phase, for example 
with octadecyl groups (C18), octyl groups (C8), or phenyl 
bonded on porous silica. The stationary phases for these col-
umns are obtained using derivatization reactions of hydrated 
silica with compounds such as alkoxy-R-silanes conducted in 
FIGURE 23.12  Schematic diagram of a typical HPLC system.
	
		
(23.20)

1254
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
special conditions. An example of silica derivatization is indi-
cated below:
The radical R in reaction (23.20) is the desired group that 
acts as an active phase. In reaction (23.20) the resulting deriva-
tized silica still contains a number of reactive groups –OC2H5. 
The remaining reactive groups on the derivatized silica with 
di- or tri-functional reagents can be used for generating one 
layer of bonded phase or more layers (up to five), depending on 
derivatization conditions. The two types of derivatization are 
indicated as horizontal polymerization and vertical polymer-
ization. More complex types of derivatization with the attach-
ment of a variety of groups can be utilized, and for other types 
of chromatography than RP, the stationary phase may be made 
with bonded R groups depending on the type of HPLC column 
necessary. The mobile phase in RP-HPLC is typically a mix-
ture of water with an organic solvent (CH3CN, CH3OH, iso-
propanol, etc.), with a range of content in the organic solvent. 
Small amounts of buffers can also be added to the mobile phase 
in RP-HPLC. The types of molecules that can be analyzed by 
RP-HPLC cover a very large range. This includes small mol-
ecules with a variety of polarities, but also large molecules such 
as proteins and nucleic acids. The elution in RP-HPLC is more 
rapid when the mobile phase contains a more organic solvent 
and less water. The interactions in RP-HPLC are considered to 
be caused by the energies resulting from the disturbance of the 
dipolar structure of the mobile phase containing water (hydro-
phobic interactions).
In HILIC the stationary phase is polar and the columns usu-
ally contain a bonded phase with polar groups (diol, amino, 
special zwitterionic, etc.) or are made of bare hydrated silica. 
The mobile phase in HILIC is less polar than the stationary 
phase, but it contains water and polar solvents such as CH3OH 
or CH3CN. HILIC separations are useful for the analysis of 
very polar molecules. The separation in HILIC is based on the 
difference in polarity and ion-polar interactions between the 
molecules, and the order of elution is the opposite to the one 
noticed for RP-HPLC.
In IEX HPLC the stationary phase can be silica based 
or polymeric (e.g., latex agglomerated ion exchangers). 
For silica-based stationary phases, the hydrated silica is 
covered with the immobilized stationary phase that can 
be strong or weak cation exchange or anion exchange and 
contains ionizable groups, such as –SO3
∞H+ or –COO∞
H+ for cation exchange phases and –N(CH3)3+OH∞ or –
NH(CH3)2+OH∞ for anion exchange phases. Polymeric 
materials with similar active groups are also used as sta-
tionary phase. The mobile phase in IEX HPLC is usually 
a buffer solution selected depending on the type of col-
umn. Ion exchange separation is mainly based on coulom-
bic interactions and it is used mainly for the separation of 
ionic species.
Gel permeation (GPC) and gel filtration (GFC) are tech-
niques based on size exclusion and are used mainly for the 
separation of macromolecular species between themselves 
and from small molecules. Stationary phase in GPC and 
GFC are porous materials either silica based, dextran based, 
or polymeric. A variety of materials with specific pore sizes 
are used to separate the molecules. In gel filtration, an aque-
ous (or mostly aqueous) solution is used to transport the sample 
through the column and is applied to molecules that are soluble 
in water and polar solvents. Gel permeation is used mainly for 
the separation of hydrophobic macromolecules and the mobile 
phase is non-aqueous.
Chiral chromatography is usually performed on chiral 
stationary phases. These phases have a specific structure 
containing specific groups indicated as chiral selectors. 
These selectors are capable of interactions with the chiral 
molecules to be separated in three different points of the 
molecule. On porous silica, for example, are bonded phases 
containing moieties able to establish both donor and acceptor 
hydrogen bonds, and groups able to establish either ′ -donor, 
′ -acceptor, or both ′ -donor and ′ -acceptor intermolecular 
bonds. These phases are known as “brush” or Pirkle type. 
Other chiral phases are based on modified cellulose, modi-
fied cyclodextrins, macrocyclic antibiotics immobilized on 
silica, etc. In most chiral separations, the mobile phase does 
not contain water and is low in strong polar solvents that 
are capable of disturbing the formation of hydrogen bonds 
between the analyte and stationary phase. Chiral chromatog-
raphy on achiral stationary phases is also possible for some 
chiral solutes, by using chiral modifiers in the mobile phase, 
although the stationary phase is not chiral. Chiral separa-
tions are frequently necessary in toxicological and pharma-
cological studies related to the analysis of many bioactive 
compounds. Other HPLC types use a variety of column and 
mobile phases, and information is available in the literature 
regarding this subject (see, e.g., [1, 4]).
The selection of a chromatographic column and mobile 
phase is important for achieving a desired separation in HPLC, 
with adequate retention times tR and good resolution R. A large 
body of information is available regarding this process (see, 
e.g., [4]). Also, computer programs have been developed for 
guiding the selection and optimization of an HPLC separa-
tion using computer simulation [24]. Computer programs can 
also assist regarding the transferring of a method from one 
column to an equivalent one [25]. The nature of the molecules 
to be separated plays an essential role in the selection of the 
appropriate HPLC method. For non-polymeric analytes, the 
molecular polarity can be used as an important guidance for 
selecting the type of HPLC to be utilized. One common way to 
characterize polarity is based on octanol/water partition coef-
ficient Kow defined for a compound X by the expression:

mutually saturated phases of octanol and water, respectively. 
The selection of the HPLC type can be guided from the values 
of log Kow (sometimes noted log P) available in the literature [26] 
or on the web [27]. Based on log Kow the selection of HPLC type 
can be made using the scheme shown in Figure 23.13. However, 
the values of log Kow provide only a general guide for the selec-
tion of HPLC type and the details regarding the selection of the 
column and mobile phase composition is a more elaborate pro-
cess based in part on empirical trials. For the separation of poly-
mers, size exclusion is frequently utilized, although proteins and 
nucleic acids separations can be done on a variety of columns.

1255
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
23.2.8.2  Detectors in HPLC
In HPLC, the most common detectors are the following: (1) spec-
trophotometric UV-Vis, (2) refractive index, (3) fluorescence, (4) 
mass spectrometric (MS) and tandem mass spectrometric (MS/
MS), and (5) other detectors less frequently utilized such as che-
miluminescence, various types of electrochemical detectors, 
and evaporative light-scattering (ELS). The detector selection in 
HPLC depends on several factors such as the nature of the ana-
lytes, the purpose of the HPLC analysis, the nature of the mobile 
phase, and the availability of a specific detector in the laboratory. 
Depending on the nature of the analyte and the detector construc-
tion, the role and the sensitivity of the detector may vary signifi-
cantly. A short description of the main types of detectors in HPLC 
is further presented.
	
1.	Spectrophotometric detectors are basically UV-VIS 
spectrophotometers equipped with a flow-through 
cell. A beam of monochromatic light passes through 
a flow cell having a small volume (e.g., 1 ōL for a 
micro cell, 5 ōL for a semi-micro, and 14 ōL for a 
standard cell, with a path length of 5–10 mm depend-
ing on the cell). The UV-VIS detectors may have a 
fixed wavelength, a variable wavelength capability 
with unique wavelength detection, or variable wave-
length capability with multiple wavelength detection 
(such as the diode array detector). Similar to a com-
mon spectrophotometer, an absorbance A−Δ of the elu-
ent is measured and it is related to the instantaneous 
molar concentration CX of the absorbing molecular 
species based on the Lambert-Beer law:
'
 
A
C L
X
ε
=
λ
λ
              (23.22)
where ωΔ is the molar absorption (absorbance) coef-
ficient at the specific wavelength λ, of the analyte and 
L is the path length of the light through the sample. 
The absorbance A'Δ across the chromatographic peak 
is translated into a peak area AX, and used for the 
determination of concentrations based on calibra-
tions (see formula (23.12)). The UV-Vis detector can 
be used for the measurement of a large number of 
compounds that have absorption bands in the range 
200–600 nm. If the entire UV-Vis absorption spec-
trum is measured across a chromatographic peak 
(as it is done with the UV-Vis diode array detectors 
or DAD), in some cases the spectrum can be used 
for qualitative identification of the analyte (although 
UV-Vis spectra are seldom sufficient for compound 
identification). Sensitivity of the UV detection 
depends on the detector construction, but mainly 
of the molar absorbance of individual compounds. 
The consistency of the UV spectrum across a chro-
matographic peak can be used for the evaluation of 
peak purity. UV-Vis detection can be used for elution 
using solvent gradients with the condition that the 
solvents used as mobile phase have low absorbance 
at the measuring wavelengths for the analyte. Tables 
with UV cutoff values for various solvents are avail-
able in the literature [1] (the UV cutoff value for a 
solvent is defined as the wavelength λ at which the 
absorbance A− vs. air in a 1 cm cell is equal with 1.0).
	
2.	Refractive index detectors (RI or RID) are based on 
the modification of the refractive index of a solution 
as a function of the concentration of the solute. RI is a 
“universal” detector and it is not selective to specific 
compounds. This type of detection can be applied 
to compounds that do not have chromophore groups 
or fluorescence-bearing groups. However, it must be 
used only in HPLC separations using isocratic mode 
and usually the sensitivity of the RI detection is not 
very high.
	
3.	Fluorescence detectors (FLD) are another type 
of detector in HPLC. They are applied to measure 
analytes that display fluorescence. The fluores-
cence emission with the intensity Fint at the emis-
sion wavelength λ2 can be expressed as a function 
of the intensity I0 of the excitation radiant energy 
with wavelength λ1 incoming into the sample, by the 
expression:

where a is a constant coefficient that incorporates

radiation. The output of the detector is given in lumi-

proportional with the fluorescence intensity, but 
specific for the measuring instrument. Expression 
(23.26) shows that the fluorescence intensity is pro-

based on peak area calibration with standards (see 
rel. (23.12)). Depending on the nature of the analyzed 
material, the detection limit using FLD can be as low 
as 10–2–10–3 ng/mL, with a linear range of four orders 
of magnitude. When appropriately selected, the use 
of elution gradient can be applied for separation 
without interfering with the fluorescence.
	
4.	Mass spectrometric detectors offer one of the best 
detection procedures for HPLC. This type of detec-
tor responds to the instantaneous amount of solute 
passing through the detector. LC-MS and LC-MS/
MS can provide exceptional sensitivity and selec-
tivity compared to other detection techniques 
achieving sensitivities as low as ng/mL levels and 
the capability to differentiate between molecules 
with different masses and fragmentation patterns. 
Two common types of ion sources are used for ion 
generation in LC-MS, indicated as electrospray ion-
ization (ESI) and atmospheric pressure CI (APCI). 
The  schematic diagram of an ESI ion source is 
shown in Figure 23.14.
The APCI ion source is similar with the ESI 
source, but the effluent from the HPLC is sent 
through a heated capillary and in a flow of gas, and 
not under an electrical potential. The jet of mol-
ecules of solvent and analytes plus those of added 
gas (N2, O2) flow by a needle charged at a high volt-
age (3–5 kV) that generates a corona discharge. Both 
sources are designed to transfer to the mass spec-
trometer only the ions formed in the source, with 
the neutral molecules such as those of the solvent 
mostly eliminated. This allows the source to remain 
at atmospheric pressure. The production of positive 
ions in these techniques is the result of formation of 
molecular ions of the type M-H+ where M are the 
molecules of the analyte. Molecules of the type M–H 
can be ionized to generate negative ions M∞. In gas 
phase, the proton affinity is characterized by the gas-
phase basicity (GPB) which is the energy (enthalpy) 
released in the reaction:
	
M + H+ κ [M–H] +
(23.27)
The ionization reaction (23.27) is characterized by 
the free enthalpy β G0
GPB given by the expression:
