(23.28)
The ionization reactions are always exergonic in the 
gas phase. However, proton affinities are convention-
ally quoted with the opposite sign from other ther-
modynamic properties (exothermic reactions having 
assigned a negative free enthalpy), and a positive 
value of β G0
GPB indicates a release of energy by the 
system. The higher is the proton affinity, the stron-
ger is the base and the weaker is the conjugate acid 
in the gas phase. Usually, β G0
GPB for most organic 
compounds lies between 500 and 1,000 kJ/mol.
Similar to the formation of positive ions, mol-
ecules of the type M–H can be ionized to generate 
negative ions in a reaction as follows:
	
M–H κ M ∞ + H+
(23.29)
The difference in the polarity between the solvent 
and the analyte molecules favors the formation 
FIGURE 23.14  Schematic diagram of the ESI ions source showing for-
mation of analyte ions.

1257
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
of positive ions (or negative ions when working in 
negative mode) from the analyte and much less from 
the solvent. Adducts between analyte molecule and 
different ions reaching the MS interface are often 
observed in LC-MS. They are formed by ion-dipole, 
ion-induced dipole, hydrogen bonds, and even by van 
der Waals interactions.
Due to the high concentration of the solvent 
(although it is less ionized than the analyte), it is com-
mon that some of its molecules are also ionized to 
form ions [Solv-H]+ (or in the case of molecules of the 
type Solv-H to form by negative ionization [Solv]∞). 
Further ionization of the analyte can be described as 
a proton transfer from the ionized solvent to the mol-
ecule of the analyte. For this reason, the sensitivity of 
the MS detectors depends on the nature of the mobile 
phase. The ions formed in the ion source are further 
separated and analyzed in the mass spectrometer. In 
both ESI and APCI ion sources mainly the molecu-
lar ions are formed. Further fragmentations of the 
analytes for MS/MS applications are achieved in a 
collision cell.
MS/MS detection (tandem type) is a very sensitive 
and selective procedure used in HPLC. A common 
tandem mass spectrometer type is the triple quadru-
pole. In a triple quadrupole the first quadrupole (Q1) 
has the role of separating the precursor (or parent) 
ions generated in the ion source. These molecular 
ions are selected for further interactions in the colli-
sion cell (Q2) where they can be fragmented. For this 
purpose, a gas (such as N2 or Ar) is introduced in the 
cell, and a specific voltage is applied to the Q2 quad-
rupole (or hexapole in some instruments). Depending 
on the collision gas pressure and on the voltage 
applied to the collision cell, the parent ions undergo 
different degrees of fragmentation (fragmentation by 
collisionally activated dissociation). The fragmenta-
tion strongly depends on the structural characteristics 
of the analyte. The third quadrupole Q3 is used for 
the separation of the resulting product (or daughter) 
ions caused by the fragmentation in the collision cell. 
Following Q3 the ions are detected by procedures 
similar to those used in GC-MS. The measurement 
applied for a number of compounds but using a single 
precursor ion and a single product ion for each com-
pound is known as MRM mode (multiple ion moni-
toring). MRM detection mode provides unmatched 
sensitivity of detection but is not useful for qualita-
tive analysis. Depending on the nature of the analyte, 
levels as low as a few pg/mL in the injected solution 
can be measured using MRM detection.
Not only triple quadrupoles can be used in MS/
MS instrumentation. Other types of MS/MS include 
ion-trap + quadrupole, quadrupole + time of flight 
(Q-TOF), and also orbitrap mass analyzers. Orbitrap 
mass analyzers are a special type of ion traps con-
sisting of an outer barrel-like electrode and a coax-
ial inner spindle-like electrode that traps ions in an 
orbital motion around the spindle and convert the 
resulting frequency signal to a mass spectrum using 
the Fourier transform (FT). This type of instrument 
provides HR spectra.
TOF mass spectrometric detectors (MS-TOF) can 
also be associated with an additional electrosepara-
tion based on ion mobility (IM or ion mobility spec-
troscopy, IMS). In this technique, the ions of the 
analyte move in an electrical field against a carrier 
buffer gas. Since collisions with gas molecules have 
a higher probability for larger molecules than for the 
smaller ones, a separation based on size is obtained. 
The separation in IM occurs at a time scale of mil-
liseconds (in the range 10–1 to 10–3 seconds), while 
in TOF mass spectrometers the detection occurs on 
a microseconds scale making the MS-TOF a suit-
able detection procedure for IMS. Using a gating 
mechanism at specified intervals of time a burst of 
ions is introduced into a drift chamber. The ions are 
moved in one direction in a linear electric field gra-
dient through the drift chamber against a flow of a 
buffer gas. The buffer gas can be He, N2, or other 
common gases and can have ambient pressure or 
reduced pressure (a few torr). After the IM drift tube, 
the mass spectrometer is used as a detector. Before 
the detector it is also possible to include a collision 
cell that produces fragmentation (product/daughter 
ions) useful for further analyte differentiation. In this 
way, molecular species can be separated based on 
their mass, charge, size, and shape, depending on the 
interactions with the buffer gas.
	
5.	Several other types of detectors are used in HPLC, 
including electrochemical detectors that can be of 
several types such as amperometric, coulometric, 
potentiometric, and conductometric detectors. These 
detectors are using a flow cell where a specific elec-
trochemical property is measured. For example, in 
amperometric detectors the electrical current mea-
sured from the oxidation of the analyte at the surface 
of an inert electrode is measured. In conductomet-
ric detectors the conductivity of the mobile phase 
is measured and changes detected when the analyte 
elutes are used for generating the chromatogram.
Less commonly utilized are chemiluminescence detectors 
which are based on the emission of light resulting from a cer-
tain chemical reaction. When applicable (luminescent mol-
ecules are not very common), such detectors can measure as 
low as a mol/mL of analyte.
Another less common type of detector is the ELS (or ELSD). 
ELSD uses the formation of particles that do not evaporate and 
can scatter light, while the mobile phase forms a gas by evapora-
tion. In this technique, the eluent is injected in the form of a spray 
from a nebulizer into a drift tube which is heated and the solvent 
is evaporated while nonvolatile molecules form a fine mist. This 
mist passes through a cell that is illuminated with a beam of light 
and the scattered light from the mist is recorded as a signal.
Other detection types were also used in HPLC such as 
Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectrometry, Raman 
spectrometry, nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), circular

1258
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
dichroism, optical rotatory dispersion, polarimetry, radioac-
tivity, plasmon resonance, and multi-angle light scattering. 
However, such detection procedures were used only in particu-
lar applications and are not routinely used in HPLC detection.
23.2.9  Selected Examples of the Use of 
HPLC in Toxicological Studies
The application of HPLC methods in toxicological studies is 
very common and essential for solving numerous problems. 
As indicated for the use in toxicology of GC and GC-MS, 
there are dedicated journals such as Journal of Analytical 
Toxicology, Toxicology or Journal of Toxicology publishing a 
large volume of information regarding numerous HPLC appli-
cations. The versatility of HPLC associated with the high sen-
sitivity of detection techniques such as MS or MS/MS makes 
this technique an invaluable tool in toxicological studies. 
These studies cover a variety of topics.
One common subject is the evaluation of metabolic changes 
caused by various pharmaceutical drugs that inadvertently may 
have adverse side effects. For example, acute kidney injury can 
be induced by the use of cisplatin. A metabolomics study on 
rats has been used for the identification of nephrotoxic mecha-
nisms of cisplatin and the identification of biomarkers of cispl-
atin-induced injury [28]. The metabolomics analysis was used 
to characterize key changes in metabolite levels during cispl-
atin-induced acute kidney injury in rats, by screening a large 
number of biomarkers in rat urine and kidney using HPLC-
TOF-MS. In a different type of study [29], testing of the levels 
in urine of several benzodiazepine compounds such as alpha-
hydroxyalprazolam, oxazepam, lorazepam, nordiazepam, and 
temazepam was performed by LC-MS/MS with the purpose to 
evaluate possibilities to reduce the use of those drugs. Another 
example using an LC-MS/MS technique [30] is the evaluation 
of the effects of vitamin E analogs on the metabolism of litho-
cholic acid. Lithocholic acid is a cytotoxic bile acid oxidized 
by human cytochrome P450 3A (CYP3A) to form 3-ketochola-
noic acid, but it is not known whether this metabolite is also 
cytotoxic. The study demonstrated that 3-ketocholanoic acid 
is also cytotoxic, and that č-tocotrienol increases lithocholic 
acid cytotoxicity.
In another study [31], cytotoxic effects of 1,3-bis(2-
chloroethyl)-1-nitrosourea to human glioma cells have been 
proven to be increased by the use of 3-bromopyruvate. The 
analysis of the mechanism for this effect was performed 
using HPLC-ESI-MS/MS. The resistance to chloroethylni-
trosoureas of tumor cells is associated with the presence of 
O6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase that metabolizes 
the chloroethylnitrosoureas. In order to decrease this effect, a 
glycolytic inhibitor, 3-bromopyruvate, was used such that the 
cytotoxic effects of chloroethylnitrosoureas to human glioma 
cells are increased. Cytotoxicity of antiviral drugs cidofovir 
and tenofovir in cultures of human proximal tubular (hPT) 
cells was evaluated in another study using LC-MS/MS [32]. 
In this study, acute cytotoxicity of cidofovir in the absence 
or presence of probenecid, and of tenofovir disoproxil fuma-
rate in the absence or presence of probenecid was assessed 
after four hours incubations by determinations of release of 
lactate dehydrogenase, ć-glutamyltransferase, N-acetyl-μ-
d-glucosaminidase or kidney injury molecule-1. The results 
demonstrate the importance of plasma membrane transport of 
antiviral drugs to elicit cytotoxicity in the hPT cells.
The effect of specific chemicals on various organs such 
as brain, kidney, and liver (e.g., [33–35]) and the monitoring 
of specific toxicants in humans or experimental animals are 
also studied using HPLC techniques with various detectors, in 
particular MS/MS type. In a study regarding brain poisoning 
[33], the use of solid lipid nanoparticles pralidoxime chloride-
loaded for the reactivation of organophosphorus-inhibited ace-
tylcholinesterase in the central nervous system was evaluated 
using an HPLC-APCI-MS method for the assay of pralidox-
ime chloride in plasma and brain. In another study [34], the 
mechanism of integration of antimony in the red blood cells 
and the associated metabolic process was studied in rats using 
HPLC-MS/MS. The study showed that most of Sb metabolites 
were integrated into Hb but Hb-conjugated Sb did not affect 
Hb oxygen binding capability. In another study [35] using 
UHPLC-MS/MS with isotope dilution the evaluation of renal 
toxicity of aristolochic acids (AAs) has been performed. AAs 
(represented mainly by AA I and II) are a group of nitrophen-
anthrene carboxylic acids found in certain plants that have 
been used medicinally but are associated with renal toxicity 
and carcinogenicity in experimental animals and humans. The 
evaluation of the levels of DNA adducts with AAs performed 
using UHPLC-MS/MS provided information regarding the 
mechanisms that produce the toxicity in human renal cells. 
Another study [36] evaluated the optimal conditions for using 
human adrenal H295R cells to detect chemicals interfering 
with the production of key adrenal steroids. Several conditions 
and model compounds were used for this purpose, involving 
LC-MS analysis of the supernatants obtained from human 
adrenal H295R cells treated with various compounds.
The evaluation of the level of active compounds from com-
monly used chemicals, food, pharmaceuticals, or recreational 
drugs (e.g., [37–44]) is another area where HPLC is frequently 
used as an analytical tool. For example, chlormequat, which is 
a common plant growth regulator affects the rat embryo growth 
both “in vitro” and “in vivo”. Serum collected from the rats 
was analyzed using LC-MS/MS proving that exposure of chlor-
mequat chloride during pregnancy may affect the embryonic 
development [37]. In another study, LC-MS/MS has been used 
for distinguishing between dermal contact and the administra-
tion of heroin by using the analysis of fingerprints [38]. The 
samples were analyzed for morphine, codeine, 6-acetylcodeine, 
6-acetylmorphine, and noscapine. It was demonstrated that 
heroin and 6-acetylmorphine were detected in all fingerprint 
samples produced from contact with heroin, even after hand 
washing. In contrast, morphine, 6-acetylcodeine, and noscap-
ine were successfully removed after hand washing. The study 
provided information for deciding the type of recent contact 
with heroin. In another study, 14 benzodiazepines were ana-
lyzed in meconium samples using an LC-MS/MS technique. 
The study demonstrated the possibility to use the technique in 
clinical and forensic evaluations [39].
The levels of aflatoxins and ochratoxin A in black and green 
tea with fungal contamination were evaluated in a study using

1259
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
HPLC with fluorescence detection [40]. The study concluded 
that regular monitoring in the tea processing must be imple-
mented for improving the product quality and diminishing 
the potential levels of mycotoxin formation. In another study 
[41] the metabolic changes in rats before and after birth were 
evaluated in relation with caffeine intake. The study attempted 
to explain the increased incidence of intrauterine growth retar-
dation and diseases after birth caused by prenatal caffeine 
exposure. Changes in amino acids and lipid metabolism were 
detected to be caused by caffeine exposure using HPLC-type 
analysis with separation on a HILIC column. Studies for the 
identification and quantitation of cannabinoids in blood were 
also performed using HPLC-HR-MS/MS on an Orbitrap-type 
instrument [42]. The described procedure offers higher speci-
ficity due to the use of high-resolution mode separating back-
ground ions from the targeted molecules.
The pharmaceutical drug flibanserin or 1-(2-{4-[3-(trifluo-
romethyl)phenyl]piperazin-1-yl}ethyl)-1,3-dihydro-2H-benz-
imidazol-2-one, a serotonin regulator, has been analyzed in 
rat plasma and brain tissue using a UHPLC-MS/MS technique 
[43]. The study showed that the level of the drug in plasma 
is significantly higher than in the brain tissue, providing 
information regarding the bioavailability of this compound. 
LC-MS/MS techniques [44] were also developed for the 
analysis in plasma and urine of flualprazolam (8-chloro-6-(2-
fluorophenyl)-1-methyl-4H-[1,2,4]triazolo[4,3-a][1,4]benzodi-
azepine). In addition, several metabolites such as −-hydroxy 
flualprazolam glucuronide, 4-hydroxy flualprazolam glucuro-
nide, and the parent glucuronide were analyzed and found as 
most abundant signals in urine for the drug use.
HPLC technique with mass spectrometric detection were 
also used for the study of specific metabolic processes (e.g., 
[45, 46]). For example, in vitro metabolism of the dental resin 
monomer leachates such as bisphenol A-glycidyl methac-
rylate, 
2,2-bis-(4-(3-methacryloxypropoxy)phenyl)propane, 
bis-(acryloyloxymethyl)tricyclo-[5.2.1.0.sup.2,6]decane, 
and 
di-2-methacryloxyethyl 2,2,4-trimethylhexamethylene-dicar-
bamate were evaluated in vitro using human liver microsomes 
and human liver cytosols, with analyses performed by LC-MS/
MS on a Q-TOF instrument [45]. Various biotransformation 
of the monomers were studied providing information for pos-
sible monitoring of the leachates in body fluids. A number of 
other metabolic processes were studied with HPLC techniques. 
In one such study, the role of interleukin-8 on benzo[a]pyrene 
metabolism and DNA damage in human lung epithelial cells 
[46] was evaluated. The levels of metabolite B[a]P-7,8-diol 
were measured “in vitro” using pulmonary BEAS-2B cells and 
HPLC with fluorescence detection for the analyte measurement.
The study of active components from various sources such 
as plants is another subject where HPLC techniques are exten-
sively utilized. For example, evaluation of the level of certain 
active constituents in a Ginkgo biloba (seed) extract such as 
vitexin and isovitexin were measured using an HPLC method 
with UV detection at 337 nm, the results being corroborated 
with cholinesterase activity from the brain of Javanese medaka 
(Oryzias javanicus) fish exposed to the extract [47].
The versatility of HPLC techniques, exceptional selectivity 
and sensitivity, capability to be applied on samples in aqueous 
media, the small amount of sample necessary for analysis, and 
other intrinsic qualities of HPLC made these techniques an 
essential part of many toxicological studies. This present short 
review captures only a general view of the technique and only 
a minute part of its numerous applications in toxicology.
23.3  SFC
(Dr. Mehdi Ashraf-Khorassini and Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
As seen in other sections of this chapter, Sections 2 and 4–9, 
on analytical methodologies linked to toxicology mechanism 
studies, some form of chromatography plays a key role in a 
significant portion of those studies. Chromatography from its 
earliest conceptions, regardless of type, was recognized as a 
separation process, wherein, very often, components of com-
plex mixtures could be well separated [1, 2], thus coupled with 
arrays of information-rich detectors, facilitating identification 
and quantification. The separation mechanisms in most cases 
have been well defined and can be attributed to the extent/
degree of interactions between the sample analytes, the mobile/
liquid phase, and the components (stationary phase) contained 
within the chromatographic column [3]. The identification and 
quantification of specific analytes can be seriously hampered 
in the presence of other components, such as complex mixtures 
like biological fluids and tissue, components of most all toxi-
cological investigations. Hence, the capability to effectively 
separate each individual component of the complex mixture 
is seen as a distinct advantage. Among the more popular types 
of chromatography employed in toxicology mechanism studies 
are gas (GC), and HPLC, vide supra. Incorporating an array 
of information-rich detectors, these separation processes have 
and continue to be widely applied. While not yet on the same 
level of use as GC and HPLC, SFC is on the verge of takings 
its place as a valuable, viable, and complementary approach in 
the study of toxicological mechanisms, vide infra.
Before providing a discussion of SFC applications to the 
study of toxicological mechanisms, a brief description of 
supercritical fluids, the SFC technique, and its advantages and 
disadvantages should be addressed. At the heart of any chro-
matographic process is the nature of the fluid (mobile phase) 
that facilitates the transport of the components of any mix-
ture through the chromatographic/separation process. In the 
case of GC and HPLC, the mobile phases are a gas (often He) 
and a liquid (both polar and non-polar single liquids and or 
mixtures such as acetonitrile, methanol, ethanol, ethylacetate, 
and mixtures thereof), respectively. The rates of movement of 
these phases through the GC and HPLC columns, are often 
for GC and HPLC expressed in linear velocity (cm/s) and mL/
min, respectively. For SFC, the mobile phase, a fluid under its 
supercritical fluid conditions, is very notably different in its 
characteristics from GC and HPLC mobile phases, but SFC 
flow rates can be measured in mL/min.
A supercritical fluid is created most often from agas or rela-
tively low boiling liquid that are themselves subjected to the 
appropriate critical temperature, Tc, and the critical pressure, 
Pc, values linked with that material [4]. Establishment of the 
conditions necessary for the creation of a supercritical fluid 
represents a change in physical phase, the material now not 
possessing, solely, the characteristics of a gas nor a liquid.

1260
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
The general properties of supercritical fluids that make them 
very attractive as chromatographic mobile phases are high dif-
fusivity (gas-like), low viscosity (gas-like), zero surface ten-
sion, tunable solubility characteristics, and for CO2, non-toxic 
[4]. As readily observed, these combined characteristics are 
notably different from gases or liquids. Serendipitously, the Tc 
and Pc values for a number of readily obtainable materials are 
well within reach of moderate hardware specifications. For He, 
CO2, SF6, Xe, and CHF3, the Tc (°C) values are ′ 268, 31.3, 
45.5, 16.6, and 25.9, respectively [4]. The Pc (atm) values for 
these materials are 2.24, 72.9, 37.1, 58.4, and 46.9, respectively 
[4]. It can be readily envisioned that these materials could find 
application as a supercritical fluid in SFC applications. This is 
especially true regarding CO2 [5–7].
Hardware possessing the capability of establishing condi-
tions to create a supercritical fluid, then the pumping capabil-
ity of passing the supercritical fluid through a chromatographic 
column, also under supercritical fluid conditions, became a 
reality and has been practiced since ~1970 [4]. Technological 
advances in hardware, fluid flow control, temperature stability 
control, detectors, modifier flow control, and column devel-
opments have served to advance SFC technology over these 
~50 years. In terms of column technologies, it is of note that 
both open tubular (ot, common with GC approaches) [8] and 
packed column (common with HPLC approaches) (pc) SFC [9] 
are now routinely practiced as components of SFC analytical 
techniques, with packed column supercritical fluid chromatog-
raphy (pcSFC) the most widely employed. The documented 
applications of SFC range over a wide array including chiral 
separations [10], pharmaceutical investigations [11], petroleum 
research [12], food [13], polymer and personal care products 
[14]. Thus, SFC may be viewed as a form of NP chromatog-
raphy, like LC, that uses a supercritical fluid such as carbon 
dioxide as the mobile phase. The underlying principles of SFC 
are very similar to those of HPLC, however, SFC typically 
utilizes carbon dioxide in its supercritical or subcritical state 
as the mobile phase; therefore, the entire chromatographic 
flow path must be pressurized at a consistently constant value. 
Variations in pressure can lead to changes in mobile phase den-
sity that can in turn lead to undesirable changes in solute solu-
bility and interactions with the solid-phase support. Because 
the supercritical phase represents a state in which liquid and 
gas properties converge, SFC is sometimes called convergence 
chromatography [15].
The recognized advantages of SFC include, vide supra, 
high diffusivity/low viscosity both contributing to greater 
component resolution per unit time, the capability of incor-
porating longer packed columns (pcSFC) affording a greater 
number of theoretical plates (component resolving capability), 
lower operating temperatures relative to GC, reducing pos-
sible problems related to component thermal sensitivity, com-
ponent isolation via simple reductions in fluid pressure, and 
direct translation to scale-up parameters facilitating isolation 
of greater amounts of components of interest [4]. The major 
disadvantages of SFC include its general lack of applicabil-
ity to water-only soluble components, and other highly polar 
molecules like proteins, and the requirement for relatively 
heavy tanks to house the mobile phase prior to its conversion 
to a supercritical fluid [16]. However, employing modifiers, 
such as polar low-molecular-weight volatile compounds like 
methanol, in conjunction with a supercritical fluid like CO2 
has shown great promise in addressing this issue. The flex-
ibility of including relatively minor amounts of polar modi-
fiers in SFC separations broadens the applicability of SFC to 
include the most likely polar biologically derived analytes that 
will most likely be essential components of the vast majority 
of all toxicology mechanism studies, vide infra. Earlier SFC 
issues with mobile phase pressure fluctuations in the column 
and depressurization aerosol formation have been solved with 
more modern hardware.
When a potential new pharmaceutical moves toward final 
acceptance a number of studies involving both animals and 
humans must be completed before final approval is given. 
Most often these studies require quantitative assessment in a 
complex biological matrix of not only the pharmaceutical of 
interest but also any metabolites that may be produced during 
the trials so that any toxicological issues can be addressed and 
hypotheses for toxicology mechanisms developed. Historically 
the method of choice for these analyses of this type in bio-
logical samples has been HPLC-MS-MS [4]. However, recent 
advances in SFC and supercritical fluid extraction (SFE) have 
shown great promise as viable complementary alternatives to 
the HPLC-MS-MS approach [17, 18]. An evaluation of online 
solid-phase SFE and chromatography (SFC) coupled to mass 
spectrometry (MS) (SFE-SFC-MS) has been completed for its 
potential usefulness with respect to metabolic profiling and 
pharmacological investigations of ketamine in humans. One 
aim of this study was to develop the SFE-SFC-MS method for 
the quantification of ketamine and its metabolites in human 
urine excluding sample preparation employing liquid-liquid 
extraction (LLE). Several of the SFE-SFC-MS conditions 
were optimized systematically following the requirements of 
the European Medicines Agency. The method, requiring a rel-
atively small volume of human urine (20 ōL per sample), was 
validated for pharmacologically and toxicologically relevant 
concentrations ranging from 25 to 1,000 ng/mL (r2 > 0.995). 
The lower limit of quantification (LOQ) for all compounds 
was found to be as low as 0.5 ng. The stability of analytes dur-
ing the removal of water from the urine samples using dif-
ferent conditions was examined closely. Thus, based on the 
performance of this approach to studies of ketamine metabo-
lites in humans, similar general approaches could, therefore, 
possibly find acceptance for the more routine application of 
online SFE-SFC-MS in toxicological investigations.
While not directly linked to or focused on the develop-
ment of toxicological mechanisms, a new SFC method for 
the quantitative analysis of pharmaceuticals within a com-
plex biological matrix could have future applications [19]. An 
automatic online solid-phase dehydrate extraction (SPDE)-
ultra-high-performance supercritical fluid chromatography 
(UHPSFC)-MS/MS system has been recently described. The 
automated SPDE procedure was coupled to the UHPSFC so 
as to allow for the direct sample extract injection into the 
UHPSFC system. This new sample extraction method serves 
to overcome one of the disadvantages of SFC, that being its 
general incompatibility with the direct analysis of analytes 
in water-borne samples. The online SPDE-UHPSFC-MS/
MS system was applied to the screening of 45 prohibited

1261
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
substances in a human urine matrix. The SPDE sample prepa-
ration resulted in a significant reduction of the sample matrix 
effects (ME) resulting in large volume injections which in turn 
led to relatively high sensitivity with the limits of detection 
(LODs) ranging from ng L∞1 to ōg L∞1. These LOD values were 
shown to be noticeably lower than those currently attained 
with the LC-MS-MS approach due in most part to the sample 
preparation procedure that simplified the sample matrix. Thus, 
this new online SPDE-UHPSFC-MS/MS system opens up the 
possibility of SFC contributing to the qualitative and quanti-
tative analysis of complex aqueous-based biological samples.
Extending the capability of SFC to include not only pharma-
ceuticals but their dominant metabolites has appeared in a new 
method employing UHPSFC [20], vide supra. The UHPSFC 
refers to a technology growth for SFC and SFC hardware 
(pumps and flow controllers) in that column dimensions have 
been reduced significantly, 2.1 mm, and the solid support par-
ticle size is much smaller at less than 2 ōm. These reductions 
result in a notably improved resolution for analytes as had been 
previously documented for HPLC approaches [21]. Employing 
photodiode array (PDA) detection the method was able to 
quantify 15 sulfonamides and their N4-acetylation metabo-
lites in serum. The complete separation time for the pharma-
ceuticals and their metabolites took only 7 minutes with LOD 
ranging from 0.15 to 0.35 ōg/mL. Recoveries between 90.1% 
and 102.2% were obtained with satisfactory precision with 
relative standard deviation (RSD) values consistently below 
3%. The proposed method was simple, accurate, time-saving, 
and “green”, it was shown to be applicable to the qualitative 
and quantitative analysis of a variety of sulfonamides in serum 
samples. The “green” aspect of almost any SFC method refers 
to the absence of or nearly the absence of any organic solvent 
participation in the analytical process.
Contained within these SFC references, as well as refer-
ences on all of the other analytical methodologies discussed 
herein, is the somewhat overlooked critical step involving 
sample preparation. Online sample preparation for UHPLC 
and UHPSFC has helped significantly in this critical step in 
the overall analytical process. The online coupling of biologi-
cal sample preparation with UHPLC and UHPSFC has gained 
increasing attention owing to the increasing requirements 
of simplifying the complex sample matrix, analyte stability, 
extraction efficiency, overall analysis efficiency, and method 
sensitivity [22]. The main sample preparation techniques that 
have been coupled with UHPLC online are SPE and in‐tube 
SPME, while SPE [23] and SFE are the main techniques that 
have been coupled with UHPSFC online [17]. These online 
sample preparation techniques, while in their early stages of 
development, do indeed address the issues linked with sample 
preparation of complex biological matrices. Focus on advances 
in sample preparation techniques is the subject of its own sec-
tion within this chapter, vide infra.
Hyphenated techniques usually refer to a combination of 
separation techniques, such as GC (GC-GC) in conjunction 
with information-rich detectors [24, 25], like mass spectrom-
eters (MS) yielding GC-GC-MS as a method descriptor. Such 
an approach has been recently described wherein HPLC, SFC 
and a mass spectrometer (HPLC-SFC-MS) have been linked 
together in a new hyphenated technique, two-dimensional 
(2D)-HPLC-SFC-MS [26]. Very often the desirable pharma-
ceutical activity of a specific compound may rest with only one 
of its two or more optical, chiral, isomers (enantiomers). Thus, 
enantioselective analysis becomes an essential requirement dur-
ing the pharmaceutical development and evaluation of chiral 
drug molecules. In pre-clinical as well as clinical studies, the 
Food and Drug Administration (FDA) mandates the assessment 
of “in vivo” inter-conversion of chiral drugs to determine their 
physiological effects [26]. In vivo analysis of the active pharma-
ceutical ingredient (API) and any of its potential metabolites can 
be quite challenging most often due to their low abundance (ng/
mL) and complex biological matrix interferences. Therefore, 
quite often, highly selective and sensitive analytical techniques 
are compulsory for the separation of the API and its metabolites 
from the matrix components and one another. Furthermore, as 
often is the case for chiral (optically active) APIs, further ana-
lytical separation is required to resolve the API and its potential 
optically active metabolites from their corresponding enantio-
mers. Using optimized parameters (such as column dimensions, 
transfer volumes, trapping column stationary phase, system tub-
ing internal diameter (i.d.), and detection techniques), the first 
separation dimension, HPLC, used primarily as a desalting 
step, efficiently separated the API from its potential metabolites 
and matrix components. The API and its metabolites were then 
trapped/focused on small trapping columns and automatically 
transferred onto the second separation dimension, SFC, for 
chiral component resolution. Analyte detection was achieved 
by both UV and MS detection. The LOD was determined to 
be 10 ng/mL. An application was described, for a complex bio-
logical matrix, where a mouse hepatocyte-treated sample was 
successfully analyzed using the optimized 2D-HPLC-SFC-MS 
system for the API and its metabolite. This hyphenated tech-
nique could be possibly viewed as comprising a complex bio-
logical sample matrix clean-up step, the HPLC portion of the 
process, followed by separation of the simplified matrix by SFC 
with detection and quantitation by UV and MS.
To test the applicability of SFC to contribute to a metabolo-
mics platform for data analysis, a series of experiments using 
a SFC/high-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) was con-
ducted on a group of known metabolites [27, 28] spiked into 
biological matrices. The influences of biological matrices, such 
as plasma and urine, were determined and evaluated under 
the SFC/HRMS approach. A pre-selected set of 49 metabo-
lites representing an array of compound types were selected, 
among them, amino acids, nucleotides, carbohydrates, steroids, 
and lipids. The assessment of the ME, the impact of biological 
fluids on the quality of MS/MS spectra, and the robustness of 
the overall SFC/HRMS method were each taken into consid-
eration. As an example of the performance of the SFC/HRMS 
approach, an average RSD (%) values of retention times for the 
known spiked metabolites were equal or below 0.5%, in the two 
biological fluids over a period of 3 weeks. Likewise, the nature 
of matrix influences was clearly demonstrated to be metabolite 
specific with wide-ranging scopes of influence. Taken collec-
tively, all of the results confirmed that SFC/HRMS could well 
be considered a valid addition to the already established chro-
matographic techniques for metabolomic studies.
Synthetic cannabinoids (SCs) are a diverse group of 
compounds that bind to cannabinoid receptors and are

1262
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
designed to enhance the pharmacological potency of 
β 9-tetrahydrocannabinol (THC, the active component of 
cannabis) [29]. SCs are known to rapidly undergo biotrans-
formation into a large number of metabolites [29]. Thus, for 
systematic toxicology analysis of SCs, a fast and efficient ana-
lytical method applicable to biological fluids is an essential 
requirement. To address this issue, a simple and fast reliable 
SFC method with UV detection for the simultaneous analysis 
of natural cannabinoids and a wide group of SCs and their 
metabolites in human urine was developed [29]. The precise, 
selective, and robust analytical method offered improved 
analysis time, high separation efficiency, and low consump-
tion of organic solvents.
A fast SFC-MS method for the analysis of test mixtures of 
60 medium- and high-polarity compounds, designed for imple-
mentation in a high-throughput metabonomics setting has been 
described [30]. The test mixtures were separated on a total of 
12 different column chemistries at three different tempera-
tures, using CO2-methanol-based mobile phases. The results 
suggested that a new generation of stationary phases, specifi-
cally designed for improved robustness in mixed CO2-methanol 
mobile phases, can improve peak shape, peak capacity, and 
resolution for all classes of polar analytes contained within the 
test mixtures. Addition of polar additives such as ammonium 
salts (formate, acetate, and hydroxide) to the mobile phase sig-
nificantly enhanced chromatographic performance, also the use 
of water or alkylamine additives was found to be beneficial for 
specific subsets of polar analytes mixture. The utility of these 
findings was confirmed by the relatively rapid separation of a 
mixture of polar metabolites in human urine using an optimized 
7 minutes gradient SFC method, where the use of the recom-
mended column and co-solvent combination resulted in a sig-
nificant improvement in chromatographic performance.
A metabolomics perspective relative to supercritical fluid 
chromatography coupled with mass spectrometry (SFC/MS) 
outlined the strengths, weakness, and future potential of the 
SFC/MS technology [31]. Along with the aforementioned 
strengths of the SFC approach, in general, new strengths were 
discussed relative to applications of the technology to a wider 
range of polar metabolites, a common characteristic of bio-
logical fluid metabolites, while maintaining its core strength 
with relatively non-polar compounds. The capability of direct 
interface with automated SFE was also mentioned with exam-
ples related to determination of markers for oral and colorec-
tal cancers. The weaknesses relative to metabolomics were 
addressed in terms of future research needed in the area of 
new column stationary phases capable of intra- and inter-class 
diverse biological fluid component separations.
As has been illustrated above, pcSFC is a high-pressure nor-
mal LC-like method that generally operates slightly below the 
critical temperature of the mobile phase (usually CO2) with 
a very small amount of a polar modifier like low-molecular-
weight alcohols, esters, and ethers. When compared alongside 
traditional flash-based LC, pcSFC offers the following advan-
tages: better resolution, faster separation, faster sample recov-
ery, and a more cost-efficient chromatography [32]. These 
benefits are demonstrated by the pcSFC chromatograms of 
selected relatively high-molecular-weight thermally labile 
pharmaceutical-like amides shown in Figure 23.15A, B, and C. 
The addition of a very small amount of a low-molecular-weight 
base modifier to the mobile phase, isopropylamine (IPA), dem-
onstrates an added flexibility of pcSFC to moderately adjust 
component retention times and peak shapes, while maintain-
ing component elution order, thus potentially providing for 
enhances in multicomponent resolution. For the separations 
performed with no added IPA, eight of the nine components, 
contained within the three mixtures, have significantly unique 
retention times. The conditions for these separations have been 
reported [32]. Similar pcSFC separation performance charac-
teristics were observed when methanol, ethanol, acetonitrile, 
tetrahydrofuran, and ethylacetate were employed as polar low-
molecular-weight mobile phase modifiers. The use of these 
different modifiers did precipitate notable changes in compo-
nent elution order accompanied by no significant change in 
overall separation time, offering yet additional flexibilities to 
the pcSFC approach (Figure 23.15).
Thus, the applications discussed above clearly demonstrate 
that the characteristics demonstrated by pcSFC in the pres-
ence of small amounts of polar mobile phase modifiers can 
play a notable role in chromatographic separations performed 
in support of toxicology mechanism investigations. A detailed 
description of the theoretical aspects of CO2-modified SFC

and SFE has been published [4, 33]. A list of the commonly 
employed relatively polar SFC modifiers with accompanying 
dipole moments appears in Table 23.4.
Listed below in Table 23.5 are selected types of molecules 
and matrices that have been the subject of toxicological mech-
anism studies employing SFC.
In selected toxicological mechanism studies, it is very often 
the pure optical isomer of the compound(s) of interest that dis-
plays the desired pharmaceutical response and hence obtaining 
that pure enantiomer is essential to building an understanding 
of any possible modes of toxicology. Thus, purification of the 
desired enantiomers in sufficient quantities to permit studies 
of the enantiomer’s pharmacokinetic and metabolic prop-
erties can be an issue. The introduction of preparative scale 
pcSFC columns and ultra-fast SFC hardware around 2010 has 
helped address the issue of obtaining sufficient quantities of 
pharmaceutically active optical isomers [4]. For preparative 
scale chromatography the advantages of pcSFC over HPLC 
include increased flow rates, reduced column pressure drops, 
reduced component retention times, reduced organic solvent 
consumption, easier product isolation, and lower overall sepa-
ration costs. Estimates are that, within pharmaceutical compa-
nies, ~90% of separations directed at obtaining ~1 g of a chiral 
pharmaceutically active compounds are performed using prep 
SFC [4]. It is important to note also that minimal to no race-
mization of the pure optical isomer was observed during the 
SFC-based separations. With retention times of less than 3.5 
minutes, isolation of 24.5 g of an optically pure isomer was 
obtained via multiple injections taking ~2 hours [4, 35]. The 
use of prep scale pcSFC directed at compounds possessing no 
chiral activity has also been widely accepted [36].
23.4  IR and Raman Spectroscopy in Toxicology
(Dr. Francis L. Martin)
23.4.1  General Comments
The application of spectrochemical techniques for quantitative 
and/or qualitative analysis of bio-constituents is now common 
[1–3], making them readily applicable to address toxicologi-
cal questions. Spectrochemical techniques generate a chemical 
fingerprint of an analyzed sample in proportion to interac-
tions between an electromagnetic radiation wave at a specific 
frequency and chemical bonds within the sample. In this phe-
nomenon, the electrons that constitute the atoms and molecules 
in the sample absorb photons with a specific light frequency, 
hence, being excited to higher energy states. This process is 
called excitation. Then, the electrons in a higher energy state 
release the total or part of the absorbed radiation returning to a 
lower energy state. This process is called emission. These exci-
tation and emission processes can occur at different light fre-
quencies, which determine the type of spectroscopy technique. 
The electromagnetic spectrum comprises a series of frequen-
cies, from long radio waves (3 Hz) down to gamma rays (>30 
Ehz). Figure 23.16 shows the different types of electromagnetic 
radiation signals according to their frequency.
The light frequency is proportional to the photon energy 
according to the Planck-Einstein relation [4]:

(6.626 × 10∞34 Jłs), and ν is the light frequency. The latter, ν, 
can be replaced by wavelength via the relationship between 
wavelength (λ), frequency (ν), and the speed of light in a vac-
uum (c, 2.998 × 108 m/s):

Thus, photon energy is inversely proportional to wavelength. 
Vibrational spectroscopy is an umbrella term for techniques 
that excite molecules to absorb or emit energy in the IR wave-
length region between ~700 nm and 1 mm (frequency = 430 THz 
to 300 GHz, energy = 1.7 eV to 1.24 meV) [5]. The spectrochemi-
cal signal is generated because all molecular bonds vibrate at 
any temperature above absolute zero (T > 0 K). Therefore, when a 
molecular bond is exposed to IR radiation with a frequency equal 
to its fundamental vibration frequency, it absorbs this radiation. 
IR radiation does not generate electronic transitions, only vibra-
tional or rotational transitions. Herein, the electron is not excited 
to higher electronic energy levels but to higher vibrational energy 
levels within the same electronic energy level (Figure 23.17).
Each electronic energy level is composed of several vibra-
tional energy levels that are composed of several rotational 
energy levels, which are in turn composed of several trans-
lational energy levels. Electronic transitions are of higher 
energy frequency and underpin techniques such as UV-Vis, 
while vibrational, rotational, and translational transitions are 
of lower energy and associated with the modalities of IR and 
Raman spectroscopy (vibrational transitions) or microwave 
spectroscopy (rotational transitions). Translational transitions 
are of so small an energy gap that any source of energy will 
generate this transition, hence inducing molecules to move.
There are two types of molecular vibration movements 
affected by IR radiation: stretching (axial deformation) and 
FIGURE 23.16  The electromagnetic spectrum by frequency and wavelength regions.
FIGURE 23.17  Energy diagram showing electronic, vibrational, rotational, and translational transitions. E stands for total energy.

1265
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
bending (angular deformation). Stretching vibrations can be 
either symmetric or asymmetric, while bending vibrations can 
be in-plane (symmetric or asymmetric) or out-of-plane (sym-
metric or asymmetric) (Figure 23.18) [5, 6].
A molecule containing N atoms has 3N degrees of freedom, 
i.e., every atom in the molecule has three coordinates (x-, y-, 
and z-coordinates) in a tri-dimensional space. Additionally, 
each molecule has the potential for three movement charac-
teristics: (1) translation: motion of the entire molecule through 
space; (2) rotation: rotational motion of the entire molecule 
around its center of gravity; and, (3) vibration: motion of each 
of its atoms relative to the other atoms. In vibrational spectros-
copy, vibration movements alone are determined. Thus, trans-
lation and rotation movements are subtracted from the total 
number of degrees of freedom (3N). Therefore, the number 
of vibrational degrees of freedom for a polyatomic non-linear 
molecule equals 3N ′  6, since there are three translation move-
ments (along the x-, y- or z-axes) and three rotation movements 
(around the x-, y- or z-axes) for any non-linear molecule. For 
linear molecules, because the rotation along the bond axis is 
invalid, the number of vibrational degrees of freedom equals 
3N ′  5. Therefore, by knowing the number of atoms in a mol-
ecule, it is possible to estimate how many vibrational signals 
will be shown (3N ′  6 for non-linear molecules, 3N ′  5 for lin-
ear molecules) [5].
23.4.2  Description of IR Spectroscopies
The IR region is commonly divided into three sub-regions: 
near-infrared (NIR) region, 0.78–3 ōm; mid-infrared (MIR) 
region, 3–50 ōm; and, far-infrared (FIR) region, 50–1,000 ōm. 
To express a detectable IR signal, a molecule must exhibit 
variation in its resultant dipole moment different from zero 
(ōR ∗ 0); therefore, homonuclear species such as O2, N2, Cl2, 
and I2 do not absorb IR radiation.
An interaction between IR radiation and molecular bonds 
occurs because any covalent bond by vibrating creates an 
electromagnetic field proportional to the variation in dipole 
moment. When an IR radiation wave reaches this electro-
magnetic field generated with the same oscillating fre-
quency, the radiation is absorbed (constructive interfering). 
A bond vibration can be approximated employing a mechan-
ical model composed by two masses connected by a spring 
(harmonic oscillator model). Thus, a diatomic molecule can 
be compared to an ideal harmonic oscillator, defined by 
Hooke’s law [5]:
	
F

where F is the force acting against the displacement y from its 
equilibrium position.
The proportionality constant k is termed the force con-
stant. In an equilibrium position, the potential energy is zero. 
Therefore, when the spring is compressed or stretched, the 
potential energy, E, will vary according to the work needed 
to move the mass:

FIGURE 23.18  Type of molecular vibrations. + refers to vibrations 
above the flat plane; – indicates motion below the flat plane.
FIGURE 23.19  Harmonic (continuous line) and anharmonic (dashed 
line) oscillators model for IR spectroscopy, where E represents the poten-
tial energy, v the vibrational energy level, and y the bond stretching 
distance.

1266
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
The potential energy curve of an idealistic simple harmonic 
oscillator is a parable (dashed line; Figure 23.19). However, 
real-world molecules do not adhere to this idealistic model. 
Therefore, a more accurate potential energy curve for diatomic 
molecules resembles an anharmonic oscillator (continuous 
line; Figure 23.19). Each energy level in these oscillator curves 
represents a vibrational level of a molecule wherein electrons 
are excited. These vibrational levels are within the electronic 
level (Figure 23.17). For NIR, the vibrational transitions occur 
in regions of high energy, where the absorptions are called 
overtones or combination bands. This generates a very super-
posed signal of chemical features in comparison with MIR 
(fundamental vibration modes), though it can be used to assess 
relevant chemical information such as concentration. In con-
trast, FIR works at low frequencies (<650 cm∞1, >15 ōm), being 
particularly applicable to studies involving investigations of 
bonds containing metallic atoms.
MIR spectroscopy has traditionally been the primary tech-
nique employed for analyzing biological materials [1], since 
its output contains fundamental vibration modes which are far 
less convoluted in comparison with NIR and it covers most of 
the covalent bonds undetected by FIR. The main fundamen-
tal vibrational mode absorptions of functional groups within 
the MIR range are shown in Figure 23.20 [7]. Most stretching 
vibrations occur in the higher wavenumber region, since these 
require more energy; bending vibrations tend to occur in the 
lower wavenumber region. These signals shift toward higher 
or lower wavenumbers according to molecular configurations 
and neighboring functional groups.
Experimental analyses using MIR spectroscopy are carried 
out using instruments composed basically of five component 
parts: (1) light source; (2) spectrograph; (3) sampling area; (4) 
detector; and (5) computer module (Figure 23.21a). The light 
source is responsible for generating the electromagnetic radi-
ation in the IR range. A spectrograph is an optical apparatus 
containing an interferometer plus diffraction grating or prism 
for diffraction of the incident IR light. The sampling area is 
the holder to contain the sample to be irradiated with IR light. 
A detector captures the diffracted IR light generating an electric 
potential response. Finally, the computer module processes the 
generated electric information transforming it into an interfero-
gram followed by an FT, converting the signal into a spectrum. 
A MIR spectrometer can work in one of two modes: transmis-
sion or reflection. In transmission mode (Figure 23.21b), the IR 
light passes through a sample and reaches the detector, so the 
final recorded IR signal is given by:

length of the optical path, and c is the concentration.
In reflection mode, the IR light reaches the material sample 
surface and bounces within the material via a phenomenon 
called reflectance, where the incident light after a certain 
degree of depth penetration reflects back to the spectrometer 
at an angle close to 180°. A common technique exploiting 
reflection is the attenuated total reflection (ATR) mode (Figure 
23.21c). Herein, a crystal, often diamond, is placed between 
the light source and sample to generate an evanescent wave 
that amplifies the signal intensity [1]. A primary disadvantage 
of the transmission mode is that it is suitable only to analyze 
liquid in relatively large volumes (>2 mL) or thin materials. 
Reflectance by means of the ATR technique is more applicable 
to the analysis of thicker solid materials or smaller liquid vol-
umes. The penetration depth of ATR using a diamond crystal 
varies according to the incident radiation frequency and mate-
rial properties, but it usually varies between 0.5 and 2 ōm.
From an economic perspective, the use of MIR spectroscopy, 
in particular ATR-FTIR spectroscopy, has many advantages. 
The instrumentation is relatively low cost, requires low-cost 
maintenance, does not require laborious or wet-chemistry 
sample preparation procedures, has a fast data acquisition, 
and is non-destructive, i.e., the sample can be re-used post-
analysis. Thus, MIR applications in biology or toxicology are 
gaining importance as substitute or auxiliary methods to more 
conventional methods that usually employ electrochemical, 
chromatographic, mass spectrometric, or thermogravimetric 
techniques. 
23.4.3  Underpinning Principles of 
Raman Spectroscopy
Raman spectroscopy is based on an anomalous scattering 
effect that occurs with <1% of sample-absorbed photons. More 
than 99% of photons absorbed by a substance encounter elas-
tic scattering (e.g., Rayleigh scattering), which leaves the state 
of a material unchanged. This is because such photons are 
absorbed, increasing the vibrational energy state of molecules, 
and then emitted from the excited vibrational energy state to 
their initial vibrational energy state. However, a small percent-
age of absorbed photons undergo an inelastic process, whereby 
a molecule does not return to the initial vibrational energy 
state, emitting photons with a specific frequency of deviation 
to maintain the equilibrium of the system [5]. Inelastic scat-
tering can be Stokes or anti-Stokes scattering, which occur 
in 1 in 10 million absorbed photons. The Stokes scattering 
occurs when a molecule absorbs part of the energy of the 
incoming monochromatic wavelength (e.g., laser) and emits a 
wavelength of less energy than the wavelength absorbed. Anti-
Stokes scattering happens when the molecule emits a wave-
length of greater energy than the absorbed wavelength (Figure 
23.22). The latter occurs under certain circumstances whereby 
a molecule is in a partially excited energy state before absorb-
ing electromagnetic radiation.
The instrument filters the elastic scattering so that only 
inelastic scattering is detected and used to generate a Raman 
spectrum. Both Stokes and anti-Stokes phenomena occur. 
However, at room temperature, there is a lower population of 
molecules in an initial excited energy state to absorb radiation. 
Therefore, the anti-Stokes signal is weaker than the Stokes 
signal [5]. Consequently, many spectrometers only work with 
Stokes scattering. In contrast to IR spectroscopy, Raman spec-
troscopy is based on a change of polarizability rather than the 
dipole moment, which makes the vibration bands in Raman 
spectra quite different from those in IR spectra. The main 
vibration modes for Raman are depicted in Figure 23.23 [8].
FIGURE 23.21  (A) FTIR components spectrometer diagram, where the spectrograph is represented by a Michelson interferometer: (1) beam splitter, 
(2) fixed mirror, and (3) moving mirror. (B) transmission mode schematic. (C) ATR mode schematic.

1268
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
The main advantage of Raman spectroscopy in comparison 
with IR spectroscopy is that the former is water transparent 
<3,000 cm∞1, since it has a weak signal >3,000 cm∞1 [5]. This 
is an important consideration for biological applications since 
liquid or fresh media are generally composed primarily of 
water, which is a problem for IR spectroscopy. IR analysis is 
often performed on dry samples to minimize the confounding 
factor of a water-interfering signal. The Raman spectrometer 
is composed of four main parts: (1) a monochromatic laser 
source; (2) a spectrograph grating; (3) a charge-coupled device 
(CCD) detector; and (4) a computer module. The monochromatic 
laser light, often in the VIS or NIR range, is used to excite the 
sample to virtual energy states where the inelastic scattering 
phenomena occur. The best compromise for biological samples 
is the NIR 785 nm laser; this does not burn material like the more 
powerful VIS region lasers (e.g., 532 nm) and is less influenced 
by fluorescence while also giving a good signal-to-noise (SNR) 
ratio. Most Raman spectrographs have a fixed diffraction grating 
instead of interferometers with moving mirrors as employed in 
FTIR spectrometers. Hence, the detector is a CCD camera detec-
tor that records the whole spectrum at once, negating the need for 
Fourier transformation. This fixed diffraction grating and CCD 
detector set-up also make the technique substantially faster than 
FTIR spectroscopy for spectral acquisition in small wavenumber 
windows. A computer module converts the CCD detector signal 
into a spectrum (Figure 23.24). 
FIGURE 23.22  Energy diagram showing the IR absorption, Rayleigh scattering, Stokes and Anti-Stokes scattering in the vibrational energy states. 
E stands for total energy.
FIGURE 23.23  Approximation of the main vibration modes for Raman (Raman shift: 3,600–400 cm∞1). Sym.: symmetric; asym.: asymmetric.

Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
23.4.4  Emerging Field of Biospectroscopy
The development of biomarker extraction assays toward 
screening and/or diagnosis of diseases such as neurodegenera-
tive disease or cancer or for risk assessment in toxicology is 
a worldwide objective. The detection and characterization of 
these complex diseases to tailor treatment usually require labo-
rious and extremely invasive procedures, in addition to costs 
associated with sample preparation and complex machinery. 
Likewise, novel approaches that can detect sub-lethal effects 
that may result in toxicological consequences are required. 
For a number of decades now, toxicological endpoints have 
consistently relied on endpoints that are induced after high-
dose exposures of treatments. Additionally, determining 
long-term effects such as carcinogenesis in vivo also relies on 
high exposures often not relevant to real-world scenarios and 
these experiments are time consuming, adding significant cost 
implications.
Biospectroscopy essentially encompasses vibrational spec-
troscopy techniques harnessed to computational algorithms to 
analyze biological materials [9]. The identification of chemical 
patterns within biological samples using their spectrochemical 
information has fundamental importance to overcome issues 
in the investigation of fundamental biological mechanisms 
or disease diagnosis and is an emerging area of technologi-
cal innovation. Biospectroscopy allows fast, reagent-free, non-
destructive, and low-cost analysis of biological materials and 
has been applied to a wide range of applications, including can-
cer detection based on liquid biopsies or tissue analysis [9, 10], 
toxicology assays [11], environmental studies [12], taxonomic 
identification [13], and detection of cellular mechanisms [14]. 
Based on profiling extracted variance distinguishing different 
categories (e.g., control vs. treatment), different insights can be 
derived based on the computational algorithm used to address 
potential toxicological effects. For instance, classification 
algorithms can address whether a particular treatment causes 
alterations in target cells compared to control populations; this 
is commonly visualized in a scores plot. From this, altered 
wavenumbers contributing to classification may generate spec-
tral biomarkers in a weighted fashion plotted on a loadings 
plot. In other contexts, forward feature extraction algorithms 
may allow one to predict early if a particular endpoint may 
become relevant, e.g., cancer. Recognizing a dichotomous 
endpoint of effect early in an in vivo experiment would have 
enormous reduction and refinement implications and poten-
tially significantly reduce cost.
Biological samples have a specific spectrochemical signa-
ture within a region called the bio-fingerprint region, which 
ranges from 1,800 to 900 cm∞1 for IR spectroscopy and 
2,000–500 cm∞1 for Raman spectroscopy [15]. This bio-fin-
gerprint region comprises key absorptions of lipids (νs(C=O), 
δs(CH2)), proteins (Amide I, Amide II, νs(COO∞)), nucleic 
acids (νasPO2
∞, νsPO2
∞), and carbohydrates (νsCO–O–C) [1]. 
The main spectrochemical signatures for IR spectroscopy and 
Raman spectroscopy (Figure 23.25) consist of spectral mark-
ers (or biomarkers) within the bio-fingerprint region.
FIGURE 23.24  Diagram showing the Raman spectrometer components: (1) beam splitter; (2) objective lenses; and (3) lens.
FIGURE 23.25  Biochemical-cell fingerprint of (A) IR and (B) Raman 
spectra with tentative peak assignments. (Reprinted (adapted) with per-
mission from Ref: 15. Copyright 2011 American Chemical Society).

1270
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Several types of clinical samples, including biofluids [cere-
brospinal fluid (CSF), saliva, blood, or urine], cytology, or tis-
sue, can be readily analyzed using either IR spectroscopy or 
Raman spectroscopy to facilitate disease screening and diag-
nosis using this bio-fingerprint region, since the spectral data 
experimentally acquired can be used as input information for 
category classification models using computational tools, or 
chemometrics (Figure 23.26) [16]. 
23.4.4.1  Routine Sample Preparation
Sample preparation for biospectroscopy is minimal, since both 
IR spectroscopy and Raman spectroscopy are typically reagent-
free and non-destructive techniques. From blood, plasma or 
serum is generally used for spectral acquisition. Small ali-
quots of these biofluids (10–250 ōL) are usually pipetted onto 
low-emission (low-E) or aluminum-covered glass slides and 
allowed to dry overnight at room temperature [1, 17]. A similar 
procedure can be performed for urine, whereby centrifugation 
is performed to precipitate solid material and the supernatant 
is analyzed [18]. Saliva is measured post-drying, while CSF 
fluid is analyzed diluted in an ethanol 70% (vol/vol) solution to 
avoid bacterial proliferation and contamination. Samples can 
be measured in liquid state using Raman since this technique 
is “water transparent”. However, they must be properly dried 
and stored in desiccators prior to FTIR measurements using 
ATR-FTIR spectroscopy.
Tissues can be analyzed fresh, snap-frozen, formalin-
fixed paraffin-embedded (FFPE), or FFPE after de-waxing. 
Cytology can be analyzed fresh, snap-frozen, or collected 
into a preservative medium; for the latter, a number of washes 
will be required to remove contaminating spectral signature 
from the preservative medium. Ideally, fresh tissue should be 
used as it provides the most unadulterated spectrochemical 
information. However, this type of sample quickly undergoes 
autolysis or decomposition unless specific measures are taken. 
Hence, most applications involve snap-frozen or FFPE tis-
sue samples. Snap-frozen samples are preferably analyzed by 
Raman spectroscopy due to water interference in FTIR spec-
troscopy [1, 2], while FFPE tissue can be analyzed either using 
Raman or FTIR spectrometers. De-waxing can be performed 
prior to analysis in order to remove interfering paraffin signals 
[1]. However, there are also computational ways to minimize 
the influence of the paraffin signals in the spectrum [19], thus 
FFPE tissue can also be measured directly.
FIGURE 23.26  Diagram showing a variety of clinical samples (CSF, saliva, blood, urine) that can be used for disease screening and diagnosis using 
their bio-fingerprint spectrochemical signature. (Reprinted with permission from Ref: 16. Copyright 2014 John Wiley & Sons, Inc.).

Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
23.4.4.2  Generation of Large Datasets Harnessed 
to Chemometrics Techniques
Advances in spectrometer instrumentation allow for ready 
generation in an almost automated fashion of large amounts 
of complex spectral data from biological samples. By its very 
nature even within a single sample type, such spectral infor-
mation is highly heterogeneous. In looking for differences 
between different categories (e.g., control vs. treated, control 
cell vs. cancer cell), such heterogeneity will give rise to vari-
ance that will vastly outweigh the small, subtle distinguishing 
features that are the diagnostic markers being sought. As a 
consequence, the mathematical (chemometric) techniques to 
process the experimental data generated and extract relevant 
information are as important as the analytical instrumenta-
tion used to acquire them. Only in an information-extrac-
tion process is a clinical diagnosis possible to be achieved. 
Chemometrics is defined as “the science of relating measure-
ments made on a chemical system or process to the state of the 
system via application of mathematical or statistical methods” 
[20]. It is the tool that can be applied to convert complex spec-
trochemical data into meaningful information, thus enabling 
one to draw relevant classification and mechanistic informa-
tion from experimental data. Chemometric techniques also 
must develop alongside instrumentation and data complexity.
Chemometrics includes multivariate calibration and clas-
sification techniques and should be used where the spectral 
signatures exhibit overlapping bands, unknown sources of 
variation or interference, poor SNR, non-linearity in absolute 
wavelengths, or other adverse effects in the reactional medium 
[21]. Multivariate calibration techniques are employed when 
there are several discrete values as reference labels among the 
samples distributed in an order of importance (e.g., 1, 2, 3, 
…). These are mainly used to estimate chemical concentra-
tion values in quantitative analyses based on the spectral data. 
For qualitative analysis, where there are few category labels 
among the samples and the aim is to distinguish or classify 
them, multivariate classification techniques are employed.
Multivariate classification is divided into unsupervised and 
supervised methods [22]. Unsupervised methods are used 
mainly for exploratory analysis in order to identify natural 
patterns or clustering that arise from the data. Hence, no pre-
defined labels are inputted in the model. The most common 
unsupervised method for exploratory analysis of spectral data 
is principal component analysis (PCA) [23]. PCA decom-
poses the spectral data into a few principal components (PCs) 
responsible for most of the original data variance. Each PC is 
orthogonal to each other and they are distributed in a decreas-
ing order of explained variance, so the 1st PC accounts for 
the highest explained variance, followed by the 2nd PC and 
so on. Each PC is composed of scores (projections of the 
samples on the PC direction) and loadings (angle cosines of 
the wavenumbers projected on the PC direction) [3]. The PCA 
scores highlight the variance on sample direction, thus dem-
onstrating clustering and trend patterns. This is used to assess 
similarities/dissimilarities among the samples. PCA loadings 
exhibit the variance on wavenumber direction, thus being used 
to identify important wavenumbers or spectral markers [23] 
(Figure 23.27).
For instance, the spectral features that distinguish the 
red from the blue class (Figure 23.27) are characterized 
by an increase at wavenumber 1,590 cm∞1 (+ loadings on 
PC1, + scores on PC1) and a decrease at wavenumbers 
1,370 cm∞1 and 1,100 cm∞1 (′  loadings on PC1, ′  scores on 
PC1). However, although PCA is a very useful technique for 
exploratory analysis of a dataset, it is not a proper classifica-
tion technique, since even though PCA gives an indication 
of the sample nature, it is unable to predict the category of a 
given blind sample. For this reason, supervised classification 
techniques are employed in the PCA scores, selected wave-
number features, or in the whole original spectrum region in 
order to obtain diagnostic results.
The most common supervised classification techniques are 
K-nearest neighbor (KNN), artificial neural networks (ANN), 
linear discriminant analysis (LDA), quadratic discriminant 
analysis (QDA), and support vector machines (SVM). KNN 
classifies the samples based on the distance from each of the 
samples in the training set using the K-nearest samples, so 
the classification of an unknown sample is based on the con-
cept of majority vote. That is, the sample is classified to the 
group that has the most members of training samples among 
its neighbors [24]. ANN is a more sophisticated method based 
on neuron interconnections, being inspired by how the human 
brain works. ANNs are based on a series of nodes that con-
nects to each other in different depth layers. The contributions 
for all nodes are multiplied by constants and added before a 
non-linear transformation within the node, which are often a 
Gaussian transformation function that extracts features from 
the dataset [24].
LDA is one of the commonest methods used in super-
vised classification of spectral data. It calculates the dis-
criminant function (DF) between two classes according to the 
Mahalanobis distance between the samples. QDA works simi-
larly; however, it is less used than LDA probably due to the 
relative lack of available QDA algorithms. The main differ-
ence between LDA and QDA is that the former uses a pooled 
covariance matrix for sample distance calculation, while QDA 
uses the individual variance-covariance matrix of each class 
for this calculation [25, 26]. The LDA and QDA classification 
scores for sample I of class k are calculated as follows [27]:
