SVM is defined as a method of supervised classification in 
which decision boundaries (hyperplanes) are determined that 
maximize the separation of data in different classes [20]. In 
other words, SVMs are binary classifiers that work by finding 
a classification hyperplane in a high-dimensional space, which 
separates two classes of samples providing the largest margin 
of separation [28]. It is a linear technique by nature; however, 
it uses a non-linear step called the kernel transformation [29]. 
The SVM kernel function is responsible for transforming the 
data into a different feature space, changing its classification 
ability [25]. This adds to the power of SVM compared to other 
discriminant algorithms such as LDA or QDA. LDA or QDA 
DFs, as well as SVM discrimination, are shown in Figure 23.28.
LDA is less susceptible to over-fitting and usually shows 
good classification ability when classes have similar variance 
structures, i.e., when they have similar distributions, and also 
performs well even with small datasets. QDA performs better 
than LDA when classes have different variance structures/distri-
butions but underperforms compared to LDA in small datasets 
[26]. SVM, although highly susceptible to over-fitting and more 
time consuming, generally performs better than LDA or QDA.
The input classification variables for LDA, QDA, or SVM can 
be PCA scores in PCA-LDA, PCA-QDA, or PCA-SVM algo-
rithms or specific wavenumbers selected by variable selection 
algorithms such as the successive projections algorithm (SPA) 
[30] or genetic algorithm (GA) [31]. SVM can also work with the 
full spectral region. LDA and QDA require a number of variables 
equal or smaller than the number of samples in the training set.
23.4.5  Spectrochemical Imaging in Biology
Although useful in many ways, conventional spectroscopy tech-
niques lack one fundamental aspect in the analysis of complex 
heterogeneous samples: spatial information. Spectrochemical 
techniques using single-spectrum acquisition rely exclusively 
on positional measures, without giving information on spatial 
distribution. This majorly excludes its usability in applications 
involving structural investigations of constituents distributed 
over tissue segments. To overcome such limitations and derive 
both spatial and spectral information in combination, hyper-
spectral imaging techniques can be employed.
Hyperspectral computational algorithms combine imag-
ing and spectroscopy. Herein, an image is generated for each 
sample segment being analyzed. In this, for each image posi-
tion (pixel) across a sample, a spectrum is also generated, thus 
generating a three-dimensional (3D) object for each location 
analyzed. This is referred to as a hyperspectral “data-cube” 
(Figure 23.29). This enhances the data acquisition by provid-
ing access to spatial information along the x- and y-axes, and 
chemical information along the z-axis. 
23.4.5.1  Hyperspectral Imaging Techniques 
in Biology Applications
Hyperspectral imaging techniques vary according to the fre-
quency of the electromagnetic region employed to irradiate 
a sample, and most spectroscopy techniques can be adapted 
for imaging. IR (MIR or NIR), UV or VIS, and fluorescence 
or Raman spectroscopies are the most applicable for hyper-
spectral imaging. MIR or Raman hyperspectral imaging 
approaches can be implemented by coupling a microscope and 
an automated sample platform to a conventional spectrometer. 
In such a set-up, the IR (for FTIR) or laser (for Raman) light 
beam is focused via an objective lens onto a small area of a 
sample being analyzed. Then, one of three data acquisition 
configurations may be used: point scan, line scan, or area scan 
[32]. Although generating greater spectral information, point 
scans require long image-map acquisition times, therefore 
making this approach unsuitable for most real-time applica-
tions. In contrast, area scans enable the acquisition at discrete 
wavelengths of whole images rapidly over a wide area but are 
incapable of determining in-depth detail in samples. The opti-
mum acquisition mode is often a line scan, as this approach 
combines fast image acquisition with detailed spectral infor-
mation obtained via a 2D array detector.
For data acquisition, the set-up varies according to the 
instrumental technique employed. Changing parameters 
including distance from the sample to the detector and light 
source power may affect the result. Hyperspectral images can 
be derived in reflectance, transmittance, fluorescence, or scat-
tering modes. Prior to measurement, an instrument is usually 
calibrated using white or dark references to account for the 
uneven intensity of the light source, the spectral response of 
FIGURE 23.29  Hyperspectral “data-cube”, where the spatial informa-
tion is shown on the x- and y-axis, and the spectral information on the 
z-axis. (CC BY-NC 2.0, https://imgbin.com/png/6s6tUKPJ/hyperspec-
tral-imaging-data-cube-photon-etc-market-analysis-multispectral-png).

1274
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
the device, and the dark radiation present in the measurement 
chamber that may influence the imaging detector. However, 
this calibration procedure fails to take into consideration mor-
phological information of the sample to be analyzed. Imaging 
a flat sample is straightforward and is unhampered by mor-
phological changes. However, non-flat samples (e.g., round-
shaped or cylindrical-shaped) as a consequence of the fact that 
their surface curvature creates a gradient distance variation 
between sample and detector, resulting in differences in the 
optical path length, give rise to distortions in imaging. These 
need to be corrected for by additional pre-treatments prior to 
analysis [32].
23.4.5.2  Data Analysis in Hyperspectral Images
Frequently, feature extraction techniques are employed to 
reduce and compress hyperspectral images, since the type 
of data generated here usually requires extensive amounts 
of storage space and computational processing power. To 
speed up such computational analysis, spatial- and chem-
ical-relevant features are extracted from images via multi-
variate-based techniques. This is possible because although 
the hyperspectral data contains millions of data points, only 
a small portion of them contain the information relevant to 
the property being explored. This is the most critical aspect 
regarding processing hyperspectral images. The overall sen-
sitivity and robustness of the machine vision system depend 
significantly on the responsiveness of the features containing 
the desired information for analysis. Initially, one needs to 
decide which type of features to extract: spectral features, 
textural features, or some combination thereof. The primary 
detail is to determine whether the desired information is 
proportional to the number of pixels or spectral signatures, 
regardless of how they are distributed in the hyperspectral 
image or if the desired information is related to the spatial 
distribution of pixels [33].
The simplest means of feature selection is to select wave-
lengths at peaks or troughs of a spectral curve based on 
maximum or minimum intensity differences [32]. However, 
some relevant wavelengths may be overlooked applying this 
approach because of the multivariate nature of the data. 
Therefore, it might be required to use multivariate image 
analysis (MIA). MIA was developed in the late 1980s to pro-
cess images that encompass more than one measurement per 
pixel. The method can perform at either a large-scale (wide 
area) or down to a pixel level. The large-scale level focuses on 
analyzing each hyperspectral image as a “sample”, by incor-
porating all the information contained in the image to derive 
an analytical parameter. The pixel-level approach focuses on 
analyzing individual pixel spectra within each hyperspectral 
image [34].
MIA is highly efficient at extracting spectral features. It 
achieves this by initially unfolding the hyperspectral data-cube 
into a 2D data matrix. Because of this, the spectral feature 
for each pixel refers to a row. Then, a chosen data reduction 
technique is employed. PCA and multivariate curve resolution 
alternating least squares (MCR-ALS) are the most commonly 
used techniques. MCR-ALS decomposes the unfolded data X 
into a bilinear model as follows [34]:
	
X
CS
E
T
=
+

(23.45)
MCR results generate concentration distribution maps C, with 
the pure spectra of image constituents S and the residuals E. 
This decomposition separates the information regarding dif-
ferent constituents in an image to find its purest information or 
to detect pixels with selective information. It can be applied to 
one or more images in combination, in instances where multi-
image analysis is used. Here, a multilayer image from a single 
sample or a series of images with related chemical composi-
tion can be explored [34].
Other methodologies that focus on finding “purest” compo-
nents via such an exploration approach include algorithms such 
as SIMPLISMA (SIMPLe-to-use Interactive Self-modeling 
Mixture Analysis) and fixed-size image window-evolving fac-
tor analysis. The purest pixels provide an approximation of 
the pure spectra being searched for, while the purest spectral 
channels, when unfolded, allow construction of approximate 
distribution maps of the purest constituents [34]. Additional 
algorithms for feature selection in hyperspectral images are 
minimum redundancy maximum relevance, genetic algorithm 
(GA), successive projections algorithm (SPA), and neural net-
work approaches that extract the weight of each input in the 
back-propagation [32]. Thereafter, the spectral and/or spatial 
features extracted from the images are used as input variables 
for classification algorithms.
A significant consideration with hyperspectral imaging is the 
analysis time required for experimental data. Hyperspectral 
image datasets can readily take up gigabytes of space and 
depending on the number or size of the images, data analytics 
such as PCA may take days or weeks to process using standard 
desktop computers. Supercomputing is often used, but this 
adds a significant cost. There is an increasing focus on build-
ing up computer clusters of systems that can work indepen-
dently in tandem. In addition, there is also a drive to develop 
faster, accurate chemometrics methods to analyze hyperspec-
tral data-cubes in biological applications, allowing usage with 
lower-capacity computers.
23.4.6  Current Challenges in Biospectroscopy
Biospectroscopy remains an emerging field, where the pri-
mary challenge currently is its translation to real-world appli-
cations. Prior to proceeding to translation, there are a series of 
regulatory and validation hurdles to be surmounted. These are 
almost exclusively related to the analysis of the spectral data, 
since protocols for sample preparation and experimental mea-
surements are already well established [1, 2, 35].
23.4.6.1  Sample and Data Complexity
Biological samples are inherently highly complex by nature, 
with a number of chemical constituents, hence spectral features 
from very different biomolecules exhibiting marked overlap. 
Therefore, only multivariate analysis techniques incorporat-
ing the interrogation of multiple wavenumbers at one time 
are capable of providing discrimination in such a complex

1275
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
environment. Traditionally, a pre-defined spectral dataset is 
used for model construction, i.e., to train the model, while an 
external test set is used to validate the model toward unknown 
samples. However, there remains a lack of clarity on how to go 
about selecting samples for training or test sets. Manual selec-
tion of sample spectra runs a high risk of introducing bias, thus 
random selection is a preferred alternative. However, random 
selection is fraught with risk of extrapolation, i.e., the classi-
fication model lacks the required category variation to allow 
it to accurately predict further external and unidentified sam-
ples. A strategy often used in analytical applications to bypass 
this problem is the Kennard-Stone (KS) algorithm [36]. The 
KS algorithm systematically selects samples for the training 
set based on maximizing the training space. However, it does 
not take into account random or extreme behaviors that often 
happen in biological contexts. Therefore, there remains a need 
for algorithms to optimally select samples for training and test 
sets in biospectroscopy applications.
23.4.6.2  Uncertainty in Diagnostic Accuracy
Because of the intrinsic multi-dimensionality of IR or Raman 
spectra, the challenge of distinguishing and identifying trends 
within typical complexity of the spectral dataset can be dif-
ficult. In addition, over-fitting can be a major problem in pre-
dictive computational models such as SVM. Frequently, the 
model is well-trained and internally validated with a good 
accuracy, but the model is not robust enough to predict real-
world external samples where subtle sources of variation or 
random noise occur. Over time, these models need to be con-
tinually validated with further external samples, especially as 
the requirements for the model grow, i.e., increasing numbers 
of samples being interpreted. For this reason, mathematical 
tools to predict uncertainty in model diagnostic in order to 
foresee future performance are needed.
In addition, overall systems (spectrochemical analyzer and 
computational algorithm) for different analytical endpoints 
need to be regularly standardized to allow for instrument 
slippage, inter-user variance, inter-site differences, and inter-
instrument alterations. In many respects the analyzer at the 
front end generating the spectrum is a small portion of the 
biospectroscopy approach and the major component is the 
mathematical challenges at the backend required to maintain 
the data architecture, validity, and robustness for classifica-
tion and predictive analyses. Measurements made in different 
centers, even using the same sample preparation, instrument 
manufacturer, and measurement settings, are different. All 
this is because there are underlying variations in environ-
ment, such as humidity and CO2 level, in the instrument, such 
as aging of parts, and in sample handling that may affect the 
spectral response. For this reason, a protocol showing how to 
standardize spectral response across different centers using 
computational tools is needed as a final step before imple-
menting biospectroscopy in real-routine applications, since in 
this scenario samples will need to be measured by different 
instruments, analysts, and in different locations, and the diag-
nostic result will need to be consistent across these different 
environments.
23.4.7  Potential Outputs in Toxicological Settings
Toxicological assessments for risk-benefit analyses have tra-
ditionally been based on high-dose exposure-assay-endpoint 
assessments [37–39]. High-dose exposures have been tradi-
tionally employed to facilitate the acquisition of robust, statis-
tically positive, and reproducible results using endpoint such 
as chromosomal aberrations or carcinogenesis, which are rare 
events normally [40]. For several years now, the relevance of 
findings arising from such protocols has been questioned given 
that such high-dose exposures are far removed from (e.g., 
FIGURE 23.30  Data organization and processing toward classification and/or predictive (based on known outcomes, gold standard) model construc-
tion based on IR or Raman spectra.

1276
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
direct application of agent to cells in medium, oral gavage in 
vivo) typical exposure routes and far in excess of real-world 
situations (i.e., mM assay treatment vs. nM environmental/
dietary exposure). This has consistently cast doubt on one’s 
ability to extrapolate true risk from high-dose experiments to 
real-world settings. Biospectroscopy techniques potentially 
deliver a new paradigm for toxicological risk assessments 
whereby classification allows the identification of low-dose 
(i.e., real-world effects) alterations (away from control) and 
predictive algorithms built on known outcomes [39, 41, 42] 
negate the need for extended timeframes, especially for in vivo 
carcinogenic studies.
The basic premise of a biospectroscopy experiment employ-
ing a spectrochemical analytical technique harnessed to a 
computational algorithm is that one can build up a reference 
range within the heterogeneity of a typical biological sample 
(e.g., different cell types or similar cells but in varying phases 
of their lifespan) of what represents a normal or control state 
(Figure 23.30). Exposure-induced alterations that fall outside 
this reference range point to an atypical state that can be con-
sidered suspect. The computational algorithms (e.g., PCA, 
PCA-LDA) employed can be tailored to minimize within-
category heterogeneity while maximizing between-category 
discriminating features [43, 44]. The wavenumber absorbance 
intensities responsible for classification in an atypical state can 
be tentatively assigned to various biochemical constituents 
[45]. Following on from this, spectrochemical analyses har-
nessed to computational algorithms can be harnessed to gold 
standard outcomes such as histopathology showing cancer for-
mation, symptoms, or altered gene expression [46] to facilitate 
the development of predictive algorithms built and validated 
on known outcomes [47]. Biospectroscopy techniques are not 
designed to replace conventional toxicological endpoints but to 
serve as an adjunct to methods such as immunohistochemistry, 
cytokinesis-block micronucleus assay, serum biomarkers such 
as leakage of enzymes or RT-qPCR.
Figure 23.31 shows a hypothesized treatment-spectrochem-
ical alteration paradigm uncovered using a biospectroscopy 
approach. Treatments on the y-axis are at a range of concen-
trations from low-dose to high-dose concentration ranges. 
Along the x-axis, variable separation of categories is observed 
along a linear discriminant. A biphasic treatment concentra-
tion-spectral effect alteration is observed [48]. At low-dose (≥) 
concentrations, a small but marked treatment-related effect is 
noted. There is a point almost like a physiological threshold 
whereupon a plateau occurs and there appears to be no fur-
ther response or a stabilization. Once a further threshold con-
centration level is reached, there is again a treatment-related 
effect in a high-dose (high ōM to low mM, depending on 
solubility) range, but this time there is a steeper increase in 
spectral alterations. One might argue that there are minimal 
sub-lethal changes occurring in the low-dose range that could 
have a longer lasting effect on a living organism, whereas a 
more profound, close to cytotoxic, set of alterations occurs in 
the high-dose exposure scenario. The ability to identify dif-
ferent dose-response effects in different dose-response ranges 
exploring possible toxicological effects opens up an entire new 
area of exploration hitherto logistically close to impossible to 
examine in traditional in vitro or in vivo assays.
An alternative approach is to undertake an exploratory 
analysis using PCA in order to determine how categories of 
spectra taken from biological material (treated cells or tissues, 
for instance) fall into alignment in relation to each other in 
an unsupervised fashion. This allows one to visualize how 
clusters fall into place and how they may relate to each other 
in terms of similarity (closer to each other) or dissimilarity 
(further way from each other). In simple terms, each IR spec-
trum (if acquired at 4 cm∞1 wavenumber resolution generally 
FIGURE 23.31  A hypothetical treatment-spectrochemical alteration effect using a biospectroscopy approach. Treatments on the y-axis are at a range 
of concentrations from low-dose to high-dose exposures. Along the x-axis, variable separation of categories is observed along a linear discriminant. 
Circular symbols represent spectral points; the triangular symbol is the mean spectrum for each category.

1277
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
equals 249 data points in the bio-fingerprint region) is reduced 
to a single point in an n-dimensional hyperspace. These spec-
tral points are plotted along eigenvectors of the covariance 
matrix to give rise to score plots (Figure 23.32). In unsuper-
vised exploratory analysis such as PCA, these eigenvectors 
are known as PCs. The basic premise is that spectral points 
that are similar co-cluster and those that are dissimilar segre-
gate away. One can hypothesize a situation where, following 
various treatments, spectral points co-cluster and form a con-
tinuous trend of treatment categories within 95% confidence 
intervals (CIs) [38, 49]. Plotted along the individual PCs are 
the loadings plots that can give a weighting to the wavenum-
bers responsible for the variance associated with category 
separation (in the example in Figure 23.32, most relevant fea-
tures discriminating T1 and T2 or T4 and T5 are plotted along 
PC2, whereas most relevant wavenumbers separating T2 and 
T3 are along the “pseudo-spectrum” loadings plot associated 
with PC1). This form of trend analysis is novel in toxicologi-
cal studies and is especially applicable to the study of mixture 
effects [49, 50].
23.4.8  Imaging toward Objective 
Characterization of Biological Samples
A major challenge using conventional microscopy (e.g., bright-
field, fluorescence) is the subjectivity associated with an indi-
vidual’s direct visual interpretation of an image, especially in 
the absence of a gross distortion of cell or tissue architecture. 
The challenge is not distinguishing between control tissue 
architecture and a highly undifferentiated tumor mass in a tis-
sue section; it is where there is the ambiguity of a mild altera-
tion. Alterations leading to sub-lethal toxicological effects 
may be subtle or not apparent, increasing the likelihood that 
they are missed; however, it’s these low-level alterations that 
are likely to be more relevant to the long-term health of an 
organism and its offspring. Consequently, there has been an 
increasing interest in the application of sensor-based tech-
nologies, which allows one to objectively image material. Of 
note is the high degree of spatial resolution that such imaging 
modalities can achieve. Figure 23.33 shows an atomic force 
microscope (AFM) image of the edge of a transformed Syrian 
hamster embryo (SHE) colony in vitro. This reagent-free and 
non-destructive approach allows one to visualize in a novel 
fashion the disorganized growth pattern at the edge of this col-
ony. Such sensor-based imaging can now be used as an adjunct 
to more conventional methodologies involving staining or 
molecular analysis. Not only can such images derived from 
sensor-based technologies uncover additional visual informa-
tion, but harnessing these to computational algorithms can 
also allow novel approaches to highlight insidious alterations. 
An example is the application of acoustic algorithms to raise 
an audible alarm as a wealth of data is being scanned.
In spectrochemical imaging, a spectrum is generated for 
each pixel in the original image, where both spatial and 
chemical information are considered. Further to this, spec-
trochemical methods harnessed to computational algorithms 
allow the rapid acquisition of tens of thousands of fingerprint 
spectra to construct an image of the tissue architecture based 
on internal chemical bond vibrations (Figure 23.34). This 
powerful approach allows one to visualize the tissue archi-
tecture not based on a simple staining protocol that requires 
subjective visual interpretation but constructed from pre-
processed spectral numerical data (normalized to remove 
confounding factors such as differences in topology) derived 
from chemical bond vibrations. This allows one to image in 
an objective fashion the biomolecular constituents of the bio-
logical sample being analyzed. The critical aspect herein is 
the ability to potentially visualize sub-molecular alterations 
in an unaltered state. Figure 23.34 shows an image map based 
on Amide 1 throughout the length of a hair follicle traversing 
a cross-section of normal human skin. This includes the bulge 
along the length of the hair follicle where the majority of skin 
FIGURE 23.33  AFM image of a SHE colony indicating a disorganized 
architecture associated with transformation.
FIGURE 23.32  An exploratory analysis in a 2-D scores plot of spec-
tra derived from biological material following one of five different treat-
ments (T1, T2, T3, T4, or T5). Following PCA, spectral points are plotted 
along PC1 responsible for the majority of the variance and PC2, which 
accounts for the majority of the remaining variance. The category clus-
ters have 95% confidence intervals (CIs).

1278
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
stem cells are believed to reside. Such sensor-based imaging 
technologies allow novel insights into molecular structure and 
functionality.
Hyperspectral techniques combine imaging and spectros-
copy. To reiterate, an image is generated for the segment being 
analyzed but, for each image position (pixel), a spectrum is 
also generated, creating a 3D object for each sample measured, 
also called a hyperspectral “data-cube” [51]. This enriches, 
even more, the data obtained since one can access spatial 
information along the x- and y-axes and chemical information 
along the z-axis in a 3D array. This can be seen as stacking up 
single images of the segment being analyzed at different wave-
lengths one above the other. Thus, each wavenumber response 
(a 2D image) is stacked up one above the other in a manner 
similar to paper sheets in a book in order to form a 3D object 
in the “data-cube”. Owing to their complexity, multivariate 
analysis is often employed for data processing and interpreta-
tion of hyperspectral images [34]. These analyses often extract 
features and provide classification and predictive responses 
with minimal or no user interference, therefore creating reli-
able computational models to analyze images in an automated 
fashion.
Hyperspectral imaging is a powerful tool to obtain both 
chemical and spatial information of biological systems. The 
potential to be able to image non-destructively through a flat 
sample like a tissue section or liquid biopsy (e.g., plasma, 
serum, urine) to visualize alterations in constituents or the 
spatial location of exogenous materials such as microplas-
tics has enormous implications in toxicology. To date, few 
algorithms are capable of working with full 3D images, in 
which reshaping or averaging procedures are often performed 
to reduce the data complexity. We recently proposed a new 
algorithm of 3D-PCA for exploratory analysis of complete 
3D spectrochemical images obtained through Raman micro-
spectroscopy [52]. Our results show that 3D-PCA can clearly 
differentiate both different categories of samples based on 
scores plots, where higher loadings coefficients are observed 
in amino acids, lipids, and DNA regions. Given the emer-
gence of tunable light sources harnessed to spectrochemical 
techniques, it is highly likely that future computational algo-
rithms will seek to image the spatial location of contaminants 
and the sub-cellular entities they may align to or concentrate 
in. This will undoubtedly be a powerful tool in toxicology 
investigations.
FIGURE 23.34  A focal plane array (FPA) image of a hair follicle in human skin following synchrotron-based FTIR microspectroscopy acquired in 
transreflectance mode.

1279
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
23.4.9  Toward Translation to Monitor 
for Toxicological Effects in 
Humans: A New Frontier
Given the increasingly recognized problems in extrapolating 
toxicological findings from in vitro and in vivo laboratory set-
tings to real-world situations, there is an increasing need to be 
able to monitor general populations in real-world situations. 
There are many instances where this could be applicable. In 
the face of a rising obesity crisis, at-risk populations could 
be readily monitored for pre-diabetic states. Where there is a 
catastrophe, for instance, the Grenfell Tower fire in 2017, the 
adjacent exposed populations can be prospectively monitored 
for neurodegenerative changes, cancer, etc. There is rising con-
cern about exposure to nanoparticles and nanotoxicology with 
the important question being the relevance of such exposures to 
humans and other organisms [53, 54]. In the absence of appro-
priate assay systems to measure nanoparticle-induced effects 
in target cells, it is difficult to ascertain whether nanoparticles 
generate toxic effects directly or due to their large adsorption 
surfaces that they act as delivery vehicles for other environ-
mental contaminants, such as benzo[a]pyrene [55].
The most pervasive of all nanoparticles in our environment 
is a virus and the recent emergence of the SARS-CoV-2 coro-
navirus giving rise to the Covid-19 pandemic has highlighted 
the need for rapid test systems applicable to implementation 
in a general population setting. Applying the same strategy 
as basically outlined in Figure 23.30, using symptoms and 
RT-qPCR findings as known outcomes, we applied a computa-
tional algorithm to build and validate a model for prediction of 
external samples consisting of further pharyngeal swabs taken 
from study participants in a clinical point-of-care setting [56]. 
These swabs were then analyzed using ATR-FTIR spectros-
copy and the resultant spectra inputted into a GA-LDA algo-
rithm to predict the presence or absence of infection. This 
allowed us to derive a sensitivity and specificity classification 
of positive versus negative for SARS-CoV-2 infection. Using 
such an approach, one can see the feasibility of nesting these 
spectrochemical technologies in general population settings, 
allowing the implementation of prospective studies to develop 
predictive algorithms for risk to different patho-physiologies. 
There are ethical considerations to be surmounted but this 
would be a powerful new frontier in molecular epidemiology 
and risk assessment in toxicology.
Where would such technologies be applicable in the future? 
For instance, there has been an emergence of a condition known 
as long Covid characterized by persistent or recurring symptoms 
including long-term fatigue, short-term memory loss (i.e., proba-
bly neurological), cardiac effects, gastro-intestinal disturbances, 
and a range of other side effects. This spectrochemical approach 
can allow one to monitor whether the virus is intracellular in 
at least a proportion of the population and if it can potentially 
lie dormant to subsequently attack the host (i.e., the infected 
person). There are other examples of similarly-acting viral 
pathogens such as shingles caused by the varicella-zoster virus. 
Human papillomavirus (HPV) may be another example where a 
virus infection results in a long-term effect. The vast majority of 
women acquire HPV infection, primarily in their late teens/early 
twenties [57]. In the majority of infected women, their immune 
system clears this infection. However, there is a sub-group who 
retain persistent infection through their thirties and it is likely 
that it is in these women that cervical cancer arises.
As a person goes through life, one has ups and downs 
depending on natural health reserves (Figure 23.35). Typically, 
one could describe the maintenance of health reserves to 
correlate with an inverse function of time. There are times 
throughout life that health reserves will fall due to illness or 
other external causes and then recover but there will always be 
a gradual trend of loss correlated with time. This will eventu-
ally culminate with a diminishment of health reserve to the 
point of mortality in a typical life expectancy. With SARS-
CoV-2 infection, there may be an onset of long-term loss of 
health reserve with symptoms that take an extended period of 
time to recover from. This combined and a complex recurring 
viral attack is likely to accelerate loss of health reserve result-
ing in premature death. In essence, this is a toxicological insult 
which spectrochemical methods harnessed to computational 
algorithms are ideally placed to investigate and predict. If, for 
instance, cardiac effects or earlier onset of vascular dementia 
is a consequence of SARS-CoV-2, then an intervention could 
be the taking of a blood-thinning agent such as aspirin.
23.4.10  Concluding Remarks
Cellular or organism responses to exposures are a complex 
inter-play between adaptive and damage responses. Employing 
traditional conventional methods reliant on single endpoints 
such as micronucleus formation or tumor induction is fraught 
with many difficult challenges. Experiments are limited in 
terms of size and scope due to cost, subjectivity of assessment, 
or the time-consuming nature of their scoring. Consequently, 
such traditional experiments have been made further simplis-
tic, primarily for reproducibility and response induction, by 
employing high-dose regimens. Such exposures have been 
far in excess of those encountered in real-world scenarios. 
Extrapolating true risk from a traditional battery of toxicity 
tests has long been the subject of serious questions over their 
FIGURE 23.35  A hypothesized acceleration of loss of health reserve 
leading to mortality following SARS-CoV-2 infection resulting in condi-
tion known as long Covid.

1280
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
relevance. One of the major issues has been the one of chemi-
cal attrition whereby agents are dropped from a development 
pipeline because of an anomalous result in a particular cell 
line at a condition that would never be encountered in the envi-
ronment. However, the regulatory guidelines prohibit contin-
ued development of this agent for usage.
However, spectrochemical (FTIR and/or Raman spectros-
copy) analyses harnessed to computational algorithms offer 
a means of bypassing these shortcomings. This reagent-free 
and non-destructive approach allows the acquisition of large 
spectral datasets from any biological material. Point spectra 
or imaging can be readily undertaken and spectral data can be 
inputted into classifier or predictive algorithms [58] to deter-
mine risk. Such experiments can readily encompass the acqui-
sition of 100,000 spectra (Figures 23.34 and 23.36) and such 
capacity is rapidly growing as computer-processing power 
increases. This allows for an unprecedented ability to examine 
a vast range of treatment scenarios with different exposures to 
different agents, mixtures of agents, and at a wider dose (expo-
sure) range. Given the relatively non-invasive nature of the 
technique, particular samples can be monitored over time giv-
ing rise to a time-course examining the effects of an exposure 
on the very same sample. An enormous advantage of these 
approaches is the miniscule amount of sample, even compared 
to molecular biology techniques, required to generate a good 
spectrum with good SNR. For instance, 5 ōL plasma, serum, or 
urine is more than adequate to generate several spectral read-
ings. There are now imaging technologies that can generate 
sub-cellular spatial resolution [59]. The fact that one can deter-
mine SARS-CoV-2 infection at levels of <1,600 virus copies/
mL saliva suggests that the analytical sensitivity of spectro-
chemical approaches harnessed to computational algorithms 
deliver a LOD lower than more conventional test systems such 
as RT-qPCR [56].
Figure 23.36 shows an example of a single experiment 
where the effects of eight different treatments on SHE colonies 
were examined using ATR-FTIR spectroscopy in the in vitro 
transformation (pH 6.7) assay [39, 60]. Multiple spectra from 
several colonies per slide as part of a tray of slides associated 
with a single treatment can be readily analyzed. The spectral 
readings are objective and ready comparisons between the 
various categories can be made due to the ability of compu-
tational algorithms to reduce such complex and large datasets 
into a visual format. Additionally, the wavenumbers (spectral 
biomarkers) responsible for segregation of categories can be 
readily identified and tentatively assigned to bio-constituents 
such as lipids, proteins, nucleic acids, and glycogen. Post-
spectral analysis, the samples can be stained for conventional 
scoring. Thus, these novel methods are not designed to replace 
conventional microscopic or molecular biology techniques but 
to be an adjunct to these conventional methods to better inform 
the toxicologist.
FIGURE 23.36  An example of a 3D scores plot with ellipsoids representing 95% confidence intervals (CIs). IR spectra were obtained using ATR-
FTIR spectroscopy from SHE colonies grown on glass slides. Multiple slides per treatment were grown and analyzed using this reagent-free and 
non-destructive approach. Each of the eight categories represents a different treatment. The scores plots were plotted along linear discriminants (LDs) 
1, 2, and 3 following PCA-LDA.

1281
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
The application of spectrochemical approaches harnessed 
to computational algorithms for toxicology studies is a novel 
concept. Of course, toward adoption in regulatory and other 
laboratory settings, standardization for inter-user and inter-
instrument variation will need to be carefully examined [61]. 
However, there are protocols for such standardization pro-
cesses. However, there is no doubt that such sensor-based 
technologies with computational algorithms will generate a 
new approach toward assessing toxicological effects and risk 
analysis in future settings.
23.5  Ultra-Violet-Visible (UV-Vis) Spectroscopy
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
Analytical absorption spectroscopy in the UV and VIS 
regions of the electromagnetic spectrum has been widely used 
in pharmaceutical and biomedical analysis for quantitative pur-
poses and, with certain limitations, for the characterization of 
drugs, impurities, and metabolites of toxic substances. Some 
of these applications have been well recognized since the mid-
1950s [1, 2]. The principles of UV-VIS spectroscopy involve 
the measurement of light absorption by substances in the wave-
length region from 190 to 380 nm for UV absorption and from 
380 to 900 nm for VIS light absorption [1–3]. Absorption of 
light in these wavelength regions occurs when electrons within 
a molecule undergo energy state transitions. The frequency at 
which these transitions occur can be related to the structure of 
the molecules and the intensity of the absorptions can be related 
to molecule concentration. In most cases, data from UV-VIS 
experiments will be employed in conjunction with results from 
other instrumental measurements to form a more well-rounded 
array of results from which theories and hypotheses regarding 
toxicology mechanisms can be developed.
One of the disadvantages of UV-VIS is based not so much 
on the technology but rather on the fact that most of the mole-
cules having UV-VIS absorptions tend to present these absorp-
tions as relatively broad bands covering a relatively significant 
wavelength range. Thus, the overlap of absorptions can pres-
ent a notable problem when trying to discern molecular struc-
ture. Mathematical treatments of absorption spectral data have 
helped treat this issue by providing equations capable of gen-
erating derivatives of the absorption curves [4, 5]. This spec-
tral derivatization approach has helped in spectral resolution 
enhancement, spectral differentiation, qualitative and quanti-
tative analysis.
UV-Vis in addition to 1H NMR, 13C NMR, Raman, IR, and 
x-ray diffraction (XRD) has been employed to study the bio-
activity of a specific radical scavenger. Radical scavengers are 
most often organic molecules employed in biological systems 
to trap reactive free radicals known for their toxicological sig-
nificance. The radical scavenger, bis(pyrazolium p-toluene-
sulfonate), was found to bind to DNA and selected microbes 
through both covalent and electrostatic interactions [6]. The 
data from the UV-VIS portion of the investigation was impor-
tant in establishing the stability and toxicology of the radical 
scavenger molecule.
Gold nanoparticles (AuNPs) in combination with their 
“host” organic molecules and with selected anti-cancer 
pharmaceuticals are currently employed in the treatment of 
selected cancers [7]. These AuNPs serve as non-toxic carriers 
of the anti-cancer pharmaceuticals in the treatment regimes. 
In efforts to reduce any toxicological significance from the 
AuNPs, and increase their compatibility with cellular systems, 
selected-optimized organic molecules serve as hosts. These 
host molecules have been shown to significantly alter the sur-
face character of the AuNPs, thereby influencing their phar-
maceutical activity. These AuNPs have well-defined UV-Vis 
spectral characteristics. Thus, through the use of UV-Vis in 
conjunction with XRD and FTIR spectroscopy, the specifics 
of the surface of the AuNPs and their interaction with treat-
ment drugs have been effectively linked in determining their 
effectiveness as non-toxic anti-cancer pharmaceutical carriers.
Single metal nanoparticle, MNP, stability is an important 
issue to consider because MNP aggregates can affect the reac-
tive surface area and, hence, the reactivity, bioavailability, and 
toxicity of MNPs [8]. An exponential rise in the pharmaceuti-
cal applications of single MNPs in the most recent years has 
occurred. However, during the synthesis of MNPs, stabilizing 
agents must be added to the synthetic mixture to prevent the 
interaction of MNPs with one another. These interactions take 
place in part due to the fundamental physical nature of the 
particles themselves. These stabilizing or capping agents typi-
cally stabilize the MNPs through a number of molecular-based 
mechanisms. Silver, Ag, is of the more significant MNPs being 
employed today. Three different molecular-based approaches 
have been employed to prepare Ag MNPs and their stabil-
ity in selected media has been examined using by means of 
three complementary analysis techniques: (1) measuring the 
UV-VIS absorption spectra as an addition to visual observa-
tions, (2) transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and (3) 
dynamic light scattering. UV-VIS spectroscopy can be used as 
a characterization technique by providing information about 
the size and shape of the silver nanoparticles (AgNPs) [8]. 
Destabilization of the AgNPs and the formation of aggregates 
can lead to UV-VIS peak broadening or appearance of a sec-
ondary peak at longer wavelengths. Actually, an ever-increas-
ing aggregation eventually will lead to the disappearance of 
the typical UV-VIS absorption spectra, denoting the absence 
of the AgNPs and formation of aggregates. These changes can 
also be witnessed with the naked eye as solution color will 
change and possibly disappear. The difference in MNP stabil-
ity within the different environments examined was advanced 
to at least partly be responsible for the observed toxicity dif-
ferences. AgNPs have also been shown to chelate with certain 
proteins, more specifically, six intracellular protein targets 
(i.e., glutathione S-transferase, peroxiredoxin, myosin, elon-
gation factor 1, 60S ribosomal protein, and 40S ribosomal 
protein) were ultimately identified and confirmed as AgNPs- 
and Ag+-binding proteins, employing UV-VIS and other ana-
lytical techniques [9]. Having identified the specific binding 
sites should assist in the development of possible toxicology 
mechanisms.
Synthesis of AgNPs has most often involved less than 
optimal reagent consumption and related compound yields. 
Improvements in AgNP syntheses have been realized using 
a “green” approach from silver nitrate using glucose as a 
reducing agent and sucralose as a capping agent [10]. Physical

1282
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
and chemical characterization of AgNPs was performed by 
UV-Vis absorption and transmission electron microscopy 
(TEM) so that correlations between biological/toxicological 
responses with AgNPs features could be developed. A detailed 
study of the rate of AgNPs internalization process into human 
epithelioid cells of cervical cancer (HeLa) has been com-
pleted. The AgNPs were shown to impact cell viability, alter 
cell morphology, and induce the formation of reactive oxygen 
species (ROS). Graphite furnace atomic absorption spectros-
copy (GF-AAS), vide infra, was used in order to assess the sil-
ver concentration effectively internalized by the cells. Kinetic 
models were developed based on the rates of internalization.
While not directly related to the understanding of toxi-
cology mechanisms, UV-Vis spectroscopy in addition to 1H 
NMR, 13C NMR, GC, LC, thin-layer chromatography, size 
exclusion chromatography, capillary electrophoresis, and mass 
spectrometry (MS) has been presented as arrays of analytical 
technologies in the area of forensic toxicology [11–13].
Among the biggest advantages when employing UV-Vis 
technology are the accuracy of the spectrometer and the rela-
tive low cost and simplicity of the instrumentation. Among 
the main disadvantages is the lack of compound selectivity; 
for example, all nucleic acids exhibit a peak at 260 nm, with 
accompanying relatively low sensitivity [14]. Furthermore, the 
absorption of UV-VIS light is restricted to materials having 
certain molecular functional groups called chromophores, 
wherein electrons are excited at different UV-VIS frequen-
cies. Materials with no chromophores will display no UV-VIS 
spectrum, clearly a limitation for the technology. In terms of 
typical UV-VIS sensitivity, xylose in the concentration of 0.1 
to 0.5 g/L can be easily quantitated via UV/VIS techniques at 
671 nm [15]. Ultimately, the observed absorbance of any spe-
cies with a chromophore, arguably a measure of the UV-Vis 
sensitivity, depends on three parameters: (1) analyte concen-
tration, (2) sample cell path length, and (3) analyte extinction 
coefficient [2, 3], as governed by Beer’s law. A compara-
tive dataset of experimental and computational attributes of 
UV-Vis absorption spectra including molar extinction coeffi-
cients has been recently published [16].
23.6  Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (NMR)
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
Of the more powerful technologies available for investiga-
tions into toxicology mechanisms, NMR spectroscopy has 
emerged as, arguably, among the leading candidates. Based 
in part on its capability to provide qualitative and quantitative 
information on the structures of organic molecules in a non-
destructive manner has fueled its momentum. Recent advances 
in sensitivity and sample size requirements have boosted its 
applicability. Fundamentally, NMR looks directly at the nuclei 
that comprise a molecular structure and based on very subtle 
differences in the nature of the bonds attached to the nuclei 
of interest, molecular structural features of the analyte(s) of 
interest can be readily attained [1]. For example, in the case 
of 13C, 1H, 15N, 19F, and 31P NMR, detailed software programs 
are available which will predict the NMR spectrum of a vast 
array of organic molecules [2]. Such predictive capabilities can 
assist in the interpretation of NMR results, thus assisting in the 
determination of the correct structure(s) encountered. In recent 
years, the sensitivity of the NMR approach has readily reached 
the microgram level and in many cases sub-microgram levels. 
Furthermore, for a number of significant nuclei, accurate and 
precise quantitative measurements of the analytes of interest 
can be readily attained from NMR spectral data, where the 
intensity of the analyte response can be directly related to its 
concentration.
When the most recent literature surrounding toxicology 
mechanisms is reviewed from an NMR perspective, a wed-
ding of the NMR data with metabolomics is inescapable. This 
wedding appears to, arguably, dominate the NMR-based toxi-
cology literature [3]. In fact, NMR, GC/MS, and LC/MS are 
the three principal analytical technologies used in metabolo-
mics. By way of brief background, metabolomics deals with 
the whole ensemble of metabolites that may appear in a toxico-
logical investigation. The entire set of metabolites is referred 
to as the metabolome. To make the link between the two 
technologies a bit more clear, the practice of metabolomics 
has as critical components accurate and precise qualitative 
and quantitative chemical structure information on all of the 
metabolites identified in an investigation. Information from 
NMR data can provide for a large portion of these needed 
components. Four nuclei dominate the literature when NMR 
and metabolomics research are discussed: 13C, 1H, 15N, and 
31P. Aspects of NMR-metabolomics investigations, focusing 
on these nuclei appear below.
Because of its fundamental premise, a significant number 
of experimental variances to the fundamental NMR experi-
ment can be employed to enhance the value of experimental 
data. For example, because NMR-active protons (1H, 99% iso-
tope abundance) are found in almost all organic compounds, 
including, for example, most metabolites, the vast majority of 
NMR-based metabolomics studies have in their data set infor-
mation derived from 1H NMR spectra. The data is most often 
expressed in the form of numerical chemical shift values. 
These chemical shift values are related to the nature of the 
bonds attached to the proton and how these differences relate 
to that of the proton bonds in a known standard, like tetrameth-
ylsilane, TMS. The range of difference in chemical shift for 
protons is about 10 ppm, with the value of the protons in TMS 
being set at 0. Published studies have documented that quali-
tative and quantitative information on multiple components/
metabolites in human serum can be readily obtained from a 
single 1H NMR spectrum [4, 5]. These studies revealed that 
newly developed algorithms coupled with designed software 
could quantify 61 out of 67 metabolites in 30 human plasma 
spectra that showed a goodness-of-fit (r2) close to or exceeding 
0.9. The efficiency was tested and confirmed by implement-
ing the special software for quantification of 67 metabolites in 
a large data set comprising 1,342 experimental spectra from 
human plasma, in which the whole computation took less than 
1 s. In a similar fashion, the performances of a software-based 
method were investigated on known mixtures (such as syn-
thetic urine) and on plasma datasets from duck and human.
In human urine, quantitative information on an extensive 
array of free amino acids, alcohols, glycols, sugars, and car-
boxylic acids is readily available from a 1H NMR spectrum.

1283
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
Thus, metabolomics employing 1H NMR data has been applied 
to epidemiology studies wherein the number of samples ranges 
well over 1,000 [6, 7]. Intra-assay metabolite variations were 
found to be mostly <5%, thus indicating a high robustness and 
accuracy of urine NMR spectroscopy methodology. Intra-
individual metabolite variations, on the other hand, were found 
to be relatively large, ranging from 6% to 194%. However, and 
importantly, population-based inter-individual metabolite 
variations were even larger (from 14% to 1,655%), providing 
for a sound base directed toward epidemiological applica-
tions. Applications of 1H magnetic resonance metabolomics 
for quantifying circulating biomarkers in large-scale epide-
miology have been pursued. Application of this metabolomic 
technology has allowed for the molecular characterization of 
risk factors and the key issues of study design and analyses of 
metabolic profiling for epidemiology. Coupling of metabolic 
profiling data with genetic-based information was advanced 
as an approach for enhanced drug development and rather pos-
sibly translating into abilities to predict disease risks.
The capability to identify and quantify so many metabolites 
in a complex mixture, like human urine and serum, is based in 
part on the fact that any single 1H NMR signal most often has a 
very narrow width (<0.1 ppm), allowing for resolution of many 
different responses. It is important to note that additional 
somewhat more complex 1H-based NMR experiments can be 
performed on the same sample by simply adjusting instrumen-
tal parameters. These experiments are generally termed 2D 
NMR and have been successfully employed to identify even 
more metabolites in urine and serum [8, 9].
The toxicology of carbon dots has been described using 
1H NMR-based metabolomics [10]. A 1H NMR-based meta-
bolic approach supplemented with biochemical analysis and 
histopathology was used to explore the toxicity of C-dots in 
vivo. Histopathological inspection revealed that C-dots did not 
induce any obvious impairment in tissues. Biochemical assays 
showed no significant alterations of most measured biochemi-
cal parameters in both tissue and serum samples. Advanced 
analysis of NMR profiles from the samples disclosed that 
C-dots not only triggered the immune system but also dis-
turbed the function of cell membranes as well as the normal 
liver clearance, indicating that the 1H NMR-based metabolo-
mics approach provided meaningful information into the tox-
icity of C-dots in vivo.
Since most biological samples are relegated to a water 
matrix, and the water molecule contains two 1H atoms, a dis-
tinct disadvantage of 1H NMR thus rests with the requirement 
that the 1H signals from the water molecule be attenuated/
suppressed as much as possible. Successful suppression of the 
signal from the water molecule is possible and thus must be 
included as an integral part of any experiment on biological 
fluids. Some assistance in this suppression can be obtained 
through the use of D2O since D is not NMR active under these 
conditions.
As mentioned above, 1H is not the only nuclei that is NMR 
active. When compared to 1H NMR spectroscopy, which is 
characterized by narrow line widths and a relatively narrow 
chemical shift dispersion (of Σ10 ppm), 13C NMR spectroscopy 
also has narrow line widths combined with a relatively rather 
broad (Σ200 ppm) chemical shift dispersion, again with the 
chemical shift of the all-equivalent carbon atoms within TMS 
equal to 0. As a result, 13C NMR offers a significant resolution 
advantage over 1H NMR in determining molecular structure 
components. In addition, modern NMR spectrometers have 
13C signal resolution capabilities <0.1 ppm, thus providing 
for resolution of a significant increased number of responses. 
Together, the rather broad range of chemical shifts, Σ200 ppm, 
coupled with the excellent signal resolution, means that quali-
tative information on the nature of the bonds attached to the 
carbon atom is more diverse relative to the same information 
on the bonds attached to the proton. However, on the other 
hand, and very importantly, the low natural abundance of 13C 
(Σ1.1%) coupled with the inherent low sensitivity of the 13C 
nucleus (compared to other common nuclei observed by NMR 
such as 1H, 19F, or even 31P) substantially hinders the applica-
tion of 13C NMR spectroscopy to metabolomics. This low rela-
tive natural abundance practically dictates that much longer 
data acquisition times are required before meaningful metabo-
lomics information from 13C spectra can be obtained. Taking 
advantage of interactions between proton and carbon nuclei 
in the 13C NMR experiment can lead to increases in signal 
intensity, improving SNR ratios, thus reducing data acquisi-
tion time. However, 13C NMR still rests well behind that of 1H 
NMR in terms of practical approaches in metabolomics-based 
investigations. In a similar fashion, the NMR characteristics 
of the carbon atom (carbon nuclei relaxation times) make a 
quantitative analysis of 13C NMR data much less attractive 
when compared to the straightforward 1H NMR quantitative 
capabilities.
Should qualitative data be a key component of a metabolo-
mics study, then 13C NMR can be viewed as being very valu-
able. This is even more the case when 13C labeled/enriched 
precursors are employed. This approach has been effectively 
used in the study of cancer cells [11]. However, it was observed 
that even at high NMR-active isotope abundance, the acquisi-
tion of one-dimensional 13C and classical 2D 1H, 13C-HSQC 
experiments remained very time consuming. With the aim 
to provide a shorter acquisition time, the ALSOFAST-HSQC 
experiment with its rapid acquisition scheme for the analysis 
of 13C-labeled metabolites in complex biological mixtures was 
explored using optimized data acquisition parameters. The 
technique was applied to study the effect of different kinds of 
antioxidant AuNPs on a HeLa cancer cell model grown on 13C 
glucose-enriched medium. The rapid acquisition time allowed 
for NMR data correlations to be obtained in a couple of sec-
onds to a few minutes (<30 minutes) versus hours, allowing 
a simple and reliable identification of various 13C-enriched 
metabolites within the samples. Thus, it was possible to moni-
tor glucose metabolism in the cell model and study the antioxi-
dant effect of the coated AuNPs in detail.
15N NMR has some of the same issues as 13C NMR in terms 
of its practical application to the study of toxicology mecha-
nisms. To make 15N NMR valuable for metabolomics essen-
tially dictates that 15N labeled materials be employed. The 
characteristics of the 15N atom (0.37% relative abundance) from 
an NMR perspective are such that conservative estimates ren-
der the 15N nucleus 262,000 times less sensitive than the com-
parable 1H NMR experiment [3]. From a first approximation, 
this would mean that to obtain a similar SNR ratio as available

1284
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
from one 1H NMR scan would require 262,000 scans in a 15N 
experiment. However, through the use of advanced NMR data 
acquisition parameters and NMR hardware improvements, 
experimental methods for the collection and analysis of 2D 
1H 15N-amide- and 1H 13C-methyl-correlated spectra applied to 
protein drug products at natural isotopic abundance, including 
representatives from the rapidly growing class of monoclonal 
antibody therapeutics, have been described. These methods 
allowed for characterization of the higher-order structure of 
protein therapeutics [12]. These obstacles are most often over-
come through the use of 15N-enriched molecules that have the 
ability to be covalently incorporated into the molecules of 
interest and serve not only as a molecular tag (for mass spec-
trometry applications) but also improve dramatically the capa-
bility to detect the N atom in complex biological matrices such 
as metabolites [13].
Before leaving this discussion surrounding the use of isotopi-
cally enriched species in NMR-based metabolomics research, 
the use of the identical isotopically labeled species in mass-
spectrometry (MS)-based metabolomics should be briefly 
mentioned. In fact, as can be seen in the literature, these two 
approaches, NMR and MS, using isotopically enriched species 
is a common pared-approach, with both methods complement-
ing each other to add more meaningful aspects to any metabo-
lomics data set. Since both 13C and 15N isotopes are naturally 
very low in abundance, the mass spectrum of any labeled spe-
cies can be readily distinguished and observed due to these 
changes in mass.
While 31P possesses very admirable NMR characteristics, 
like 100% natural isotope abundance and excellent relative 
sensitivity, its use in metabolomics is somewhat limited by the 
relatively small number of 31P metabolites having toxicologi-
cal significance [3]. Some surfactants are known to be toxic 
to Artemia franciscana (Artemia), an organism frequently 
used to monitor the health of the aquatic environment. In liv-
ing organisms, the phospho-metabolite profile is often indica-
tive of imbalances in biological activities that may have been 
caused by environmental stressors, surfactants being among 
the most common. Taking advantage of the positive character-
istics of 31P NMR, an in vivo 31P NMR study monitoring tem-
poral changes in the phospho-metabolite profile of Artemia 
caused by surfactants has been reported [14]. The study found 
that certain surfactants did possess toxicity while others did 
not, based on quantitative and qualitative changes in the 31P 
NMR signal profile. These changes also facilitated descrip-
tions of the mechanisms defining the toxicological pathways.
The development of microcoil NMR has served to facilitate 
studies in toxicology mechanisms [15–17], simply by provid-
ing the capability to obtain NMR results on very small sample 
sizes, microliters. Conventional NMR probes, wherein the 
NMR coil resides, have a probe diameter of approximately 
5 mm. These newer microcoils have a diameter of ~50 ōm. 
Thus, where conventional NMR probe volumes were in the 
mL range, these microcoils have probe volumes on the order 
of 5 ōL. These positive changes in coil size have resulted in 
advances in the study of single-egg toxicology [16]. In some 
cases, flow systems have been designed for these microcoils 
and this allows for in vivo measurements as well as the determi-
nation of reaction-product diffusion coefficients on relatively 
very small sample volumes [17–21], 5 ōL, due for the most part 
because the NMR experiment is inherently non-destructive. A 
significant contribution to these enhanced NMR capabilities 
has been the introduction of microcoils capable of allowing 
for precise control of droplet position and shape within the 
coil. A distinct advantage of flow in vivo NMR experiments 
is the capability to study the same organisms for both control 
and exposure studies again because NMR is a non-destruc-
tive analytical approach. By doing so, individual variability 
somewhat common with in vivo experiments is essentially 
eliminated as a concern. Through a combination of isotopic 
labeling, microcoil dimensions, and flow in vivo NMR, excep-
tional details regarding toxicology mechanisms are possible 
thus advancing the overall metabolomics approach. When 1H 
flow in vivo NMR is employed metabolic flux in close to real 
time can be examined.
Solid-state NMR experiments are performed based on the 
concept of magic angle spinning (MAS) wherein the sample 
is placed in the magnetic probe at a specific angle to the mag-
netic field. Most often in the past, the MAS experiments have 
been conducted on solids. However, with advances in probe 
design, MAS is now possible with mobile fluids like blood and 
urine, also including solid materials such as muscle tissues as 
well as gel-like matrices. An HR MAS 1H NMR approach has 
been used to investigate the metabolic responses in benign 
and cancerous tissue samples from patients before and after 
treatment [22]. HR MAS 1H NMR responses from six metabo-
lites were monitored and quantitated. A further development 
the technology of the modified HR MAS 1H NMR, termed 
slow-MAS NMR, has been postulated to play a new role in 
studying metabolomics employing in intact biological tissues 
[23]. Capability for small sample sizes as 1 ōL in volume is 
important for sample-limited applications. Probe capacities of 
relatively conventional large sample volume, ~1 mL, should 
be useful for investigating, for example, an entire intact organ 
from a small laboratory animal or tissues from a surgery room 
in a clinical setting.
Conservative estimates suggest there to be at least 103 to 104 
metabolites in an average biological material such as human 
urine [24]. Thus, not surprising has been the appearance of 
software tools and data handling approaches to assist the 
metabolomics approach to understanding toxicology mecha-
nisms [25–27]. Databases on as many as 598 metabolites have 
been constructed. Multivariate statistical methods such as par-
tial least squares and PCA are now routinely employed to assist 
in fingerprinting metabolite profiles. A Bayesian deconvolu-
tion method for the analysis of NMR data has shown poten-
tial to advance metabolomics in the areas of identification and 
quantification of metabolites in human samples such as urine. 
Complementing these mathematical treatments of metabolite 
data are the readily available databases on human metabolites 
[3]. Within these databases is information on the structures 
and characteristics of over 114,000 metabolites based in part 
on NMR results.
A discussion of NMR and metabolomics is not complete 
without at least one or two illustrations of how NMR interfaces 
with other technologies to complete a more powerful picture of 
toxicological mechanisms. By combining data gathered from 
NMR and mass spectrometry (MS) studies a broad-based

1285
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
metabolic profile has been created for 30 metabolites in a sin-
gle serum specimen [28]. The wedding of the NMR and MS 
data sets has led to the quantitation of the 30 metabolites with 
excellent correlations between the data sets (R2 > 0.99) with a 
median CV of 3.2%. Such excellent correlation values and min-
imal CV values lend credence to the importance of including 
sets of data from multiple analytical approaches in a metabo-
lomics analysis. In combining data from LC with data from 
NMR spectroscopy (i.e., LC-NMR), the advantage is taken of 
the strengths of modern (HPLC, UHPLC) chromatographic 
separation techniques that can greatly simplify the complexity 
of biological mixtures. In many cases, the spectral complex-
ity of biofluids such as urine and other biological extracts is 
so great that the complexity even challenges the strength of 
advanced NMR techniques to provide meaningful qualitative 
and quantitative data. By using LC coupled with the selection 
of different column effluent fractions generated from the ini-
tially relatively complex mixture allows these now simplified 
fractions to undergo a more complete analysis by NMR and 
other techniques [29–30]. For example, through a combination 
of stable isotope labeling, separation by HPLC and detection 
by NMR, the ability to separate structurally related metabo-
lites in human urine has been demonstrated, accompanied by 
quantitative determinations of the metabolites. This type of a 
more complete analysis provides added meaningful informa-
tion for the metabolomics approach.
The aforementioned applications of NMR-metabolomics are 
not to be assumed to be all inclusive of the NMR approaches 
that have been employed in this area of research. There are 
numerous additional NMR experiment sequences that are not 
mentioned above, but nonetheless have yielded significant 
advances in the study of the mechanisms of toxicology [3]. 
Examples of these NMR approaches include 2D NMR, cor-
relation (COSY) NMR, total correlation NMR (TOCSY), 2D 
J-resolved NMR (J-RES), heteronuclear single quantum cor-
relation NMR (HSQC). All of these NMR sequences bring 
their own unique perspectives to data collection and resultant 
metabolite qualitative and quantitative information [3].
Any analytical technique reveals both its strengths and weak-
nesses when viewed objectively. The discussions above have 
obviously reflected on the strengths of the NMR-metabolomics 
approach to metabolic data collection and analysis. However, 
low relative sensitivity for biologically important nuclei, such 
as C and N, is a distinct disadvantage for the NMR approach. 
This low sensitivity could possibly result in the absence of data 
related to metabolites that themselves may represent impor-
tant metabolic pathways that would thus remain undiscov-
ered. Long acquisition times are often employed to overcome 
this shortcoming, but this, in turn, limits sample throughput. 
Finally, the cost of NMR instrumentation relative to GC/mass 
spectrometers (GC/MS), and LC/mass spectrometers (LC/
MS) places it at somewhat of a disadvantage.
A list of typical 13C and 1H chemical shift ranges, TMS = 0, 
for selected organic functional groups appears in Table 23.6 [31].
With toxicology mechanism studies, very often the mol-
ecules/metabolites of interest possess an array of organic 
functional groups, such as hydroxyl, carbonyl, ether, aromatic, 
and aliphatic. As with employing any analytical technology, 
obtaining the most information concerning the molecules of 
interest with a minimal time investment is very desirable. 
The following discussion paragraphs serve to illustrate, in a 
short tutorial fashion, how an overall NMR approach has the 
potential to provide meaningful qualitative and quantitative 
information on molecules containing a relatively wide array 
of functional groups. For illustrative purposes, the molecule 
vanillin has been selected, vide infra. The vanillin molecule 
contains the following functional groups: hydroxyl, carbonyl, 
ether, aliphatic, and aromatic. Thus, how can NMR be applied 
to characterize this molecule or molecules involved in toxicol-
ogy mechanism studies? That is how can the NMR signal data 
be manually interpreted so as to provide quantitative and qual-
itative information on the molecules of interest? (computer-
based library searches not withstanding). First, there are the 
proton and carbon chemical shift values, vide supra, serving 
to indicate the types of protons and carbons within the mol-
ecule, and second, with proton NMR data, there is the capabil-
ity of determining the ratio of the protons within the molecule 
one to another based on signal intensities. This quantitative 
capability for proton NMR is in part related to atomic-level 
characteristics related to the proton atom during the NMR data 
acquisition which does not exist for the carbon atom.
The proton NMR of vanillin appears as shown in 
Figure 23.37. Based on literature proton chemical shift val-
ues and ranges of chemical shift values observed, assignments 
can be made for the six well-resolved signals appearing in the 
spectrum, Table 23.7. Also with 1H NMR, ratios of the intensi-
ties of the signals can be directly related to the ratios of the 
protons within the molecule one to another. Notice that signal 
labeled “a” has a relative intensity ratio of 3, while all the other 
signals have a relative ratio of 1. This observation in combina-
tion with the chemical shift value is a strong indicator that this 
signal is due to the vanillin methyl group (3 protons). Such 
relative intensity values can be readily applied to 1H NMR 
data gathered in the course of toxicology mechanism studies.
Thus, the presence of six well-resolved signals across a wide 
chemical shift range for each proton type within this model 
compound speaks well to the capability of 1H NMR to pro-
vide molecular structural qualitative and quantitative informa-
tion on compounds containing a relatively diverse an array of 
protons.
The 13C NMR of vanillin appears as shown in Figure 23.38. 
Based on literature 13C chemical shift values and ranges of

determine structural features of the molecules of interest.
Continuing on this brief tutorial regarding NMR data inter-
pretation, the data presented in the discussion above can be 
employed in an alternative fashion to illustrate how NMR data 
collected from an unknown compound may be employed and

an ether, and (5) the absence of any other aliphatic carbon sig-

NMR data into account, the following can be very reasonably 
concluded as integral to the structure of the “unknown”: (1) the

Such NMR information, as discussed above, when combined 
with data collected on the unknown compound from other 
instrumental methods discussed within this chapter, can thus 
help place the role of NMR data in a proper perspective as 
key to building structural information on molecules of interest 
in toxicology mechanism studies. Automated searches of 13C 
NMR databases have provided and may also provide valuable 
assistance in the assignment of compound structure [32].
NMR along with mass spectrometry (MS) have evolved as 
the most commonly applied techniques in toxicological mech-
anism studies and metabolomics, each with their own strengths 
and weaknesses. Unlike MS, NMR is non-destructive, quan-
titative, reproducible, free of batch effects (samples do not 
interact directly with the instrument) and does not necessar-
ily require extra steps for sample preparation. Although NMR 
sensitivity for 1H and 13C nuclei has increased enormously, this 
still, however, remains the weakest point for NMR relative to, 
for example, MS [33,34]. Data published by The University of 
Wisconsin NMR facility indicates that for 1H NMR experi-
ments a detection limit of 8 ōg or approximately 16 nano-
moles can be easily realized with a data acquisition time of 
a few minutes. For 13C NMR experiments, a detection limit 
of ~2 ōmol of material can be observed in a few hours of data 
acquisition [35]. A relatively recent publication describes data 
gathered from selected NMR nuclei that have been applied 
to the study of metabolomics [36]. A list of the more com-
monly studies NMR nuclei in toxicological mechanism stud-
ies appears in Table 23.9 and how NMR results from these 
nuclei are employed in in vivo NMR-based toxicological stud-
ies appears in a recent publication [37].
23.7  Electron Spin Resonance (ESR)
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
ESR, also referred to as electron paramagnetic resonance 
(EPR), is one of the magnetic resonance spectrometry tools 
which very selectively detects only unpaired electrons, free 
radicals [1]. This specificity, this single focus, readily distin-
guishes ESR from NMR, another magnetic resonance tool, 
because, vide supra, NMR can detect and quantitate a much 
more comprehensive range of magnetic nuclei. However, this 
seemingly on the surface apparent disadvantage for ESR can 
be otherwise viewed as actually a strength. That is, with this 
sole focus, the ESR experiment can detect free radicals regard-
less of the chemical reactions that have resulted in their gen-
eration, in other words, the ESR free radical signal does not 
rely on the response from any specific nuclei. In fact, when 
free electrons interact with surrounding nuclei, more informa-
tion becomes available in the presentation features of the free 
radical signal, a term called hyperfine splitting [1]. This hyper-
fine splitting of the ESR signal is readily attributable to the 
specific spin characteristics of the neighboring nuclei. In addi-
tion, the non-destructive ESR experiment is readily adaptable 
to in situ measurements. For example, the characteristics of 
the free radical in a wide variety of different matrices are such 
that the free radical signal can be detected at very low levels. 
Some of these in situ matrices could include fat tissue, whole 
blood, brain matter, liver, kidney, muscle tissues, plus a wide 
array of inorganic species. Two distinguishing characteristics 
of the unpaired electron, free radical, from an ESR signal per-
spective are its g value and any observed hyperfine splitting 
of the ESR signal due to interaction with surrounding nuclei 
[1]. These values can assist in determining the structure of 
the molecule containing the free radical. Thus, ESR is recog-
nized as the “go-to” method for characterizing materials with 
unpaired electrons, free radicals. For a detailed discussion of 
the ESR experiment and associated hardware, the reader is 
directed toward a very recent publication [1].
It is a generally accepted tenant that harmful impacts of 
toxic materials can often result in the generation of free radi-
cals in biological tissues. Being very reactive species, these 
free radicals, thus, potentially have toxicological relevance in 
that determining their molecular structure can facilitate in the 
understanding of the underlying toxicological mechanism. A 
common approach to the study of free radicals in biological 
and other systems involves the use of a spin trap or a spin trap 
cocktail [2, 3]. The spin-trapping approach is based on the 
selective reaction between the free radical under study with 
a specifically pre-designed molecule, the spin trap, that when 
reacted effectively “stabilizes” (traps) the very reactive free 
radical. Once “stabilized”, the free radical can be examined 
in detail.
As a proof of concept, for employing the spin-trapping cock-
tail, the ability of such a cocktail to trap and measure a large 
variety of free radicals (O-, N-, C-, or S- centered) has been 
characterized [2]. The ability of the technique to unambigu-
ously detect free radical production in cells exposed to a vari-
ety of chemicals known to be radical-mediated toxic agents 
was demonstrated. The cocktail was employed because it had 
been previously demonstrated that there are no universal spin-
trapping reagents that will capture (trap) all free radicals in 
all matrices. For example, the applicability and usefulness 
of a stable cocktail of different spin traps – N-tert-butyl-
−-phenylnitrone, −(4-pyridyl-1-oxide)-N-tert-butylnitrone, 
5-diethoxyphosphoryl-5-methyl-1-pyrroline-N-oxide, and 
5-ethoxycarbonyl-5-methyl-pyrroline-N-oxide – have been 
examined [2]. The rationale for using this cocktail was, in part, 
that the cocktail could most likely cover spin-trapping reac-
tions over a relatively wide range of biologically relevant free 
radicals having, themselves, a large range of hydrophilicity and 
TABLE 23.9
Most Commonly Employed NMR-Active Nuclei in Toxicological 
Mechanism Studies
Nuclei

13C
