Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
lipophilicity (expressed as a coefficient of partition between 
octanol and phosphate buffer from values between 0.15 and 
10) a known characteristic of different cellular components. 
The following free radicals (with associated nuclei) were gen-
erated using well-defined chemical reactions: superoxide anion 
radical (O), hydroxyl radical (OH), alkoxyl radicals (RO), per-
oxide radical (OOH) sulfite radicals (S), methyl radical (CH3), 
and azidyl (N) radicals. When the free radicals were generated 
in situ with cells, each radical species was found to produce 
ESR spectra, with associated hyperfine coupling, that were 
essentially identical to those predicted by computer-simulated 
spectra. Thus, knowledge of the structure of the free radical 
available from ESR experimental data should provide mecha-
nistic information on the routes of toxicological responses.
Due to the high sensitivity of ESR and its ability to iden-
tify the free radicals in situ, an ESR spin-trapping technique 
has been used to evaluate toxic ROS generated when nano-
materials interact with biological systems [3]. Nanoparticles 
such as TiO2 are well recognized as promoting the formation 
of toxic ROS. The formation of ROS, including 1O2, OH, and 
O2
∞) were detected using different spin traps and their forma-
tion was shown to depend on the intrinsic physical properties 
of the nanomaterials, that is, including size, crystal phase, and 
surface characteristics, as well as the environmental condi-
tions surrounding the exposure. In addition, ESR spin label 
that detects changes in O2 levels in a system was shown to 
be a reliable and effective method to monitor lipid peroxida-
tion rate. Such an oxygen-monitoring capability is of critical 
importance when it comes to assessing cellular oxidative dam-
age due to ROS that could lead to subsequent adverse health 
effects. These results and the approaches employed could pos-
sibly have an application to the study of ROS generated from a 
variety of toxic materials.
Asbestos fibers are well recognized for their toxic impact 
[4]. In an effort to gain a further fundamental understanding of 
the mechanism(s) of their toxicology, ESR has been employed 
to study the molecular level interaction of the asbestos fibers 
with model membranes such as micelles and selected lipo-
somes [4]. Examinations related to the site of entrance of the 
fibers into the membrane as well as the location of the fibers 
at both the internal and external membrane interfaces were 
conducted. The physical structure of the fibers was shown to 
have differentiated interactions with the selected membrane 
substrates. The ESR-generated data strongly indicated that 
the toxicity of the fibers was most likely related to structural 
modifications of the model membranes having resulted from 
contact with the fibers.
The human monocyte U937 cell line has been used as a 
model to verify the toxicity of erionite and offretite asbesti-
form zeolite fibers [5]. A non-toxic reference, a fibrous sco-
lecite zeolite was also examined and used as a control. To 
analyze the process of fiber ingestion into cells and the cells-
fibers interactions, a spin-probe ESR analysis was performed 
supported by additional analytical techniques including 
transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and cell viability 
measurements. Erionite fibers were discovered to internalize 
in the membrane quickly mainly as aggregates with radi-
cals trapped inside and were found in the cytosol and at the 
nucleus. After 24-hour incubation time, first ­erionite fibers 
rich in sodium and potassium, and then calcium-rich erion-
ite fibers, were found to induce cell necrosis. The offretite 
fibers were shown to form rounding electron-dense filaments 
which transformed in curved filaments, first perturbing the 
cell structure and then interacting at the external surface 
more than erionite fibers and creating morphological varia-
tions in cell structure. The presumed non-toxic scolecite 
fibers were only partially internalized, inducing formation 
of swollen mitochondria and squared cells. Using the entire 
data set, the toxic effects of the fibrous zeolites were related 
to fiber morphology, chemical distribution of sites, struc-
tural variations, and formation of aggregates. The paired 
ESR-TEM analytical approach was found to provide useful 
information regarding fiber-to-cell ingestion toxic mecha-
nisms on the molecular level.
The use of a specific spin probe can facilitate the ability to 
detect the presence of ROS, vide supra. The effectiveness of 
cyclic hydroxylamine spin probes to spin trap ROS has been 
recently described [6]. Several hydroxylamine spin traps were 
shown to readily react with ROS to produce stable nitroxide 
radicals that could be quantitatively measured by ESR both in 
vitro and in vivo.
The toxicity of pulverized minerals to which future plan-
etary explorers may be exposed has been examined by 
employing ESR techniques [7]. Pulverized minerals were 
incubated in an aqueous solution containing the free-radical 
spin-trapping compound 5,5-dimethyl-1-pyrroline-N-oxide. 
These slurries were then analyzed by ESR to quantify the 
amount of hydroxyl radical (the neutral charge form of the 
hydroxide ion, denoted as OH→) formed in solution. All 
tested materials generated an ESR spectrum indicating the 
formation of OH→ with concentrations ranging between 0.1 
and 1.5 ōM. OH→ generation was positively correlated to min-
eral fluid incubation time for materials that had iron in their 
nominal chemical formulas, suggesting the possible action 
of the Fenton reaction, Fe2+ + H2O2 κ  Fe3+ + OH→ + OH∞, as 
the main mechanism for the OH→ generation. The intensity 
of the ESR signal was found to correlate with the amount 
of Fe2+ in the pulverized materials. These findings strongly 
indicated that minerals and rocks present in planetary rego-
lith as very small particles that contain Fe2+ are capable of 
generating highly reactive and highly oxidizing radical spe-
cies in solution, thus, posing a health risk to explorers visit-
ing the surfaces of planetary bodies.
As with any analytical approach, ESR has its strengths and 
weaknesses. It is a very useful quantitative approach for para-
magnetic samples. ESR is capable of providing useful data in 
volumes as low as 300 ōL and analyte concentrations as low as 
1 ōM, with data acquisition times of 15–20 minutes. Its speci-
ficity to paramagnetic species can be in some respect viewed 
as a limitation but also, conversely, as an advantage that elimi-
nates contribution from other species possibly present in the 
sample under investigation. Most paramagnetic materials do 
possess ESR responses because of the nature of the free elec-
tron, however, to obtain a reliable ESR response requires a 
very low temperature of 20 K for detection which can be an 
expensive constraint [8].

1290
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
23.8  Stable Isotopes
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
When the subject of reaction mechanisms in toxicology is 
discussed, without fail, the use of stable isotopes will soon 
surface as one of the most dependable and widely employed 
approaches [1–3], having thus been recognized as such before 
the late 1970s. Most elements exist as several isotopes that 
vary in atomic weight. Some of the biologically significant 
heavier isotopes such as those of C, H, and N are stable and 
do not decay as their radioactive isotopes are observed to do. 
These so-called stable isotopes typically have a low natural 
abundance and very importantly from a toxicology perspec-
tive, chemical and physical properties that are essentially iden-
tical to those of their more abundant counterparts. As such, 
these isotopes can be inserted/substituted for their naturally 
occurring analogs into molecules of interest and perform in 
identical fashion to their naturally occurring isotope analogs 
in both in vivo and in vitro studies. For example, carbon, a 
biological significant atom, exists naturally as a mixture of 
isotopes, 12C, 13C, and 14C. 12C is the most abundant isotope 
of carbon (98.89%); 13C, the stable isotope of carbon, is much 
less abundant (1.11%); and 14C is a radioisotope of carbon 
with a natural abundance so low as to be insignificant from 
a biological perspective. Hydrogen, another biologically sig-
nificant atom, also exists as a mixture of isotopes, 1H, 2H, and 
3H. 1H is the most abundant isotope of hydrogen (99.985%); 
2H (deuterium), the stable isotope of hydrogen, is much less 
abundant (0.015%); and 3H (tritium) is the radioactive isotope 
of hydrogen with insignificant natural abundance from a bio-
logical perspective. Other biological significant stable isotopes 
that are arguably useful for toxicology mechanistic research 
include 15N (0.37%), 17O (0.037%), and 18O (0.204%).
Hence, stable isotopes with low natural abundance make 
ideal molecular “tracers” or “tags” for studying mechanisms 
of toxicology. Two primary instrumental techniques that take 
advantage of the stable isotope in developing molecular-based 
mechanistic pathways in toxicology studies are mass spec-
trometry (MS) and nuclear magnetic resonance spectrometry 
(NMR). From the MS perspective, predictable changes in the 
mass of the “tagged” molecules/metabolites will become read-
ily apparent, easily observed, and quantifiable. Likewise, from 
an NMR perspective, molecules with 13C and or 15N tags will 
become readily apparent, qualitatively observed but somewhat 
limited from a quantitation perspective, due to the nature of 
the nuclei (relaxation times) undergoing the NMR experiment. 
It is important to note that the “tagged” or “labeled” molecules 
can be simultaneously employed directly alongside their natu-
ral abundant analogs in the same reaction media eliminating 
the necessity to employ the most probably more expensive 
labeled analog as the sole source of the molecule(s) of interest.
A wide variety of chemicals specifically labeled with these 
isotopes and at relatively high atom-% enrichments are com-
mercially available and require no special license to obtain 
such as would be necessary for their radioactive isotope ana-
logs. Following this train of thought, it might be well to pres-
ent a brief discussion as to how a labeled molecule(s) becomes 
commercially available, or more accurately what might be a 
picture of the multiple and often diverse synthetic reaction 
pathways involved in the synthesis of these special molecules. 
Obviously, the compounds having enriched isotopes are not 
found in nature, thus, they cannot be isolated therefrom and 
must consequently be synthesized. Using carbon as an exam-
ple, most all syntheses of 13C labeled compounds have as their 
initial reagent elemental carbon. Immediately, this puts all of 
the subsequent reaction pathways in perspective. Even given 
this, among different types of labeling, 13C-labeled compounds 
are universally recognized as among the most key and use-
ful in organic chemistry, life sciences, and materials design. 
However, due mostly to very involved and multiple synthetic 
pathways coupled with component yield, isolation, and puri-
fication, 13C-labeled organic molecules are very difficult to 
employ in practice due to extreme cost [4]. This is arguably 
mostly due to the rather narrow range of available labeled 
organic synthesis starting materials, coupled with the wide 
diversity of multiple organic synthetic methods often required 
to synthesize any molecule(s) of interest. Recently, a relatively 
new synthetic pathway helping to address this synthetic issue 
has appeared wherein a versatile approach for 13C2-labeling of 
organic molecules starting with 13C elemental carbon serves 
as the primary focus. First, 13C carbon is applied in the syn-
thesis of calcium carbide (Ca13C2), which is subsequently used 
to generate 13C2 acetylene – a universal 13C2 unit for labeled 
carbon atom organic transformations. Syntheses of labeled 
alkynes, O, S, N-functionalized vinyl derivatives, polymers, 
organometallic compounds, halogenated molecules, and phar-
maceutical substances have been demonstrated having Ca13C2 
as the initial reagent. The use of radicals to form new carbon‐
carbon bonds and for the incorporation of functional groups 
which can be used to incorporate isotopically labeled carbons 
has recently been described [5]. A description of some poten-
tial new synthetic methods involving the use of free radicals 
for incorporating isotopically labeled carbon into complex 
molecules or into substrates that can be attached to late-stage 
intermediates to generate labeled products was provided. The 
conversion of carbon dioxide to formaldehyde is a transfor-
mation that is of considerable significance in view of the fact 
that formaldehyde is a widely used chemical reagent, but this 
conversion is well known to be very challenging because CO2 
is markedly resistant to most all chemical transformations, 
requiring highly advanced organometallic catalysts to render 
the molecule reactive [6,7]. However, a report wherein form-
aldehyde can be readily obtained from CO2 at room tempera-
ture via the bis(silyl)acetal, H2C(OSiPh3)2 has been recently 
reported. More specifically, formaldehyde was efficiently 
released from H2C(OSiPh3)2 upon treatment with CsF at room 
temperature. Hence, the H2C(OSiPh3)2 can be seen as a form-
aldehyde surrogate and therefore could provide a new means 
to incorporate CHx (x = 1 or 2) moieties into organic mol-
ecules. Isotopologues of H2C(OSiPh3)2 could also be synthe-
sized, should, for example, 13CO2 were employed as a starting 
reagent. This potential could thereby provide an arguably more 
convenient means to use CO2 as a source of isotopic labels in 
organic molecules. Even with these recent notable advances 
in labeled isotope compound syntheses, a significant number 
of synthetic steps, coupled with the required isolation and 
purification of the intermediate compounds, will still involve

1291
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
multiple processes placing the use of labeled compounds as a 
notable investment.
A PubMed search for “stable isotopes” and “stable isotope 
labeling” methods retrieved over 15,000 papers reporting 
the use of stable isotope labeling methods since 1947. These 
search results indicate that the use of stable isotopes in bio-
logical research has and continues to be extremely popular as 
a tool for investigating toxicology mechanisms.
The stable isotope(s) approach to the study of toxicology 
mechanisms has found application in the development of 
information on the toxicity associated with nanoparticles. A 
nanoparticle or ultrafine particle is usually defined as a par-
ticle of matter that is between 1 and 100 nm in diameter. The 
properties of nanoparticles often differ markedly from those of 
larger particles of the same substance. Since the typical diam-
eter of an atom is between 0.15 and 0.6 nm, a large fraction of 
the nanoparticle’s material lies within a few atomic diameters 
from its surface. The use of nanoparticles encompasses a very 
wide area of applications including agriculture, automotive, 
construction, cosmetics, electronics, food, home appliances, 
petroleum, printing, and textiles. Nanoparticles have shown 
the capability of presenting possible toxic dangers, both medi-
cally and environmentally. Most of these dangers are due to 
the high surface-to-volume ratio, which can render the parti-
cles very reactive or even sometimes catalytic. Again, because 
of their relatively small size, nanoparticles are also able to pass 
through cell membranes in organisms, thus, their interactions 
with biological systems have become a relatively new area 
for toxicological mechanism investigations [8]. A significant 
number of these investigations into nanoparticle toxicology 
have employed stable isotope-labeled nanomaterials materi-
als. These stable isotope-containing nanomaterials have been 
employed to trace and quantify the changes in structure, chem-
ical reactions, as well as biological and toxicological proper-
ties. Some of the employed stable isotopes have included 13C, 
15N, 18O, 68Zn, 67Zn, 65Cu, 25Mg, 109Ag, and 57Fe. Examples 
of nanoparticles that have been examined for their toxicity 
include fullerene C60 nanoparticles, graphene nanoparticles, 
and nanoparticles based on inorganic materials containing, for 
example, Mg, Ag, Fe, and Ti [9–10]. In some cases nanoparti-
cle structure/toxicity relationships were developed. Analytical 
methods such as multinuclear NMR, IR, ICP mass spectrom-
etry (ICP-MS), and single particle ICP-MS were employed to 
help develop the mechanisms associated with the toxicity of 
the nanoparticles, in vivo and in vitro.
The surface chemistry of nanoparticles plays an essential 
role in how the molecules react, vide supra. Thus, modification 
of that surface could lead to significant changes in the toxicol-
ogy mechanisms of the nanoparticle. To illustrate this point, 
the nanoparticle 13C skeleton-labeled fullerene C60 (13C–C60) 
was reacted with selected reagents so as to produce function-
alized fullerene with carboxyl groups (13C–C60–COOH) or 
hydroxyl groups (13C–C60–OH) [9]. Mice were exposed to a 
single dose of 13C–C60–COOH or 13C–C60–OH. Multiple tissue 
samples were examined and analyzed by isotope ratio mass 
spectrometry. The surface modification of fullerene C60 was 
found to lead to a decrease in its accumulation level and distri-
bution rate, as well as altering its target organs. These results 
therefore demonstrated that the chemical functionalization 
of fullerene had a significant impact on its translocation and 
biodistribution properties thus influencing the toxicology 
mechanisms and that use of labeled species was an effective 
approach in developing the proposed mechanisms.
Hydrogen isotope labeling allows for the traceless and 
direct incorporation of an additional mass or radioactive tag 
into an organic molecule with almost no changes in its chemi-
cal structure, physical properties, or biological activity. Using 
deuterium‐labeled isotope analogs to study the unique mass‐
spectrometric patterns generated from mixtures of biologi-
cally relevant molecules drastically simplifies the analysis of 
the resulting data. Furthermore, incorporation of the deute-
rium into the molecule understudy has no measurable impact 
on the chemical structure of the molecule nor its biological 
activity. Tritium (3H), in particular, though not a stable hydro-
gen isotope (radioactive) has seen an increase in its application 
in toxicology mechanism studies [12].
Pharmaceuticals approved for use continue to be the sub-
ject of studies for their possible toxicological mechanisms 
[13], an example is valproic acid (VPA). VPA is a medication 
that is widely used in the treatment of epilepsy and bipolar 
disorder. However, VPA does have a negative impact on the 
liver and its impact on pregnant women has been documented. 
The metabolism of VPA has been investigated in male adult 
Sprague-Dawley rats using the twin-ion method and HiTIME 
(high-resolution twin-ion metabolite extraction) analysis. VPA 
and stable isotope 13C labeled, 13C4-VPA, were administered 
to the rats and then blood extracts were analyzed by LC-mass 
spectrometry (LC-MS). Following HiTIME analysis, 13 high-
scoring data regions were identified and corresponded to VPA 
together with 12 known VPA metabolites. MS/MS was per-
formed on selected VPA metabolites and metabolite adducts 
to assist in defining the metabolite structures. Based on the 
structures of the metabolite molecules containing the 13C 
label isotope, methods were proposed to possibly hinder their 
formation.
A xenobiotic is defined as a chemical that is not used by 
the reference organism as a nutrient chemical, is not essential 
to the reference organism for maintenance of normal physi-
ologic/biochemical function, and does not constitute a part 
of the normal array of chemicals synthesized from nutrient 
chemicals by the reference organism in regular intermediary 
metabolism [14]. Thus, it is important that methods be devel-
oped that have the capacity to accurately elucidate the struc-
ture of metabolites from the xenobiotic as part of a toxicity 
assessment. An advanced method to detect and identify com-
prehensive xenobiotic metabolites using stable isotope label-
ing, LC coupled with benchtop quadrupole high-resolution 
MS/MS (LC/HRMS/MS), data mining techniques (alignment, 
peak picking, and paired-peaks filtering), in silico metabolism 
prediction, and time-dependent profiling has been developed 
[14]. The LC/HRMS analysis was carried out using cul-
tured cells treated with unlabeled or with 13C- or 2H-labeled 
2,4-dichlorophenoxyacetic acid (2,4-D). Accurate detection of 
83 candidates for 2,4-D metabolites was confirmed. Of the 83 
candidates, 10 had previously been detected as 2,4-D metabo-
lites and 16 novel 2,4-D metabolites were described.
LC-HRMS is a powerful tool to investigate complex mixtures 
of biotransformation products originating from xenobiotics.

1292
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
A workflow based on stable isotope-assisted metabolomics 
and the bioinformatics tool MetExtract II for deciphering 
xenobiotic metabolites produced by human cells has been 
recently developed [15]. An investigation of the metabolism 
of deoxynivalenol (DON), an abundant food contaminant, 
in a liver carcinoma cell line (HepG2) and a model for colon 
carcinoma (HT29) coupled with uniformly 13C15-labeled DON 
served as reagents in the trial experiment employing the work-
flow model. The following known metabolites were detected 
DON-3-sulfate, DON-10-sulfonate 2, and a DON-antioxidant, 
DON-10-glutathione, as well as a DON-amino acid conjugate, 
DON-cysteine, for the first time. Thus, through a combination 
of this workflow, employing stable isotopes coupled with LC/
MS analysis, and tissue culture, documentation of known and 
as yet undiscovered human xenobiotic metabolites may be well 
defined.
Organisms are exposed as a matter of course to a wide 
range of xenobiotics. Generally, metabolism of xenobiotics 
to produce water-soluble compounds is one of the primary 
defenses against these compounds [16]. It would seem plau-
sible that common enzymatic processes may exist to induce 
these conversions. Such is the case wherein different phenolic 
xenobiotics were found to undergo a common metabolic path-
way to form lipophilic ester compounds and that the pathway 
occurred within and among different phenolic compounds in 
different organisms. In discovering this common pathway, a 
wide array of analytical approaches, including use of stable 
isotope compounds, GC/MS, LC/MS, LC/MS/MS, and ana-
lyte derivatization were employed. Having a well-defined 
enzymatic pathway as a starting premise could possibly assist 
in the judicious selection of stable isotope-containing com-
pounds for the study of their toxicology mechanisms.
Thus, from a fundamental perspective, a stable isotope can 
serve as a traceable, stable, and quantifiable entity throughout 
a toxicology mechanistic study. Most often, the most desirable 
stable isotope itself is a substance that possesses a relatively 
low natural abundance, so that when incorporated into the 
molecule(s) of interest, any molecules containing the isotope 
that are produced during the toxicology study will then be 
readily recognized, its structure elucidated, and subsequently 
quantified. Table 23.10 contains a list of the more popular 
stable isotopes employed in toxicological mechanism studies.
23.9  Atomic Absorption Spectroscopy (AAS) 
and Inductively Coupled Plasma ICP
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
Atomic absorption (AAS) has long been recognized as 
the method of choice, regardless of sample matrix, when the 
determination of metal concentration is the issue [1]. Atomic 
absorption spectroscopy (AAS) and atomic emission spectros-
copy (AES) are spectro-analytical procedures for the quantita-
tive determination of chemical elements using the absorption 
of optical radiation (light) by free atoms in the gaseous state. 
Atomic absorption spectrometry (AAS) detects elements in 
either liquid or solid samples through the application of char-
acteristic wavelengths of electromagnetic radiation from a light 
source. Individual elements will absorb specific wavelengths 
differently, and these absorbances are measured against known 
calibrated standards. In effect, AAS takes advantage of the 
different radiation wavelengths that are absorbed by different 
elements. AES is a method of chemical elemental analysis that 
uses the intensity of light emitted from a flame, plasma, arc, 
or spark at a particular wavelength to determine the quantity 
of an element in a sample. Both AAS and AES employ condi-
tions that subject elements of interest to specific experimental 
conditions such that absorption or emission of radiation at spe-
cific wavelengths can be detected. Both approaches possess 
detection limits for a wide array of elements/metals at the sub-
ppm range. In addition, both approaches take advantage of the 
specificity of wavelength absorption or emission linked to and 
unique to each individual metal.
ICP mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) is a type of mass spec-
trometry that is closely related to AAS and AES in that it takes 
advantage of an ICP to ionize the sample, much in the same 
way that AAS and AES processes ionize samples of interest 
[2]. The ICP process atomizes the sample and thereby cre-
ates atomic and small polyatomic ions, which are then passed 
through to, most often, a mass spectrometer (MS). Since all 
elements have a unique mass, the MS is able to detect their 
presence, and very predictable ion current responses as a func-
tion of concentration are a hall mark of the approach. It is very 
important to note that all three of these approaches, AAS, 
AES, and ICP-MS are compatible with biological fluid analy-
ses and possess the capability to detect and quantitate targeted 
elements at very trace levels. ICP differs from AAS and AES 
in that detection and quantitation of multiple metals from one 
sample are possible while AAS and AES are element specific. 
In some cases chemical “ashing”, usually involving acid diges-
tion, of relatively complex samples is required so as to convert 
the metal of interest to a common ionic form.
Thus, it should be no surprise that these technologies have 
found themselves front and center in the toxicological assess-
ments and modes of action with MNPs [3–6]. MNPs have 
become well recognized for their applications in the treatment 
of diseases, however, accompanying these significant benefits 
are still the issues associated with human exposure to these 
TABLE 23.10

Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
new materials and the resultant possibility of toxic responses. 
Since the essential component of MNPs is a metal ion, then the 
ability to trace and quantify these metals in biological systems 
becomes an essential component of toxicological mechanism 
studies. AAS, AES, and ICP analytical methodologies focus-
ing on the analysis of nanoparticles containing, Ti, Au, Zn, 
and Ag, within biological matrices will be presented below.
As presented above, and as is often the case, multiple ana-
lytical approaches are employed to assess toxicology mecha-
nisms. For example, AAS in combination with UV-VIS and 
transmission electron microscopy (TEM), has been success-
fully employed to develop toxicology mechanisms for AgNPs, 
vide supra [7]). In another study on AgNPs, to quantify the 
level of silver deposition within an in vivo platform Drosophila 
melanogaster following dietary exposure to AgNPs during the 
larval stage, atomic absorption spectrometry (AAS) equipped 
with a GF was employed. The AAS approach assisted in 
determining the location and the cumulative amount of silver 
within selected tissue samples [8]. AgNPs were shown to cause 
a variety of ROS-mediated stress responses, including apopto-
sis, DNA damage, and autophagy.
The determination of biological fates of ZnO nanoparticles 
in biological systems is critical in interpreting biokinetic 
behaviors and predicting toxicity potential. A review focus-
ing on physico-chemical factors affecting the biokinetics of 
ZnO nanoparticles, in concert with understanding bioavailable 
fates and their interaction with proteins has been published 
[9]. Contained within the review are a number of references 
to ZnO data from AAS experiments of selected tissue and 
biological samples and how the data was interpreted to build 
models of toxicological mechanisms. In a recent study of ZnO 
nanoparticles in biological systems, biopsies obtained from the 
livers and kidneys of mice exposed to ZnO nanoparticles were 
analyzed using transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and 
AAS. The biopsy of the liver of the mice given a dose of ZnO-
NPs showed evidence of steatosis. The AAS absorption results 
showed the accumulation of Zn in both the liver and the kidney 
of the mouse [10].
ICP-MS and AAS have been employed in the characteriza-
tion of AuNPs in selected biological matrices [11–13]. The 
cytotoxicity impact of AuNPs wrapped in PEG on the TNBL 
HCC-1937 breast cancer cell line has been described. PEG-
AuNPs impact analysis on the HCC1937 TNBL cell line 
showed a clear toxic action, most probably correlated with 
the documented accumulation of the nanoparticles within the 
cells. Also reported was the use of AAS in the assessment 
of the potential of pectin (PEC)-AuNPs as a carrier of anti-
retroviral drug zidovudine (AZT). In vitro cellular uptake of 
AuNPs by macrophages was quantified by AAS. The in vivo 
tissue distribution of PEC-AuNPs was carried out in Wistar 
rats to evaluate its potential to target HIV reservoir sites.
Recently described was a method validation process for Ti 
determination from TiO2NPs in biological matrices using a 
closed-vessel microwave digestion and detection by quadrupole 
ICP mass spectrometry (Q-ICP-MS), in both standard and colli-
sion/reaction cell (CCT) modes [14]. Several method validation 
criteria such as linearity, LOQs, precision under repeatability 
conditions, and intermediate precision reproducibility were 
evaluated. Furthermore, the method was compared on different 
Q-ICP-MS devices and on an HR-ICP-MS using various sam-
ples of rat tissues exposed to two different sizes of TiO2NPs. 
The results demonstrated that the Q-ICP-MS method evaluated 
permitted an accurate determination of Ti present as TiO2NPs at 
low concentration levels near the LOQ of 0.10 mg/kg.
Among the more comprehensive and exhaustive summaries 
focusing on the uptake and biodistribution of nanoparticles has 
been published by The Swedish Chemical Agency [15]. The 
report provides in abstract form the findings from well over 
300 peer-reviewed publications and contained within these 
references are details on a very wide array of qualitative and 
quantitative analytical methodologies.
The main advantages of AAS and ICP-MS are their elemen-
tal-based sensitivities and specificities with elemental detec-
tion limits in the range of nanograms to picograms/mL. Their 
main disadvantage is that minimal to no molecular structural 
information is produced [16].
23.10  Sample Preparation Technologies
(Dr. Mehdi Ashraf-Khorassini and Dr. William M. Coleman, 
III)
Arguably, on the rare occasion, a target pharmaceutical and/
or its metabolite(s) as well as a suspected toxic material con-
tained within a complex sample such as a biological material 
can be directly analyzed without some type of sample prepara-
tion. For example, direct addition of a sample to an NMR or 
ESR sample tube, or dilution with an appropriate solvent in 
a UV-VIS sample cell, or coating onto IR transparent discs, 
or direct insertion into an MS are among the possible direct 
sample analytical approaches. However, on most occasions, 
complex biological sample matrices containing multiple ana-
lytes of interest require some type of preparation/separation/
component isolation before undergoing meaningful qualitative 
and quantitative analyses. These types of sample preparation 
often take on the form of an extraction whose two main pur-
poses are to first simplify the matrix surrounding the analytes 
of interest by removing extraneous components to avoid inter-
ferences and ME and, second, possibly concentrate the ana-
lytes so as to make subsequent qualitative identification and 
quantitative assessments more rigorous. There have been some 
notable recent advances in extraction technologies surround-
ing target compounds from complex biological matrices and 
they deserve mention here since these separation technologies 
have become an integral component of most all instrumental 
methods directed at providing data for toxicology mechanism 
understandings. These relatively newer methods often are 
practiced without the use of any or at least minimal amounts 
of organic solvents, thus eliminating any issues linked with the 
residual used solvents.
In some cases, for example, molecularly imprinted poly-
mers (MIPs) have been developed that are capable of specific 
molecule extraction from a complex matrix like human urine 
[1], vide infra. While LLE still plays an important role in 
complex biological sample preparation, however, three tech-
nologies, SPME, SPE, and SFE, seem to present acceptable 
methodologies in this field and will be discussed below as they 
are applied to approaches in the development of analytical

1294
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
procedures directed at generating qualitative and quantitative 
data used to construct toxicology mechanisms.
23.10.1  SPME
SPME has grown into a versatile, facile, non-exhaustive 
sample preparation tool that has found analytical applications 
across a wide range of compounds in a very wide array of stud-
ies, such as flavor and fragrance, environmental, and diverse 
bioanalytical applications. Several reviews compiling the most 
significant applications of SPME in specific areas, including 
analysis of wine volatiles, in vivo analysis of pollutants, food 
analysis, in vitro and in vivo metabolomics studies, pharma-
ceutical and biomedical analysis have been recently published 
[2–8]. There are a number of permeations on the fundamen-
tal SPME concept focused in part on alterations in the nature 
of the SPME fibers employed. These include SPME itself, 
microextraction by packed sorbent, and different types of 
liquid-based microextraction (LPME), including single-drop 
microextraction, hollow-fiber supported LPME, three-phase 
LPME, mini-tip SPME, and dispersive liquid-liquid microex-
traction. The application of SPME to environmental and toxi-
cological investigations has been described as bringing a new 
paradigm to the analytical science [9]. A significant contribu-
tor to this paradigm shift in analytical approaches has been 
the development of biocompatible phases for the SPME fiber 
that can be readily employed in vivo including human breath, 
human saliva, and human skin. Somewhat surprisingly, in fact, 
SPME was recognized some years earlier as having potential 
in the area of toxicology analyses [10]. Most often the analyte 
having been previously adsorbed onto the SPME fiber is then 
released from the fiber via thermal desorption or organic sol-
vent extraction.
A new generation of SPME coatings based on polytetra-
fluoroethylene amorphous fluoroplastics (PTFE AF 2400) as a 
particle binder has been developed [11]. The new coating was 
tested for both thermal and solvent-assisted desorption, dem-
onstrating its compatibility with both gas (thermal) and liquid 
chromatographic (liquid) platforms. The diversity in the chemi-
cal nature of the PTFE AF 2400 coating allowed for a broad 
range of analyte adsorptive properties including differences 
in hydrophobicity, molecular weight, and chemical functional 
groups. The performance of PTFE AF 2400 was compared to 
the performance characteristics of already established SPME 
fiber coatings based on different polymers such as divinylben-
zene/carboxen/PDMS (DVB/Car/PDMS) and octadecyl/ben-
zenesulfonic acid/polyacrylonitrile (C18/SCX/PAN) in order 
to assess the new prototype versus the existing technology. 
This new PTFE AAF 2400 SPME fiber was used to validate an 
analytical method for the determination of banned doping sub-
stances, achieving limits of quantitation below the minimum 
required performance limits set by the World Anti-Doping 
Agency for most compounds contained within whole blood 
samples. Thus, this new fiber will most likely be incorporated 
into analytical approaches directed at various toxicology mech-
anism studies using metabolomics applications that involve the 
use of data from both gas and LC-based investigations.
A critical review of recent developments of matrix-com-
patible SPME coatings has been published [12]. As addressed 
earlier in this chapter, the matrices associated with biological 
samples are among the most complex with the full array of 
organic and inorganic species of varying structure and con-
centration contained therein. An overview of direct immer-
sion (DI)-SPME applications for food, environmental and 
bio-clinical studies was provided and the role of new matrix-
compatible SPME coatings for in vivo and in situ analysis 
was described. Employing the DI-SPME extraction approach 
involves the DI of the SPME fiber into the sample matrix 
wherein the sorption material bound to the SPME fiber is tai-
lored so as to preferentially extract the analyte(s) of interest. In 
some cases, the exposed DI-SPME fiber was directly inserted 
into a mass spectrometer, at elevated desorption temperatures, 
whose conditions were set to monitor the ions associated with 
the targeted analytes. Opportunities for these matrix-compati-
ble SPME fibers for use in metabolomics were discussed.
The optimized use of any amount of a human biological 
sample/fluid in analytical approaches is often a mayor priority. 
Thus, every opportunity to minimize the amount of sample 
consumed for any targeted endpoint is critical. To help address 
this issue, from an SPME perspective, work has been recently 
published wherein only 1 ōL of a blood sample was required 
for the determination of targeted and untargeted analytes [13]. 
An SPME mini-tip featuring a tip apex (1 mm) (normal SPME 
fiber length, 10 mm) was coated with biocompatible polymers, 
PAN and N-vinylpyrrolidone-co-divinylbenzene, also known 
as HLB particles. The SPME mini-tips were successfully used 
for the targeted (MS/MS) quantitation of polar, semi-polar, 
and non-polar drugs of abuse in 1 ōL of blood. Low LODs, 
~1 ng/mL, and LOQs were obtained for most of the targeted 
analytes. Also, metabolomic profiling of LC/HRMS data on 
biological samples extracted employing a mini-tip SPME 
approach was demonstrated. These findings serve to demon-
strate that rigorous miniaturized analytical methodologies 
can be and have been developed and applied when very small 
amounts of biological fluids are available.
23.10.2  SPE
The concept behind SPE is similar to that of SPME and rests 
with the relative solubility/distribution of the analytes of inter-
est (contained within the matrix) between the sample liquid 
phase in which they are dissolved and the solid/liquid phase 
covalently attached to a solid support. For SPE to be effective, 
the analytes of interest must be exhaustively and somewhat 
selectively extracted from the sample matrix and stably held 
there (captured) until such time as their elution can be affected.
In a significant number of systematic toxicology mechanism 
studies the absolute identity of the substance(s) that are pres-
ent in the sample of interest is most likely unknown or at best 
somewhat postulated. This somewhat obvious fact was recog-
nized some years earlier when a systematic approach to the use 
of SPE in toxicological investigations was described [14]. With 
such an unresolved situation, a single extraction procedure 
involving a single type of SPE material could not be reason-
ably expected to capture all of the possible analytes of inter-
est. Thus, a generalized SPE procedure wherein a compromise 
must be reached in that the substances of interest are isolated 
at a yield as high as possible and the interfering substances

1295
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
from the biological material are effectively removed was rec-
ommended. In the resulting reduction to practice, a new SPE 
extraction cartridge containing diatomaceous earth, polysty-
rene-divinylbenzene copolymer, and mixed-mode bonded 
silica as column material contained within the SPE cartridge 
was developed. These materials theoretically represented a 
wide array in their capabilities to bind both polar and non-
polar materials that may be of interest. This general philoso-
phy was advanced in a later publication that incorporated these 
advances made in SPE technologies [15].
The technologies behind SPE have progressed in the past 
several years so that automated SPE procedures are now some-
what routine in toxicological investigations. For example, after 
successful application to proficiency testing schemes, an auto-
mated SFE method is now routinely employed for the quantita-
tive determination of cocaine, 23 opioids, and metabolites in 
human urine [16]. An SPE workstation for complete automa-
tion of urine sample extraction, right from urine tubes through 
to vials ready for UHPLC-MS analysis has been described. 
Analyte recoveries, LOQ, LOD, and RSD values were all 
found to be within acceptable values.
A multi‐analyte screening automated SPE-based method 
for the quantification of 50 acidic/neutral drugs in human 
plasma based on online SPE-HPLC with PDA detection has 
been developed, validated, and applied for clinical investiga-
tion [17]. Numerous SPE materials and HPLC columns were 
extensively examined for optimal analyte recoveries and sepa-
rations. A computer‐assisted database was created for the auto-
mated detection of 223 analytes of toxicological interest.
Even given the technological advances of SPE and SPME as 
components of an overall toxicology mechanism investigation, 
vide supra, the use of the more conventional LLEs should not 
be dismissed out of hand as not having any ability to facili-
tate sample preparations. This point was well made when the 
advantages and disadvantages of SPE compared with LLE for 
use as methods for metabolomics analysis of human plasma 
were described employing LC/MS in the analysis schemes 
[18]. The evaluation involved the use of organic solvents as 
precipitation agents primarily focused on the removal of pro-
teins from plasma samples. These solvents included methanol, 
ethanol, methanol/ethanol mixtures. The use of solvents as 
extraction agents (methyl-t-butylether, MTBE) was coupled 
with three different SFE configurations. Stable isotope-con-
taining compounds were employed to more fully understand 
how sample ME influenced both analyte recovery and sensi-
tivity. Overall, the best approach for most analytes was found 
to employ methanol precipitation, coupled with SPE using 
IEX and polymer supports (PEP-2) along with extraction using 
either methanol/ethanol mixtures, methanol, or MTBE when 
examining analytes contained within plasma.
23.10.3  Molecularly Imprinted Polymers (MIPs)
MIPs could possibly be considered a hybrid of the basic 
SPE approach. In MIP technology, insoluble polymers are 
imprinted/shaped on a molecular level with the configurational 
structure of the molecules of interest. These sites are so spe-
cifically designed within the MIP that when the MIP molecu-
lar cavities are not occupied, only the specific compound from 
which the MIP was derived can be reversibly sorbed into the 
polymer [1]. The efficient and selective extraction of specific 
compounds in complex biological matrices using MIPs has 
seen a number of recent applications in toxicological analyses. 
In the world of forensic as well as clinical toxicology, proper 
sample preparation is one of the key steps in the identification 
and quantification of drugs of abuse. MIPs can be synthesized 
for sample extraction and their versatility allows the polymer 
to be employed in off-line, benchtop extractions, or on/inline 
instrument extractions, essentially eliminating most ME while 
maintaining analyte integrity. A review of MIP technology for 
the analysis of pharmaceuticals and metabolites 1994–2020 
has been recently published [1].
23.10.4  Supercritical Fluid Extraction (SFE)
The SFE process can take full advantage of the characteristics 
of a supercritical fluid, vide supra, to effect very efficient com-
ponent extractions. For example, the flexibility to vary tempera-
tures and pressures above or below the Tc and Pc values for the 
fluid can induce and alter the extraction strength/capacity of the 
supercritical fluid over a notable wide range. That is, supercriti-
cal fluids physical properties are tunable to impart changes in 
density, viscosity, and diffusivity all of which influence “sol-
vent” strength. In selected instances, the addition of a relatively 
small amount of an SFE solvent modifier such as methanol can 
impart additional flexibility in “tuning” the solvent strength of 
the SFE process. These modification in solvent characteristics 
are representative of some of the advantages of SFE; however, 
there are notable disadvantages among them being, relatively 
high equipment-based set-up costs and a rather complete knowl-
edge of supercritical fluid properties are required [19–24].
By the mid-1990s SFE was recognized for its ability to sup-
port investigations into toxicology mechanism studies [19]. 
Clearly, chromatographic techniques themselves are actually 
separation techniques employing a wide variety of parameters 
to effect the desired separation. Complementing the chro-
matographic separations that have been discussed above are 
a number of non-chromatographic separation techniques that 
can be employed in the analysis of biological fluids to aid in 
developing toxicology mechanism information, among them 
being: derivatization, IM, LLEs, SPE, liquid-solid extractions, 
and SFEs, are but a few. Arguably, the primary foci of an 
extraction are twofold: simplification of the sample matrix and 
concentration of the analyte(s) of interest. A review of publi-
cations (2016–2018) regarding SFE and online SFE coupled 
to chromatographic separation techniques has revealed the 
technology to be applicable to the analysis of complex bio-
logical matrices [20] and, thus, has reinforced the position 
of SFE in toxicological investigations. SFE and online SFE-
chromatographic separations applications for extracting and 
analyzing a very wide range of food, soil, and botanical sam-
ples have also been documented [21]. The general principles 
of SFE have been outlined [22, 23]. SFE is not necessarily 
restricted in its use in conjunction with SFC separations. For 
example, SFE, in combination with HPLC, has been success-
fully employed for the analysis of anabolic drugs [25]. This 
approach involved the direct linking of the SFE hardware to 
the HPLC system. The SFE approach was shown, due in part

1296
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
to the unique solvent characteristics of the supercritical fluid, 
to have simplified the sample matrix, thus imparting and the 
improved resolution capability of the HPLC-based separation.
Covalently modified magnetic core nanoparticles (mag-
NPs), such as SiO2@Fe3O4 have been employed in the extrac-
tion of nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) from 
blood samples [26]. Blood is well known as a complex bio-
logical fluid and extractions of components and their metabo-
lites undergoing toxicological mechanism studies are often 
contained at trace levels within this complex matrix. SFE has 
been used to eliminate ME from such a complex blood sample 
before extraction of the magnetic core nanoparticles with the 
mag-NPs. Upon simplification of the sample matrix by SFE 
coupled with optimum magnetic SPE (MSPE) [27], conditions 
resulted in improved NSAID LODs to approximately 0.2–
0.3 mg kg∞1. The RSDs for the extraction of the NSAIDs from 
blood samples via SFE-MSPE were <10.2%. Hence, SFE was 
shown to effectively assist in toxicology mechanism studies 
by simplifying the complex matrix associated with complex 
biological samples.
Although not directly applicable to toxicology mechanism 
studies, an SFE/SFC/MS/MS report on the extraction and 
qualitative and quantitative analysis of disease markers in 
serums would seem to again demonstrate the SFE approach 
for biological matrix extractions [28]. Biomarkers for oral 
and colorectal cancers were readily detected using the SFE/
SFC/MS/MS approach. The findings compared very well 
with results on the same samples previously attained using 
the more conventional and established LC/MS/MS approach. 
Thus, SFE/SFC/MS/MS offers a complementary alternative to 
the more established LC/MS/MS approach for the extraction, 
qualitative, and quantitative analysis of compounds contained 
within a complex biological matrix.
Natural products have been known to play a vital role in 
disease prevention and treatment almost since recorded his-
tory [29]. In some cases the toxicity associated with natural 
products and or their extracts can adversely impact their use. 
In order to avoid these unwanted toxic side effect(s), complete 
qualitative and quantitative analyses of the natural product 
and or its extract(s) could be seen as vital components of a 
metabolomics-based toxicology assessment. A recent proposal 
for the assessment of the toxicology of mixtures, such as natu-
ral products, has as its first step the complete analysis of the 
chemicals present in the complex matrix [30]. To accurately 
and precisely accomplish these endpoints in complex natural 
products and or their extracts is often, however, not a trivial 
matter. For example, since the FDA is now on record as stating 
that cannabidiol (CBD, a component extracted from cannabis, 
a natural material) has medicinal uses, results from analyses of 
CBD samples from a variety of sources, conducted by different 
analytical companies, has shown an undesirable wide disparity 
in final results [31]. These disparities could have been created 
by a variety of inconsistencies including the lack of standard 
methods, possible sample inhomogeneity, absence of readily 
available appropriate standards, sample preparation variability, 
sample instability, multiple hybrid varieties and species [31]. 
FIGURE 23.39  A Comparison of Supercritical Fluid CO2 and Methanol for Extraction Efficiency of CBDA from a Hemp Sample.

1297
Modern Instrumental Methods for Studying Mechanisms of Toxicology
As such, therefore, these disparities could have a direct nega-
tive impact on any assessments related to the potential toxicol-
ogy of such natural products and or their extracts. To address 
one of these issues (sample preparation variability), SFE has 
been employed to provide a chemical profile of medical can-
nabis extracts [32]. Often elevated temperatures are employed 
when preparing organic solvent-based natural product extracts 
for qualitative and quantitative analyses so as to improve com-
ponent extraction efficiency. These elevated temperatures can 
often be a cause for concern in that undesirable/unintended 
chemical reactions could occur during the extraction process 
altering the natural array of the thermally labile components 
possibly present within the extract [33]. Since the critical tem-
perature, Tc for CO2 is 31 °C, this thermally induced component 
modification concern possibly leading to sample preparation 
variability is mitigated to a great extent. When SFE was per-
formed on a popular commercial strain of medical cannabis, 
62 compounds were identified. The types of compounds iden-
tified included terpenes, flavonols, cannabinoids, cannabinoid 
derivatives, acids, flavones, sterols, acid esters, etc., thus rep-
resenting a very wide array of organic functional groups and 
reactivities [32]. Therefore, limiting the thermal history of any 
similar natural product extract with this wide array of func-
tional groups should help in creating more consistent natural 
product extracts. This improved sample consistency in turn 
should lead to improved assessments of toxicological impact 
and assist in establishing more rigid toxicology mechanisms.
By way of illustration, Figure 23.39 presents conditions and 
recovery data obtained when SFE (CO2) and methanol extrac-
tions of hemp are compared regarding the extraction efficiency 
of CBDA, cannabidiolic acid. Among the important points of 
this data are (1) the CBDA extraction efficiencies were very 
similar, having employed moderate conditions for each extrac-
tion type, (2) a minimum amount of organic solvent (etha-
nol trap wash) was employed in the SFE extraction, (3) the 
extraction times for both experiments were similar, and (4) the 
extraction temperatures for both procedures were well below 
the point for any thermal degradation possibilities. Arguably, 
these extraction efficiencies and extraction conditions again 
suggest that SFE can play an important role in extractions 
related to the toxicological evaluations of natural products.
A known flexibility of the CO2-based SFE extraction tech-
nology is the capability to dictate the solvent density (extrac-
tion capability) by adjusting the pressure of the CO2. This 
flexibility is clearly demonstrated in Figure 23.40, wherein the 
sample extraction pressure on a single hemp sample has been 
systematically adjusted with decreasing amounts of CBDA in 
the extract being observed. Thus, very conveniently, with min-
imal equipment setting changes, multiple sequential extrac-
tions under selected conditions can be performed thereby 
relatively easily optimizing extraction conditions.
23.11  Summary
(Dr. William M. Coleman, III)
When taken individually, the information collected through 
the application of the technologies discussed above could pos-
sibly be viewed as having the potential to create individual 
puzzle pieces. Each piece of the puzzle is unique in its own 
right and can thus stand alone, but when combined with, con-
nected to, or related to another “piece of the puzzle” then a 
more complete “picture” of the mechanism(s) surrounding 
toxicological phenomenon can be more clearly elucidated. 
Arguably, application of the chromatographic processes dis-
cussed herein offers the potential of creating one of the more 
important pieces if not the key puzzle piece. The improved 
resolving capability of modern-day chromatographic col-
umns with improved separation speed, when coupled with 
information-rich detectors like mass spectrometry represent 
FIGURE 23.40  High-Performance Liquid Chromatographic Analysis of Consecutive CO2 SFEs of a Hemp Sample at Different Pressures.

1298
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
a formidable combination. The uniqueness of IR and Raman 
spectroscopy to provide information on the nature of chemical 
bonds and how this chemical bond information is integrated 
into toxicological mechanism development are unsurpassed. 
UV-Vis spectroscopy with its capability to detect the pres-
ence of chromophores and relate spectral characteristics of 
the chromophore(s) to chemical structure in a relatively inex-
pensive approach creates its own unique puzzle piece. The 
“magnetic” characteristics of many nuclei when exposed to 
examination via nuclear magnetic resonance and ESR spec-
trometries offer unparalleled information on the nature of 
chemical bonds and place these technologies in concert with 
chromatographic processes as among the most powerful in 
creating meaningful pieces of the puzzle. Although relatively 
expensive, when used judiciously, incorporation of stable iso-
topes into the study of the mechanisms of toxicology can yield 
indispensable pieces of the puzzle. Relative low levels of the 
stable isotopes can be incorporated alongside their natural 
abundance counterparts and serve as readily detectable molec-
ular tags. When metal ions are under consideration as having 
a toxicological impact, then atomic absorption and ICP spec-
troscopies are the predominate approaches to creating their 
own unique puzzle pieces. Assisting in the construction of the 
puzzle pieces are the sample preparation technologies whose 
two-fold tasks to simplify sample matrix and concentrate ana-
lytes have been clearly illustrated. A very important common 
thread that brings all of the puzzle pieces together to form a 
meaningful picture of toxicological mechanisms is metabolo-
mics whose application has been illustrated in a number of 
examples contained within this chapter.

that make them attractive in chromatographic and 
extraction technologies related to biological fluids?

studies.

logical media?
